## Module 2: Object-Oriented Programming (OOP)

### 2.1 Classes and Objects

- **Understanding Classes**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **class** in Java is a **blueprint or template** that defines the **structure and behavior** of objects.
    
    It encapsulates **data (fields)** and **functions (methods)** into a single unit.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - A class serves as a **user-defined data type** from which objects are created.
    - It defines:
        - **Attributes (fields/variables)** that represent the **state** of an object.
        - **Methods** that represent the **behavior** of an object.
    - Each **object** created from the class has its own copy of instance variables but shares the same method definitions.
    
    ### **Technical Understanding**
    
    - A class is declared using the **`class`** keyword.
    - The **class body** is enclosed within `{ }`.
    - Java classes can contain:
        - Fields (variables)
        - Methods
        - Constructors
        - Nested classes
        - Initialization blocks
        - Static members
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class ClassName {
        // 1. Fields (Instance Variables)
        type variableName;
    
        // 2. Constructor
        ClassName() {
            // initialization code
        }
    
        // 3. Methods (Instance Methods)
        returnType methodName(parameters) {
            // method body
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Explanation of Syntax Parts**
    
    | Component | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | `class ClassName` | Declares a new class. The class name must follow Java naming conventions (PascalCase). |
    | `{ ... }` | Defines the body or scope of the class. |
    | `fields` | Represent data/state of an object. |
    | `constructor` | Used to initialize objects when created. |
    | `methods` | Define behaviors or actions of objects. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Rules and Notes**
    
    1. **Class Naming Rules**
        - Class names must start with a **letter** (uppercase recommended).
        - Can contain letters, digits, `_`, or `$`.
        - Should not be a Java **keyword**.
    2. **Access Modifiers**
        - A class can be declared as **`public`** or **default (package-private)**.
        - Only **one public class** is allowed per file, and the file name must match that class name.
    3. **Fields and Methods**
        - Declared inside the class body.
        - Can have different **access levels** (`public`, `private`, `protected`).
        - Instance fields and methods belong to the **object**, not the class.
    4. **Constructors**
        - Must have the **same name** as the class.
        - Have **no return type**.
        - Used for **initializing objects**.
    5. **Compilation**
        - Each `.java` file can contain multiple classes.
        - The compiler generates a separate **`.class` file** for each class.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Example**
    
    ### Example 1: Simple Class Definition
    
    ```java
    // Class Definition
    class Car {
        // Fields (state)
        String color;
        int speed;
    
        // Constructor
        Car(String c, int s) {
            color = c;
            speed = s;
        }
    
        // Method (behavior)
        void displayInfo() {
            System.out.println("Color: " + color + ", Speed: " + speed + " km/h");
        }
    }
    
    // Main class
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            // Object creation
            Car car1 = new Car("Red", 120);
    
            // Accessing method
            car1.displayInfo();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output:**
    
    ```
    Color: Red, Speed: 120 km/h
    
    ```
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - `Car` defines the structure and behavior (fields + method).
    - `car1` is an **object** created from the `Car` class.
    - Each object has its own copies of `color` and `speed`.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    - When the source code is compiled:
        - The compiler generates a **`.class` file** for each class.
        - Each `.class` file contains **bytecode**, which the **JVM** executes.
    - During object creation:
        1. JVM allocates **memory** for the new object.
        2. Fields are initialized to **default values** (e.g., `0`, `null`, `false`).
        3. The **constructor** is invoked to assign specific values.
        4. A **reference variable** (like `car1`) holds the memory address of that object.
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Real-world Analogy**
    
    Think of a **class** as a **blueprint** for building houses:
    
    - The **blueprint** = class (defines the design)
    - The **house** = object (built using the design)
    - The **rooms, doors, color** = fields (attributes)
    - The **openDoor(), paint()** = methods (behaviors)
    
    You can build many houses (objects) from one blueprint (class), each with different attributes.
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Differences (Class vs Object)**
    
    | Aspect | Class | Object |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | Definition | Blueprint or template | Instance of the class |
    | Memory | No memory allocated when declared | Memory allocated when created |
    | Access | Cannot access directly | Accessed using reference variable |
    | Declaration | Using `class` keyword | Using `new` keyword |
    | Example | `class Car {}` | `Car car1 = new Car();` |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - Without a class, **no object** can exist in Java — everything revolves around classes.
    - A **class** is a **logical construct**, while an **object** is a **physical entity in memory**.
    - Class members (fields, methods, constructors) can have **different scopes**.
    - Classes can contain:
        - Instance variables
        - Static variables
        - Constructors
        - Methods
        - Inner classes
        - Blocks (static and instance)
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    - A **class** defines the structure and behavior for objects in Java.
    - It groups related **data (fields)** and **operations (methods)** together.
    - The **JVM** creates objects based on the **blueprint (class)** using the **constructor**.
    - Every object has:
        - Its **own state** (field values)
        - Shared **behavior** (method definitions)
    - Understanding class structure is the foundation of **Object-Oriented Programming (OOP)** in Java.
    
    ---
    
- **Creating Objects**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    In Java, an **object** is an instance of a class that represents a real-world entity. It is created using the **`new`** keyword, which dynamically allocates memory for the object and initializes it using a **constructor**.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - A **class** defines the blueprint or structure.
    - An **object** is the actual entity that holds data and can invoke methods.
    - Objects are created at **runtime** when the program executes.
    - Each object has:
        - **Identity** – unique reference in memory.
        - **State** – represented by field values.
        - **Behavior** – defined by methods.
    
    Creating an object means:
    
    1. Allocating memory for it.
    2. Initializing its instance variables.
    3. Invoking the constructor.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Syntax**
    
    ```java
    ClassName objectName = new ClassName();
    
    ```
    
    ### **Explanation of Syntax**
    
    | Component | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | `ClassName` | The name of the class (data type of the object). |
    | `objectName` | Reference variable that stores the memory address of the object. |
    | `new` | Keyword that allocates memory for the object in the heap. |
    | `ClassName()` | Constructor call that initializes the object. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Rules and Notes**
    
    1. **`new` Keyword**
        - It is used to create an object dynamically at runtime.
        - It returns a **reference** to the memory allocated on the heap.
        - If memory is insufficient, it throws **`OutOfMemoryError`**.
    2. **Constructor Call**
        - When `new` is used, the class constructor is automatically invoked.
        - If no constructor is defined, the compiler provides a **default constructor**.
    3. **Reference Variable**
        - Holds the memory address (reference) of the object.
        - Without a reference, the object becomes **eligible for garbage collection**.
    4. **Multiple Objects**
        - Each call to `new` creates a new and distinct object with its own memory.
    5. **Anonymous Objects**
        - Created without assigning to a reference variable.
        - Used when the object is required only once.
        
        Example:
        
        ```java
        new Car().displayInfo();
        ```
        
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Example**
    
    ### Example 1: Creating Objects Using `new` Keyword
    
    ```java
    class Car {
        String color;
        int speed;
    
        // Constructor
        Car(String c, int s) {
            color = c;
            speed = s;
        }
    
        // Method
        void displayInfo() {
            System.out.println("Color: " + color + ", Speed: " + speed + " km/h");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            // Object creation using new keyword
            Car car1 = new Car("Red", 120);
            Car car2 = new Car("Blue", 150);
    
            // Accessing object methods
            car1.displayInfo();
            car2.displayInfo();
        }
    }
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Color: Red, Speed: 120 km/h
    Color: Blue, Speed: 150 km/h
    ```
    
    ### Example 2: Anonymous Object
    
    ```java
    class Bike {
        void start() {
            System.out.println("Bike started...");
        }
    }
    
    public class Demo {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            new Bike().start(); // Anonymous object
        }
    }
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Bike started...
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    1. **Compilation Phase**
        - The compiler checks class definitions, constructors, and method calls.
        - Generates `.class` files for all classes.
    2. **Runtime Phase**
        - When `new` is encountered:
            1. JVM allocates **memory** on the **heap** for the object.
            2. All instance variables are initialized to **default values** (`0`, `false`, `null`).
            3. The **constructor** is invoked to assign specific values.
            4. A **reference** to the newly created object is stored in the variable on the stack.
        - The JVM’s **Garbage Collector** automatically deletes unused objects.
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Real-world Analogy**
    
    Consider a **class** as a blueprint for a mobile phone.
    
    - When you manufacture a phone based on that blueprint, the **physical phone** is the **object**.
    - Each phone (object) can have different colors and storage (instance variables).
    - The process of assembling the phone is like using the **constructor** to initialize object attributes.
    - The **new keyword** is the act of starting the manufacturing process.
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Differences (Class vs Object vs Reference)**
    
    | Aspect | Class | Object | Reference Variable |
    | --- | --- | --- | --- |
    | Definition | Blueprint or template | Instance of the class | Variable holding the address of the object |
    | Memory | No memory for data | Allocated on heap | Stored on stack |
    | Creation | Using `class` keyword | Using `new` keyword | Declared normally |
    | Example | `class Car {}` | `new Car()` | `Car c;` |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - The **`new`** keyword always returns a **reference** to the created object.
    - Objects are stored in the **heap memory**, and references are stored in the **stack memory**.
    - When the reference variable goes out of scope or becomes null, the object becomes **eligible for garbage collection**.
    - Java supports **anonymous objects**, which are useful for **one-time use** scenarios.
    - Constructors ensure **object initialization**, making sure no uninitialized object exists.
    - Each time `new` is called, **a new memory location** is reserved for the object.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    - **Objects** represent real entities and are created from classes using the **`new`** keyword.
    - The **`new`** keyword allocates memory and invokes a **constructor** for initialization.
    - Every object has its **own set of instance variables** stored in heap memory.
    - Objects are accessed through **reference variables**, and unused ones are cleared by the **Garbage Collector**.
    - Understanding object creation is essential for mastering **OOP and memory management** in Java.
- **Constructors**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **constructor** in Java is a **special method** used to initialize objects. It is automatically called when an object is created using the **`new`** keyword.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - A constructor has the **same name as the class**.
    - It **does not have a return type**, not even `void`.
    - Its primary purpose is to **initialize instance variables** of a newly created object.
    - When an object is created, the **JVM calls the constructor** to ensure the object starts in a valid state.
    - If no constructor is defined, Java provides a **default constructor** automatically.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class ClassName {
        // Constructor Declaration
        ClassName() {
            // initialization code
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Explanation of Syntax Parts**
    
    | Component | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | `ClassName()` | Constructor declaration (same as class name). |
    | `{ ... }` | Constructor body, contains initialization code. |
    | `new ClassName()` | Invokes the constructor when creating an object. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Rules and Notes**
    
    1. Constructor name **must be identical** to the class name.
    2. Constructors **cannot have a return type**.
    3. Constructors **cannot be abstract, static, final, or synchronized**.
    4. Every class has at least **one constructor** (default or user-defined).
    5. Constructors can be **overloaded**.
    6. Constructors **cannot be inherited**, but they can be **called** from another constructor using `this()` or `super()`.
    7. If a constructor is not defined explicitly, the **compiler provides a default one**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Types of Constructors**
    
    ### **A. Default Constructor**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **default constructor** is a **no-argument constructor** automatically provided by the Java compiler if no other constructor is defined.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class Example {
        Example() {
            System.out.println("Default constructor called");
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Student {
        String name;
        int age;
    
        // Default Constructor
        Student() {
            name = "Unknown";
            age = 18;
        }
    
        void display() {
            System.out.println(name + " - " + age);
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Student s1 = new Student();
            s1.display();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Unknown - 18
    
    ```
    
    ### **Notes**
    
    - If no constructor is defined, the compiler inserts:
        
        ```java
        Student() { }
        
        ```
        
    - The default constructor initializes fields with **default values** (e.g., `0`, `null`, `false`).
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. Parameterized Constructor**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **parameterized constructor** accepts arguments and initializes an object with user-defined values.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class ClassName {
        ClassName(type param1, type param2) {
            // initialization code
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Car {
        String model;
        int speed;
    
        // Parameterized Constructor
        Car(String m, int s) {
            model = m;
            speed = s;
        }
    
        void display() {
            System.out.println(model + " runs at " + speed + " km/h");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Car c1 = new Car("Honda", 150);
            Car c2 = new Car("BMW", 220);
    
            c1.display();
            c2.display();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Honda runs at 150 km/h
    BMW runs at 220 km/h
    
    ```
    
    ### **Notes**
    
    - The user provides values at the time of object creation.
    - Helps create multiple objects with **different initial states**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. Constructor Overloading**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Constructor overloading** means defining **multiple constructors** in the same class with **different parameter lists** (number, type, or order of parameters).
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Provides flexibility to initialize objects in **different ways**.
    - Improves **code readability and usability**.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class ClassName {
        ClassName() { ... }
        ClassName(int a) { ... }
        ClassName(int a, String b) { ... }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Box {
        int length, width, height;
    
        // Default constructor
        Box() {
            length = width = height = 1;
        }
    
        // Parameterized constructor (1 parameter)
        Box(int l) {
            length = width = height = l;
        }
    
        // Parameterized constructor (3 parameters)
        Box(int l, int w, int h) {
            length = l;
            width = w;
            height = h;
        }
    
        void displayVolume() {
            System.out.println("Volume: " + (length * width * height));
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Box b1 = new Box();
            Box b2 = new Box(5);
            Box b3 = new Box(2, 3, 4);
    
            b1.displayVolume();
            b2.displayVolume();
            b3.displayVolume();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Volume: 1
    Volume: 125
    Volume: 24
    
    ```
    
    ### **Notes**
    
    - Overloaded constructors are **distinguished by their parameter lists**.
    - Java determines which constructor to call based on the **number and type of arguments** passed.
    
    ---
    
    ### **D. Constructor Chaining (`this()`)**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Constructor chaining** is the process of **calling one constructor from another constructor** in the same class or superclass to reuse initialization code.
    
    ### **Types of Constructor Chaining**
    
    1. **Within the same class** — using `this()`
    2. **From parent class** — using `super()`
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class ClassName {
        ClassName() {
            this(parameterList); // calls another constructor in the same class
        }
    
        ClassName(type param) {
            // code
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Student {
        String name;
        int age;
        String course;
    
        // Constructor 1
        Student() {
            this("Unknown", 18); // calling another constructor
        }
    
        // Constructor 2
        Student(String n, int a) {
            this(n, a, "Not Assigned"); // chaining again
        }
    
        // Constructor 3
        Student(String n, int a, String c) {
            name = n;
            age = a;
            course = c;
        }
    
        void display() {
            System.out.println(name + " | " + age + " | " + course);
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Student s1 = new Student();
            Student s2 = new Student("John", 21, "Java");
    
            s1.display();
            s2.display();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Unknown | 18 | Not Assigned
    John | 21 | Java
    
    ```
    
    ### **Rules**
    
    - `this()` must be the **first statement** inside a constructor.
    - Only **one constructor call** (`this()` or `super()`) is allowed per constructor.
    - It helps **reduce code duplication** and improves **maintainability**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    1. When the class is compiled, the compiler generates bytecode for each constructor.
    2. When an object is created:
        - The **`new`** keyword allocates memory in the **heap**.
        - The **constructor** is automatically invoked.
    3. If **no constructor** is explicitly defined, the compiler injects a **default constructor**.
    4. If **any constructor** is defined, the compiler does **not** provide a default one.
    5. The **JVM** calls constructors in a **hierarchical order** during inheritance:
        - Parent class constructor (`super()`)
        - Then child class constructor (`this()`)
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Real-world Analogy**
    
    Think of constructors as **different ways to prepare a cup of coffee**:
    
    - **Default constructor:** Makes a plain coffee with default ingredients.
    - **Parameterized constructor:** Allows you to specify sugar, milk, and flavor.
    - **Overloaded constructors:** Give multiple preparation options.
    - **Constructor chaining:** Reuses one coffee-making process to build upon another.
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Differences Between Constructor and Method**
    
    | Aspect | Constructor | Method |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | Purpose | Initializes object | Performs actions/operations |
    | Name | Same as class | Can have any name |
    | Return Type | None | Must have a return type |
    | Invocation | Automatically on object creation | Manually called |
    | Inheritance | Not inherited | Inherited |
    | Use of `this()` | Used to call another constructor | Used to refer to current object |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - If no constructor is defined, the compiler adds a **default constructor**.
    - If any constructor is defined, the **default constructor is not provided**.
    - Constructors can be **overloaded** but **not overridden**.
    - The **`this()`** call must be **first** in the constructor body.
    - Constructors are invoked **automatically** when an object is created.
    - **Chaining** improves code reuse and readability.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    - **Constructors** initialize objects and ensure valid object states.
    - **Default constructors** assign default or preset values.
    - **Parameterized constructors** allow flexible initialization.
    - **Constructor overloading** provides multiple initialization paths.
    - **Constructor chaining** enables reusability and reduces code duplication.
    - Together, they form the foundation of object creation and initialization in Java’s **Object-Oriented Programming model**.
- **The 'this' Keyword**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    In Java, the **`this`** keyword is a **reference variable** that refers to the **current object** — the object on which the current method or constructor is being invoked.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - The `this` keyword is implicitly passed to all **non-static methods** and **constructors**.
    - It helps the JVM and compiler distinguish between **instance variables** and **local variables or parameters** with the same name.
    - It can also be used to:
        - Refer to current object members (fields or methods).
        - Invoke other constructors in the same class (`this()`).
        - Return the current object.
        - Pass the current object as an argument.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Syntax**
    
    ### **Basic Syntax**
    
    ```java
    this.memberName;
    this.methodName();
    this();
    ```
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    | Syntax | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | `this.variable` | Refers to the instance variable of the current object. |
    | `this.method()` | Calls a method of the current object. |
    | `this()` | Calls another constructor of the same class. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Rules and Notes**
    
    1. `this` can only be used **inside instance methods or constructors**.
    2. It **cannot be used** inside **static methods**, because static methods belong to the class, not to any specific object.
    3. The `this()` call must always be the **first statement** in a constructor.
    4. Every non-static method implicitly receives `this` as the **first hidden parameter**.
    5. If not explicitly used, Java internally uses `this` to access instance members.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Use Cases of `this` Keyword**
    
    ### **A. Referring to the Current Object**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The `this` keyword can be used to refer to the **current object** that invoked the method.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Car {
        String color;
        int speed;
    
        void setDetails(String color, int speed) {
            this.color = color;  // 'this' refers to the current object
            this.speed = speed;
        }
    
        void display() {
            System.out.println("Color: " + color + ", Speed: " + speed);
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Car c1 = new Car();
            c1.setDetails("Red", 150);
    
            Car c2 = new Car();
            c2.setDetails("Blue", 200);
    
            c1.display();
            c2.display();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Color: Red, Speed: 150
    Color: Blue, Speed: 200
    
    ```
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - When `c1.setDetails()` is called, `this` refers to `c1`.
    - When `c2.setDetails()` is called, `this` refers to `c2`.
    - It ensures correct reference to each object’s instance variables.
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. Differentiating Instance Variables from Parameters**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    When method or constructor parameters **have the same name** as instance variables, the `this` keyword is used to **distinguish** them.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Student {
        String name;
        int age;
    
        // Constructor
        Student(String name, int age) {
            this.name = name; // 'this' differentiates instance variable from parameter
            this.age = age;
        }
    
        void display() {
            System.out.println(name + " - " + age);
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Student s1 = new Student("Alice", 20);
            s1.display();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Alice - 20
    
    ```
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - The parameter `name` shadows the instance variable `name`.
    - Using `this.name` explicitly refers to the **instance variable**, not the parameter.
    - Without `this`, assignment would occur between local variables only.
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. Calling Other Constructors**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `this()` can be used inside a constructor to **invoke another constructor** of the same class.
    
    This process is known as **constructor chaining** within the same class.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    this(parameterList);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Student {
        String name;
        int age;
        String course;
    
        // Constructor 1
        Student() {
            this("Unknown", 18); // Calls Constructor 2
        }
    
        // Constructor 2
        Student(String name, int age) {
            this(name, age, "Not Assigned"); // Calls Constructor 3
        }
    
        // Constructor 3
        Student(String name, int age, String course) {
            this.name = name;
            this.age = age;
            this.course = course;
        }
    
        void display() {
            System.out.println(name + " | " + age + " | " + course);
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Student s1 = new Student();
            Student s2 = new Student("John", 21, "Java");
    
            s1.display();
            s2.display();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Unknown | 18 | Not Assigned
    John | 21 | Java
    
    ```
    
    ### **Rules**
    
    - `this()` must be the **first statement** inside the constructor.
    - Only **one constructor call** (`this()` or `super()`) is allowed in a constructor.
    - Helps **reuse initialization logic** between constructors.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    1. The **JVM** implicitly passes a reference to the current object (`this`) whenever a non-static method or constructor is called.
    2. During **compilation**, if a field or method name is not prefixed with `this`, the compiler automatically adds it where needed.
    3. The **`this()`** call is resolved at compile time — the compiler checks which constructor to call based on arguments.
    4. The **reference of `this`** is stored in the **stack frame** of each method call to ensure correct association with object data in the heap.
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Real-world Analogy**
    
    Imagine you are an employee filling a form.
    
    - You write **“my name is …”** or **“my ID is …”** — here “my” refers to **yourself**, not someone else.
        
        Similarly, in Java, **`this`** refers to **the current object itself**, distinguishing it from other objects or parameters.
        
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Differences Between `this` and `super`**
    
    | Aspect | `this` | `super` |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | Refers to | Current class object | Parent class object |
    | Used in | Instance methods and constructors | Subclass constructors and overridden methods |
    | Calls | Other constructors in same class | Parent class constructor or method |
    | Must be first statement | Yes (when used as `this()`) | Yes (when used as `super()`) |
    | Access | Access current class members | Access parent class members |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - `this` keyword is **not available** in static contexts.
    - It can be **used to pass the current object** to another method or constructor:
        
        ```java
        methodName(this);
        
        ```
        
    - Useful in **event handling** and **builder patterns** where objects pass themselves.
    - Helps prevent **naming conflicts** between parameters and fields.
    - Improves **code clarity** and ensures proper access to current object members.
    - Every object has its own `this` reference at runtime.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    - The **`this` keyword** is a reference to the **current object** of a class.
    - It is used to **differentiate instance variables**, **call other constructors**, and **access members** of the same object.
    - The **compiler** implicitly uses `this` for non-static members.
    - When using `this()`, it must appear **first in the constructor**.
    - Mastery of `this` is essential for **constructor chaining**, **encapsulation**, and **clean OOP design** in Java.

### 2.2 Methods

- **Method Declaration**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **method** in Java is a **block of code** that performs a specific task, defined once and reused many times.
    
    **Method declaration** defines how a method is identified, what it returns, its accessibility, and what parameters it takes.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - Methods define the **behavior** of an object.
    - They improve **code reusability**, **readability**, and **modularity**.
    - Each method in Java has:
        - An **access modifier** (visibility)
        - A **return type**
        - A **method name**
        - A **parameter list**
        - A **method body**
    
    A method is always **declared inside a class or interface** and **invoked using objects** (unless it’s static).
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Syntax**
    
    ```java
    modifier returnType methodName(parameterList) {
        // method body
        // statements
        return value; // if returnType is not void
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Explanation of Syntax Parts**
    
    | Component | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | `modifier` | Defines access control (e.g., `public`, `private`, etc.). |
    | `returnType` | Data type of value the method returns; `void` if nothing is returned. |
    | `methodName` | Identifier used to call the method. Must follow Java naming conventions. |
    | `parameterList` | Comma-separated list of input values (zero or more). |
    | `method body` | Contains executable code enclosed in `{}`. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Rules and Notes**
    
    1. Every method **must be declared inside a class** or interface.
    2. Method name must follow **Java identifier rules**.
    3. A method **may or may not return** a value.
    4. A **void** method does not return anything.
    5. Parameters must have **types** and **names**.
    6. **Return type must match** the type of value returned.
    7. Methods can be **overloaded** (same name, different parameters).
    8. Methods can be **static** or **instance** methods.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Components of Method Declaration**
    
    ### **A. Access Modifiers**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Access modifiers define the **visibility or accessibility** of a method to other classes or packages.
    
    ### **Types**
    
    | Access Modifier | Accessible Within Class | Same Package | Subclass | Other Packages |
    | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
    | `public` | ✔ | ✔ | ✔ | ✔ |
    | `protected` | ✔ | ✔ | ✔ | ✖ |
    | *default* (no modifier) | ✔ | ✔ | ✖ | ✖ |
    | `private` | ✔ | ✖ | ✖ | ✖ |
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Example {
        public void displayPublic() { System.out.println("Public method"); }
        private void displayPrivate() { System.out.println("Private method"); }
        protected void displayProtected() { System.out.println("Protected method"); }
        void displayDefault() { System.out.println("Default method"); }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. Return Types**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The **return type** specifies the **type of value** a method sends back to the caller.
    
    ### **Rules**
    
    - If the method returns a value, its **type must match** the declared return type.
    - If the method doesn’t return any value, declare it as **`void`**.
    - The **`return`** statement is used to send a value back.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Calculator {
        // Method with return value
        int add(int a, int b) {
            return a + b;
        }
    
        // Method with void return type
        void displayResult(int result) {
            System.out.println("Result: " + result);
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Calculator c = new Calculator();
            int sum = c.add(5, 10);
            c.displayResult(sum);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Result: 15
    
    ```
    
    ### **Notes**
    
    - Methods can return **primitive types**, **objects**, or **collections**.
    - Returning an object allows chaining multiple operations.
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. Method Names**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **method name** is the **identifier** used to call a method.
    
    ### **Naming Rules**
    
    - Must follow **Java identifier rules**:
        - Begin with a letter, `$`, or `_`.
        - Cannot start with a number.
        - Cannot be a keyword.
    - **Naming Convention:**
        - Use **camelCase**.
        - Should **describe the action** (e.g., `calculateTotal()`, `printMessage()`).
    
    ### **Examples**
    
    ```java
    void calculateSum() { ... }
    void displayDetails() { ... }
    int getTotalMarks() { ... }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **D. Parameters**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Parameters (also called **formal parameters**) are **variables** defined in the method declaration to receive data from the caller.
    
    ### **Types**
    
    1. **No Parameters**
        - Method does not take any input.
    2. **Single Parameter**
        - Method takes one input value.
    3. **Multiple Parameters**
        - Method accepts several input values.
    4. **Variable Arguments (varargs)**
        - Method can take an unspecified number of arguments using `...`.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    returnType methodName(type param1, type param2, ...) { ... }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Example {
        // Method with parameters
        void greet(String name) {
            System.out.println("Hello, " + name);
        }
    
        // Method with multiple parameters
        int multiply(int a, int b) {
            return a * b;
        }
    
        // Method with variable arguments
        void displayNumbers(int... nums) {
            for (int n : nums)
                System.out.print(n + " ");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Example e = new Example();
            e.greet("Alice");
            System.out.println("Product: " + e.multiply(4, 5));
            e.displayNumbers(1, 2, 3, 4);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Hello, Alice
    Product: 20
    1 2 3 4
    
    ```
    
    ### **Notes**
    
    - Parameters are **local variables** within the method.
    - They exist only during **method execution**.
    - Method arguments are **passed by value** in Java.
    - Variable arguments (`varargs`) must be the **last parameter** in the list.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    1. The **compiler** checks the method signature for correctness (name, parameters, return type).
    2. During execution:
        - The **JVM** creates a new **stack frame** for each method call.
        - The **parameters and local variables** are stored in this frame.
        - When the method completes:
            - If there’s a return value, it’s passed back to the caller.
            - The stack frame is destroyed.
    3. **Method Overloading Resolution** happens at **compile time**, based on argument types and count.
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Real-world Analogy**
    
    A **method** is like a **recipe** for cooking:
    
    - The **method name** is the recipe name.
    - **Parameters** are the ingredients passed to it.
    - **Method body** is the step-by-step procedure.
    - **Return value** is the dish prepared.
    - **Access modifier** decides who can use this recipe.
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Differences Between Methods and Constructors**
    
    | Aspect | Method | Constructor |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | Purpose | Defines behavior or action | Initializes object |
    | Name | Any valid identifier | Same as class name |
    | Return Type | Must have (can be void) | No return type |
    | Invocation | Must be explicitly called | Automatically called on object creation |
    | Overloading | Supported | Supported |
    | Return Statement | Optional for `void`, required for others | Not allowed |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - Methods enable **code reuse** and **modular programming**.
    - **Return type** must match the **type of returned value**.
    - **Access modifiers** determine where a method can be called from.
    - **Parameters** pass data into methods; Java uses **pass-by-value**.
    - **Method names** should be descriptive and follow **camelCase**.
    - Overloaded methods improve **flexibility** and **readability**.
    - The **JVM** handles method invocation through **stack frames**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    - A **method declaration** defines the structure and accessibility of a method.
    - It includes **access modifiers**, **return type**, **method name**, and **parameters**.
    - Methods enhance **reusability**, **organization**, and **encapsulation** in Java programs.
    - Correct declaration and naming ensure **clarity**, **maintainability**, and **consistency** in object-oriented design.
- **Method Types**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    In Java, methods are classified into different **types** based on their **behavior, declaration, and purpose**.
    
    Each method type serves a unique role in object-oriented design, influencing how and when the method can be called or overridden.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    Java provides four primary types of methods:
    
    1. **Instance Methods** – Operate on individual objects.
    2. **Static Methods** – Belong to the class rather than any object.
    3. **Abstract Methods** – Declared without implementation and must be defined in subclasses.
    4. **Final Methods** – Cannot be overridden by subclasses.
    
    Understanding these helps developers control **behavior inheritance**, **memory allocation**, and **method invocation**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Syntax**
    
    ```java
    modifier [static|abstract|final] returnType methodName(parameterList) {
        // method body (if not abstract)
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Rules and Notes**
    
    1. **Each method type affects inheritance and polymorphism differently.**
    2. **Static and final methods cannot be overridden.**
    3. **Abstract methods cannot have a body.**
    4. **Instance methods require an object to be called; static methods do not.**
    5. **Method modifiers** like `abstract` and `final` cannot be used together.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Types of Methods**
    
    ---
    
    ### **A. Instance Methods**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    An **instance method** belongs to an **object** of a class. It operates on **instance variables** and can access both **instance** and **static members**.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    returnType methodName(parameters) {
        // method body
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Car {
        String color;
        int speed;
    
        // Instance method
        void displayDetails() {
            System.out.println("Color: " + color + ", Speed: " + speed);
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Car c1 = new Car();
            c1.color = "Red";
            c1.speed = 150;
            c1.displayDetails();  // instance method call
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Color: Red, Speed: 150
    
    ```
    
    ### **Notes**
    
    - Invoked using an **object reference** (e.g., `c1.displayDetails()`).
    - Can access both **instance and static variables**.
    - Stored in the **method area**, executed through the **object’s reference** on the heap.
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. Static Methods**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **static method** belongs to the **class** rather than any specific object.
    
    It can be called **without creating an instance** of the class.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    static returnType methodName(parameters) {
        // method body
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class MathUtils {
        // Static method
        static int add(int a, int b) {
            return a + b;
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            // Called using class name
            int result = MathUtils.add(10, 20);
            System.out.println("Sum: " + result);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Sum: 30
    
    ```
    
    ### **Rules**
    
    1. Can be called **without an object** using `ClassName.methodName()`.
    2. Can access **only static members** directly.
    3. Cannot use **`this`** or **`super`**.
    4. Commonly used for **utility functions** and **factory methods**.
    
    ### **JVM Behavior**
    
    - Static methods are loaded **once per class** into the **method area**.
    - Memory-efficient since they are **shared among all objects**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. Abstract Methods**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    An **abstract method** is a **method without a body** (implementation).
    
    It is declared using the `abstract` keyword and must be **implemented by subclasses**.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    abstract returnType methodName(parameters);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    abstract class Shape {
        abstract void draw(); // Abstract method
    }
    
    class Circle extends Shape {
        void draw() {
            System.out.println("Drawing a Circle");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Shape s = new Circle();
            s.draw();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Drawing a Circle
    
    ```
    
    ### **Rules**
    
    1. Must be declared **inside an abstract class or interface**.
    2. Cannot have a **body**.
    3. **Subclasses must override** abstract methods.
    4. **Cannot be static, final, or private**.
    5. The class containing abstract methods **cannot be instantiated**.
    
    ### **JVM Behavior**
    
    - JVM cannot directly invoke abstract methods.
    - Calls are dynamically bound to the **subclass implementation** during runtime.
    
    ---
    
    ### **D. Final Methods**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **final method** cannot be **overridden** by any subclass.
    
    It ensures that the behavior defined in the superclass method remains unchanged in derived classes.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    final returnType methodName(parameters) {
        // method body
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Vehicle {
        final void start() {
            System.out.println("Vehicle is starting...");
        }
    }
    
    class Car extends Vehicle {
        // void start() { } // ❌ Compilation error: cannot override final method
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Car c = new Car();
            c.start();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Vehicle is starting...
    
    ```
    
    ### **Rules**
    
    1. Final methods **cannot be overridden**, but can be **inherited**.
    2. They can be **overloaded** within the same class.
    3. Declaring a method final improves **security** and **performance**.
    4. **Static and final** can be used together, but **abstract and final** cannot.
    
    ### **JVM Behavior**
    
    - The **method call is resolved at compile time** (early binding).
    - This enhances **execution speed** and prevents **runtime polymorphism** for that method.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    | Method Type | Binding Type | Memory Location | Access Type | Overridable |
    | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
    | Instance | Dynamic (Runtime) | Heap | Through Object | Yes |
    | Static | Static (Compile-time) | Method Area | Through Class | No |
    | Abstract | Dynamic (Runtime, via subclass) | Heap | Through Object | Yes (must) |
    | Final | Static (Compile-time) | Heap | Through Object | No |
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Real-world Analogy**
    
    - **Instance Method:** Like actions performed by each employee (each has their own data).
    - **Static Method:** Like company policies — shared by all employees.
    - **Abstract Method:** Like a company rule stating “Every department must define its own work process.”
    - **Final Method:** Like a non-changeable company rule — cannot be modified by departments.
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Differences Between Method Types**
    
    | Feature | Instance Method | Static Method | Abstract Method | Final Method |
    | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
    | Belongs To | Object | Class | Class (must be overridden) | Object |
    | Requires Object | Yes | No | Yes (after implementation) | Yes |
    | Has Body | Yes | Yes | No | Yes |
    | Can Use `this` | Yes | No | No | Yes |
    | Overridable | Yes | No | Must be overridden | No |
    | Inheritance Behavior | Inherited and overridden | Inherited, not overridden | Must be implemented | Inherited, not overridden |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - **Instance methods** are the default type and represent **object behaviors**.
    - **Static methods** improve memory usage and are ideal for **utility or helper functions**.
    - **Abstract methods** enforce **common interfaces** across subclasses.
    - **Final methods** provide **stability** by locking method behavior.
    - **Method binding** type (static or dynamic) affects performance and polymorphism.
    - **Combination rules:**
        - `abstract` + `final` ❌ Not allowed
        - `abstract` + `static` ❌ Not allowed
        - `static` + `final` ✅ Allowed
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    - Java supports multiple **method types** to achieve flexibility, reuse, and control in OOP design.
    - **Instance methods** define per-object behavior.
    - **Static methods** define shared or utility behavior.
    - **Abstract methods** define a template for subclasses.
    - **Final methods** lock method implementation to ensure consistent behavior.
    - Together, these methods form the backbone of **inheritance, encapsulation, and polymorphism** in Java.
- **Method Overloading**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Method overloading** in Java is a feature that allows a class to have **multiple methods with the same name** but **different parameter lists** (different in number, type, or order of parameters).
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - Overloading is a type of **compile-time polymorphism** (or **static polymorphism**).
    - It enables a method to **perform similar actions** on different types or numbers of data.
    - The **compiler decides** which version of the method to call based on the **method signature** used in the call.
    
    A method’s **signature** includes:
    
    - Method name
    - Parameter types
    - Number and order of parameters
    
    It **excludes** the return type.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class ClassName {
        // Method 1
        returnType methodName(type1 param1, type2 param2) { ... }
    
        // Method 2 (Overloaded)
        returnType methodName(type3 param1) { ... }
    
        // Method 3 (Overloaded)
        returnType methodName(type1 param1, type2 param2, type3 param3) { ... }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Rules and Notes**
    
    1. Methods must have the **same name** but **different parameter lists**.
    2. The **return type alone** is **not enough** to overload a method.
    3. Overloading can occur:
        - By changing the **number** of parameters.
        - By changing the **data type** of parameters.
        - By changing the **order** of parameters.
    4. It is resolved **at compile time** — hence, it’s a form of **compile-time polymorphism**.
    5. Overloading can exist **within the same class** or between **subclass and superclass** (if accessible).
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Example: Basic Method Overloading**
    
    ```java
    class Calculator {
        // Method 1 - Two int parameters
        int add(int a, int b) {
            return a + b;
        }
    
        // Method 2 - Three int parameters
        int add(int a, int b, int c) {
            return a + b + c;
        }
    
        // Method 3 - Two double parameters
        double add(double a, double b) {
            return a + b;
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Calculator c = new Calculator();
    
            System.out.println("Sum (int, int): " + c.add(10, 20));
            System.out.println("Sum (int, int, int): " + c.add(10, 20, 30));
            System.out.println("Sum (double, double): " + c.add(10.5, 20.5));
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Sum (int, int): 30
    Sum (int, int, int): 60
    Sum (double, double): 31.0
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Type Promotion in Overloading**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Type promotion** in method overloading occurs when a smaller data type is **automatically converted** (promoted) to a larger compatible type during method invocation.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - If no exact match is found for a method signature, Java promotes arguments to the next **higher type** in the order:
        
        ```
        byte → short → int → long → float → double
        char → int → long → float → double
        
        ```
        
    - The **most specific** matching method is called.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Display {
        void show(int a) {
            System.out.println("int method called");
        }
    
        void show(double a) {
            System.out.println("double method called");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Display d = new Display();
            d.show(5);      // int type (exact match)
            d.show(5.5);    // double type
            d.show('A');    // char promoted to int
            d.show(10L);    // long promoted to double
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    int method called
    double method called
    int method called
    double method called
    
    ```
    
    ### **Notes**
    
    - If more than one method is applicable after type promotion, the compiler raises an **ambiguity error**.
    - Type promotion happens **only if there’s no exact match**.
    - Widening conversions are **preferred** over boxing (e.g., `int → long` preferred over `int → Integer`).
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Return Type in Overloading**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The **return type** of overloaded methods **can differ**, but it **does not participate** in overloading resolution.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - The compiler differentiates overloaded methods **only by their parameter list**, not by their return type.
    - If two methods have the same name and parameters but different return types, it causes a **compile-time error**.
    
    ### **Invalid Example**
    
    ```java
    class Example {
        int show() { return 10; }
        double show() { return 20.5; } // ❌ Error: same method signature
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Valid Example**
    
    ```java
    class Example {
        int show(int a) { return a; }
        double show(double a) { return a; }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Example e = new Example();
            System.out.println(e.show(10));     // Calls int version
            System.out.println(e.show(10.5));   // Calls double version
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    10
    10.5
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    1. **At Compile Time:**
        - The compiler looks for methods with the same name.
        - It selects the best match based on **argument types, number, and order**.
        - It applies **type promotion** if needed.
        - The method call is **bound to its definition at compile time** (static binding).
    2. **At Runtime:**
        - The JVM executes the chosen method as compiled.
        - No runtime polymorphism occurs in overloading.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Real-world Analogy**
    
    Imagine a **customer service phone line**:
    
    - You call one number, but it connects you to different departments based on your **input type** (1 for Billing, 2 for Technical Support, etc.).
    - The **phone number (method name)** is the same, but **responses differ** based on **parameters** you provide — this is **method overloading**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Differences Between Overloading and Overriding**
    
    | Aspect | Method Overloading | Method Overriding |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | Definition | Same method name, different parameters (within same class) | Same method name, same parameters (in subclass) |
    | Polymorphism Type | Compile-time | Runtime |
    | Return Type | Can differ | Must be same or covariant |
    | Inheritance | Not required | Requires inheritance |
    | Access Modifier | Can change (with rules) | Cannot reduce visibility |
    | Binding | Static | Dynamic |
    | Purpose | Code reuse and flexibility | Modify or extend parent behavior |
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Important Points**
    
    - Overloading is a **compile-time** mechanism.
    - **Parameter list** is the only differentiating factor — not return type.
    - Supports **type promotion** to resolve compatible matches.
    - Overloaded methods can have **different access modifiers and return types**.
    - Reduces **method name redundancy** and improves **readability**.
    - **Ambiguity errors** can occur if multiple overloaded methods are equally suitable.
    - Can coexist with **method overriding** in inheritance hierarchies.
    
    ---
    
    ## **11. Summary**
    
    - **Method overloading** allows defining multiple versions of a method with the same name but different parameter lists.
    - It enhances **flexibility**, **readability**, and **code reusability**.
    - **Type promotion** ensures compatibility when no exact method match exists.
    - The **return type** plays no role in overload resolution.
    - Overloading is resolved **at compile time**, making it a key feature of **static polymorphism** in Java.
- **Method Parameters and Return Values**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    In Java, **method parameters** are variables that receive values when a method is called, and **return values** are data sent back to the caller after method execution.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - **Parameters** allow data to be passed **into** a method.
    - **Return values** allow data to be passed **out of** a method.
    - Java uses a **pass-by-value mechanism**, meaning copies of the argument values are passed to methods.
    - Methods can return **primitive types**, **objects**, or **collections**.
    - Java also supports **variable-length arguments (varargs)** for flexibility in passing multiple values.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Syntax**
    
    ```java
    returnType methodName(parameterList) {
        // method body
        return value;  // if returnType is not void
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example Structure**
    
    ```java
    int add(int a, int b) {
        return a + b;
    }
    
    ```
    
    | Component | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | `parameterList` | Inputs to the method, defined by type and name. |
    | `returnType` | Data type of the returned value (`void` if nothing is returned). |
    | `return` | Keyword used to send the result back to the caller. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Rules and Notes**
    
    1. Parameters act as **local variables** within the method.
    2. **Number, type, and order** of parameters in a method declaration define its **signature**.
    3. A method can return **only one value** directly, but that value may be an **object containing multiple fields**.
    4. If the return type is **`void`**, the method does not return any data.
    5. Java **always passes parameters by value** — never by reference.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Pass by Value Mechanism**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Java’s **pass-by-value mechanism** means that **copies** of variables are passed to methods, not the actual variables themselves.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - For **primitive data types**, the value itself is copied.
    - For **objects**, the **reference (address)** is copied, not the object itself — but both references point to the same memory location.
    - Changes made to primitive parameters **do not affect** the original variables.
    - Changes made to object fields **do affect** the original object.
    
    ### **Example 1: Pass by Value (Primitive Type)**
    
    ```java
    class Demo {
        void modify(int x) {
            x = 50;
            System.out.println("Inside method: x = " + x);
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            int x = 10;
            Demo d = new Demo();
            d.modify(x);
            System.out.println("Outside method: x = " + x);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Inside method: x = 50
    Outside method: x = 10
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - The method receives a **copy** of `x`.
    - Changing `x` inside the method does **not affect** the original variable.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example 2: Pass by Value (Object Reference)**
    
    ```java
    class Box {
        int length;
    }
    
    class Demo {
        void modify(Box b) {
            b.length = 20;
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Box box = new Box();
            box.length = 10;
    
            Demo d = new Demo();
            d.modify(box);
            System.out.println("Length after modification: " + box.length);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Length after modification: 20
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - The **reference value** of `box` is copied, pointing to the same memory object.
    - Changing `b.length` affects the original `box` object.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Returning Objects from Methods**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A method can return an **object** rather than a primitive value, allowing complex data to be returned.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - The method’s return type must match the **object’s class type**.
    - Returning objects is common in **factory methods**, **data transfer**, and **builder patterns**.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Student {
        String name;
        int marks;
    
        Student(String n, int m) {
            name = n;
            marks = m;
        }
    }
    
    class Demo {
        Student getTopper() {
            Student s = new Student("Alice", 95);
            return s;  // returning object
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Demo d = new Demo();
            Student topStudent = d.getTopper();
            System.out.println(topStudent.name + " scored " + topStudent.marks);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Alice scored 95
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - The `getTopper()` method returns a **reference to a Student object**.
    - The object can be used by the caller for further operations.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Returning `this` Reference**
    
    Methods can also return the **current object** using `this`, useful in **method chaining**.
    
    ```java
    class Account {
        double balance;
    
        Account deposit(double amount) {
            balance += amount;
            return this;  // returning current object
        }
    
        Account withdraw(double amount) {
            balance -= amount;
            return this;
        }
    
        void showBalance() {
            System.out.println("Balance: " + balance);
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            new Account().deposit(1000).withdraw(200).showBalance();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Balance: 800.0
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Variable Arguments (varargs)**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The **varargs** (variable-length arguments) feature allows a method to accept **an arbitrary number of arguments** of the same type.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    returnType methodName(type... varName) { ... }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - Introduced in **Java 5**.
    - The parameter type must be followed by **three dots (`...`)**.
    - Inside the method, the argument is treated as an **array**.
    - Only **one varargs parameter** is allowed, and it must be **the last** in the parameter list.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Display {
        void show(String... names) {
            System.out.println("Number of names: " + names.length);
            for (String n : names)
                System.out.println(n);
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Display d = new Display();
            d.show("Alice", "Bob", "Charlie");
            d.show("David");
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Number of names: 3
    Alice
    Bob
    Charlie
    Number of names: 1
    David
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - The `show()` method can take **any number of `String` arguments**.
    - At runtime, Java **creates an array** of arguments automatically.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Combining Varargs with Other Parameters**
    
    ```java
    void display(String message, int... nums) {
        System.out.print(message + ": ");
        for (int n : nums)
            System.out.print(n + " ");
        System.out.println();
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Valid call:**
    
    ```java
    display("Numbers", 1, 2, 3, 4, 5);
    
    ```
    
    **Invalid call:**
    
    ```java
    void display(int... nums, String msg) { }  // ❌ varargs must be last
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    1. The **compiler** checks parameter compatibility and ensures **type safety**.
    2. During method invocation:
        - Primitive types are **copied**.
        - Object references are **copied**, pointing to the same memory location.
    3. For **varargs**, the compiler **creates an array** automatically.
    4. The **JVM allocates stack memory** for parameter values during each method call.
    5. When a method returns an object, the **reference** to that object is passed back.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Real-world Analogy**
    
    Imagine a **restaurant order system**:
    
    - The **parameters** are the ingredients or items you provide to the chef.
    - The **method** (chef) prepares the meal (performs the logic).
    - The **return value** is the finished dish sent back to you.
    - If you can order multiple items of the same type, it’s like **varargs** — the system adjusts automatically.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Differences Between Pass by Value (Primitive vs Object)**
    
    | Aspect | Primitive Type | Object Reference |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | Passed Value | Copy of actual value | Copy of reference |
    | Affects Original | No | Yes (if internal state changes) |
    | Stored In | Stack | Reference on stack, object on heap |
    | Example | `int`, `float`, `char` | `String`, `Array`, `UserDefinedClass` |
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Important Points**
    
    - Java strictly follows **pass-by-value**, not pass-by-reference.
    - Methods can **return primitive types, objects, or `this` references**.
    - **Returning objects** is powerful for object reuse and data encapsulation.
    - **Varargs** simplify method declarations for **multiple arguments**.
    - **Only one varargs parameter** is allowed per method, and it must be **the last**.
    - **JVM handles parameter passing** via stack frames during method execution.
    
    ---
    
    ## **11. Summary**
    
    - Java methods exchange data using **parameters (inputs)** and **return values (outputs)**.
    - **Pass-by-value** ensures method safety by isolating variable copies.
    - **Returning objects** enables complex data communication between methods.
    - **Varargs** make method calls more flexible and concise.
    - Together, these mechanisms support **reusable, flexible, and modular method design** in Java.
- **Advanced Method Concepts**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Advanced method concepts in Java include mechanisms and behaviors that go beyond basic method declaration and usage, addressing how methods interact with **generics**, **varargs**, and **compile-time resolution**.
    
    These concepts—**bridge methods**, **heap pollution**, and **method signatures**—are crucial for understanding **type safety**, **compiler behavior**, and **method resolution** at the JVM level.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Topics Covered**
    
    1. **Bridge Methods** in Generics (Type Erasure Impact)
    2. **Varargs Heap Pollution** Warnings
    3. **Method Signature** Rules (Name + Parameters)
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Bridge Methods in Generics**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **bridge method** is a **synthetic (compiler-generated)** method created by the **Java compiler** to preserve **polymorphism and type safety** in generics after **type erasure**.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - Java implements **generics** using a process called **type erasure**, meaning generic types are **removed at compile time** and replaced with their **upper bound types** (like `Object` or a specific class).
    - Because of this, **method signatures** that were originally different at compile time can **collapse** into the same erased signature.
    - To maintain correct method overriding behavior, the **compiler automatically inserts bridge methods**.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Parent<T> {
        T data;
        void setData(T data) {
            this.data = data;
        }
    }
    
    class Child extends Parent<String> {
        @Override
        void setData(String data) {
            this.data = data;
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    **After Type Erasure:**
    
    ```java
    class Parent {
        Object data;
        void setData(Object data) { this.data = data; }
    }
    
    class Child extends Parent {
        String data;
    
        // Actual method
        void setData(String data) { this.data = data; }
    
        // Compiler-generated bridge method
        void setData(Object data) {
            setData((String) data);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - The compiler creates the **bridge method** `void setData(Object data)` in `Child` so that it correctly overrides the `Parent` method after type erasure.
    - Without this bridge method, **runtime polymorphism** would break since the method signatures would differ.
    
    ### **Key Points**
    
    - Bridge methods ensure **runtime type compatibility** when using generics.
    - They are **invisible in source code** but present in the **compiled bytecode**.
    - Bridge methods are marked as **synthetic** (`ACC_SYNTHETIC`) in the `.class` file.
    - They are generated only when **type erasure changes method signatures** in subclass implementations.
    
    ---
    
    ### **JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    1. During **type erasure**, generic types are replaced with their bounds (often `Object`).
    2. If subclass methods have a **different erased signature** than superclass methods, the compiler inserts a **bridge method**.
    3. At runtime, the **bridge method** redirects the call to the **actual overriding method**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Real-world Analogy**
    
    Imagine a translator (bridge method) that allows two people speaking different languages (generic vs. erased type) to communicate seamlessly — ensuring the expected message (method call) still goes through correctly.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Varargs Heap Pollution Warnings**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Heap pollution** occurs when a **variable of a parameterized type** refers to an object that **is not of that parameterized type**, potentially causing **`ClassCastException`** at runtime.
    
    This can happen frequently when using **varargs** with generics.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - In varargs (`T... args`), Java internally **creates an array** to hold the arguments.
    - Since **arrays are covariant** but **generics are invariant**, storing different parameterized types in the same array can cause heap pollution.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Heap Pollution**
    
    ```java
    import java.util.*;
    
    public class Demo {
        static void addToList(List<String>... lists) {
            Object[] array = lists; // varargs -> array
            array[0] = List.of(100); // heap pollution: inserting List<Integer>
            String s = lists[0].get(0); // ClassCastException
            System.out.println(s);
        }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            addToList(new ArrayList<>(), new ArrayList<>());
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Exception in thread "main" java.lang.ClassCastException
    
    ```
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - The varargs parameter `List<String>...` becomes `List[]` internally.
    - Assigning `List<Integer>` into `List<String>` creates **heap pollution**.
    - At runtime, the cast fails when the JVM expects a `String`.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Avoiding Heap Pollution**
    
    1. Use the **`@SafeVarargs`** annotation for methods that do not perform unsafe operations on varargs parameters.
    2. Avoid storing mixed-type objects in generic arrays.
    3. Prefer **`List<T>` or collections** over generic arrays for flexibility and safety.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example with @SafeVarargs**
    
    ```java
    import java.util.*;
    
    public class SafeDemo {
        @SafeVarargs
        static <T> void printLists(List<T>... lists) {
            for (List<T> list : lists)
                System.out.println(list);
        }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            printLists(List.of("A", "B"), List.of("C", "D"));
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    [A, B]
    [C, D]
    
    ```
    
    ### **Notes**
    
    - `@SafeVarargs` suppresses unchecked warnings for **trusted varargs methods**.
    - It can be applied only to:
        - **static methods**
        - **final methods**
        - **constructors**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Heap Pollution Summary Table**
    
    | Cause | Example | Result |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | Assigning generic arrays | `Object[] arr = new List<String>[10];` | Compilation error |
    | Mixing parameterized lists | `List[] lists = new List<String>[2]; lists[0] = new ArrayList<Integer>();` | Runtime exception |
    | Unsafe varargs with generics | `void method(List<String>... lists)` | Heap pollution risk |
    | Solution | Use `@SafeVarargs`, avoid generic arrays | Safe execution |
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Method Signature Rules**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **method signature** is a unique combination of **method name** and **parameter list (types, order, and number)** used by the Java compiler to identify methods.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - The **return type** and **access modifiers** are **not part** of the method signature.
    - The signature is critical in determining **method overloading**, **overriding**, and **binding**.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Example {
        void show(int a) { }
        void show(String s) { }  // valid overloading — different parameter type
        // int show(int a) { return 0; } ❌ Invalid — same signature, different return type
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Valid Method Signatures**
    
    | Method | Signature |
    | --- | --- |
    | `void display(int a)` | `display(int)` |
    | `void display(int a, String b)` | `display(int, String)` |
    | `void display(String b, int a)` | `display(String, int)` |
    
    ### **Invalid Scenarios**
    
    - Two methods with **same name and parameter list** but **different return types** cause a **compile-time error**.
    - Method signature conflicts break **method overloading** rules.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Method Signature in Overriding**
    
    - The overriding method must have the **exact same signature** as the superclass method.
    - The **return type** can differ only if it is a **covariant type** (subclass of the original return type).
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Parent {
        Object getData() { return null; }
    }
    
    class Child extends Parent {
        @Override
        String getData() { return "Child Data"; } // Covariant return type allowed
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    | Concept | Compiler Role | JVM Behavior |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | Bridge Method | Generates hidden methods to maintain overriding post type erasure | Dynamically binds correct version |
    | Varargs | Converts parameters into arrays | Allocates array on heap for arguments |
    | Method Signature | Used for overload resolution | Uses method table for invocation |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    - **Bridge Methods:** Like an interpreter ensuring smooth communication between two versions of a system (generic vs erased types).
    - **Heap Pollution:** Like mixing fragile chemicals in the wrong container — type mismatch can cause an explosion (runtime error).
    - **Method Signature:** Like a unique fingerprint for identifying a specific person (method) among many with the same name.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - **Bridge methods** preserve polymorphism in **generic inheritance** after type erasure.
    - **Heap pollution** occurs due to unsafe use of **generic varargs**, mitigated by `@SafeVarargs`.
    - **Method signature** is determined only by the **method name** and **parameter list**, not the return type.
    - **Type erasure** can generate synthetic methods, affecting reflection and debugging.
