    - **Varargs arrays** may lead to **heap pollution** because generics and arrays interact unsafely.
    - Java ensures **type safety** at compile time but relies on **runtime checks** for generic method correctness.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    - **Bridge methods** ensure correct overriding behavior in generic classes affected by **type erasure**.
    - **Varargs heap pollution** is a runtime risk caused by generic arrays — mitigated with **`@SafeVarargs`**.
    - **Method signature** determines method identity and overload resolution; it consists of **method name + parameter types** only.
    - Understanding these advanced method mechanisms is crucial for mastering **type safety**, **method resolution**, and **runtime behavior** in Java.

### 2.3 Encapsulation and Data Hiding

- **Access Modifiers**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    In Java, **access modifiers** are **keywords** that define the **visibility** or **accessibility** of classes, methods, constructors, and variables to other classes and packages.
    
    They are a key part of **encapsulation**, controlling how much of an object’s internal data and behavior is exposed to the outside world.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - Access modifiers **determine scope** — which other classes or packages can see or use a particular member (variable, method, or class).
    - There are **four access levels** in Java:
        1. **private**
        2. **default** (no modifier)
        3. **protected**
        4. **public**
    - The choice of modifier depends on **how much access** you want to grant to a member or class.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Syntax**
    
    ```java
    <access_modifier> class ClassName {
        <access_modifier> type variableName;
        <access_modifier> returnType methodName(parameters) { ... }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    public class Example {
        private int a;
        int b;                // default
        protected int c;
        public int d;
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Rules and Notes**
    
    1. **Classes** can only be declared as `public` or **default** (no modifier).
        - `protected` and `private` are **not allowed** for top-level classes.
    2. **Class members (fields, constructors, methods)** can have **any of the four modifiers**.
    3. The **modifier affects visibility** across:
        - The **same class**
        - The **same package**
        - **Subclass in another package**
        - **Completely different packages**
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Types of Access Modifiers**
    
    ---
    
    ### **A. private**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **private** member is **accessible only within the same class**.
    
    It provides the **highest level of encapsulation**.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class Student {
        private String name;
    
        private void showName() {
            System.out.println("Name: " + name);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Student {
        private int id = 101;
    
        private void display() {
            System.out.println("Student ID: " + id);
        }
    
        void accessPrivate() {
            display();  // valid: inside same class
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Student s = new Student();
            // s.display(); // ❌ Error: display() has private access
            s.accessPrivate(); // ✅ allowed through public method
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Student ID: 101
    
    ```
    
    ### **Notes**
    
    - Private members are **not inherited** by subclasses.
    - Commonly used to **protect internal data** and enforce **encapsulation**.
    - Access is only allowed **within the same class**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. default (Package-Private)**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    When no access modifier is specified, the member or class has **default (package-private)** access, meaning it is visible **only within the same package**.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class Example {
        int data = 10; // default access
        void show() { System.out.println("Default Access"); }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    // File: pack1/Example.java
    package pack1;
    
    class Example {
        void show() {
            System.out.println("Default Access: same package only");
        }
    }
    
    // File: pack1/Test.java
    package pack1;
    
    public class Test {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Example e = new Example();
            e.show(); // ✅ allowed: same package
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Notes**
    
    - Not accessible from **other packages**, even via inheritance.
    - Default access is often used for **package-level helper classes** or **internal APIs**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. protected**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **protected** member is accessible:
    
    - Within the **same package**, and
    - In **subclasses**, even if they are in **different packages**.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    protected returnType methodName() { ... }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    // File: pack1/Parent.java
    package pack1;
    
    public class Parent {
        protected void show() {
            System.out.println("Protected method in Parent");
        }
    }
    
    // File: pack2/Child.java
    package pack2;
    import pack1.Parent;
    
    public class Child extends Parent {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Child c = new Child();
            c.show(); // ✅ allowed: inherited protected access
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Protected method in Parent
    
    ```
    
    ### **Notes**
    
    - `protected` provides **controlled visibility** to subclasses.
    - It supports **inheritance with encapsulation**.
    - Can be accessed by:
        - Same package (non-subclasses)
        - Subclasses in different packages
    
    ---
    
    ### **D. public**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **public** member or class is accessible **from anywhere** in the program.
    
    It provides the **widest level of access**.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    public class Example {
        public void show() {
            System.out.println("Accessible everywhere");
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    // File: pack1/Example.java
    package pack1;
    
    public class Example {
        public void display() {
            System.out.println("Public Access: Available Everywhere");
        }
    }
    
    // File: pack2/Test.java
    package pack2;
    import pack1.Example;
    
    public class Test {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Example e = new Example();
            e.display(); // ✅ accessible across packages
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Public Access: Available Everywhere
    
    ```
    
    ### **Notes**
    
    - Commonly used for **API methods** or **entry points** (e.g., `public static void main`).
    - Should be used carefully to **avoid exposing implementation details**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Access Level Implications**
    
    | Access Modifier | Within Same Class | Same Package | Subclass (Different Package) | Different Package (Non-subclass) |
    | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
    | **private** | ✔ | ✖ | ✖ | ✖ |
    | **default (package-private)** | ✔ | ✔ | ✖ | ✖ |
    | **protected** | ✔ | ✔ | ✔ | ✖ |
    | **public** | ✔ | ✔ | ✔ | ✔ |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Additional Notes**
    
    - **Top-level classes** can only be `public` or **default**.
        - `private` or `protected` classes at the top level cause a compilation error.
    - For **nested (inner) classes**, all modifiers (`private`, `protected`, `public`) are allowed.
    - Using the correct modifier is a part of **good encapsulation and API design**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    1. The **compiler** enforces access control at **compile time**, preventing illegal references.
    2. The **JVM** enforces access control again during **class loading** and **execution**, ensuring runtime safety.
    3. If code violates access restrictions, the JVM throws:
        
        ```
        java.lang.IllegalAccessError
        
        ```
        
    4. Access modifiers affect the **class file’s access flags**, controlling **visibility and inheritance behavior**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    Think of a **company’s hierarchy**:
    
    - **private** → Confidential file accessible only to one employee.
    - **default** → Internal file shared within one department (package).
    - **protected** → Shared with internal members and some partner teams (subclasses).
    - **public** → Available company-wide (accessible from anywhere).
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - **Access modifiers control visibility**, not memory or performance.
    - **Encapsulation** relies heavily on private and protected modifiers.
    - **Protected** strikes a balance between privacy and inheritance.
    - Avoid using **public fields** — instead, provide **getter/setter methods**.
    - Always design APIs with the **principle of least privilege** — expose only what’s necessary.
    - A **public class** must be saved in a file with the **same name** as the class.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    - Java provides four access levels: `private`, `default`, `protected`, and `public`.
    - Access modifiers regulate **where and how members or classes can be accessed**.
    - `private` offers **maximum security**, while `public` provides **maximum accessibility**.
    - `protected` allows subclass visibility, even across packages.
    - `default` restricts access to the **same package only**.
    - Proper use of access modifiers ensures **encapsulation**, **modularity**, and **maintainable object-oriented design**.
- **Getters and Setters**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Getters and Setters** are special methods used to **access (get)** and **modify (set)** the values of private instance variables in a class.
    
    They form the foundation of **encapsulation** — one of the four pillars of Object-Oriented Programming (OOP).
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - In Java, instance variables are usually declared **private** to prevent **direct access** from outside the class.
    - **Getter methods** provide **controlled read access**, while **setter methods** provide **controlled modification** of private data.
    - This approach allows:
        - **Data hiding**
        - **Validation before modification**
        - **Read-only or write-only access**
        - **Better maintainability**
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class ClassName {
        // Private field
        private dataType variableName;
    
        // Getter method
        public dataType getVariableName() {
            return variableName;
        }
    
        // Setter method
        public void setVariableName(dataType variableName) {
            this.variableName = variableName;
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Rules and Notes**
    
    1. Instance variables are **declared private** for encapsulation.
    2. Getters and setters are usually **public**, allowing controlled external access.
    3. Getter names follow the convention: `getVariableName()` (or `isVariableName()` for booleans).
    4. Setter names follow the convention: `setVariableName()`.
    5. Inside the setter, the keyword `this` is used to differentiate between the **parameter** and the **instance variable**.
    6. You can include **validation logic** inside setters to ensure only valid data is assigned.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Example: Basic Getters and Setters**
    
    ```java
    class Student {
        // Private fields
        private String name;
        private int age;
    
        // Getter for name
        public String getName() {
            return name;
        }
    
        // Setter for name
        public void setName(String name) {
            this.name = name;
        }
    
        // Getter for age
        public int getAge() {
            return age;
        }
    
        // Setter for age
        public void setAge(int age) {
            this.age = age;
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Student s = new Student();
            s.setName("Alice");
            s.setAge(20);
            System.out.println(s.getName() + " - " + s.getAge());
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Alice - 20
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Implementing Encapsulation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Encapsulation** is the mechanism of **binding data and methods** that operate on that data into a single unit while **restricting direct access** to the data.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - Getters and setters help achieve **encapsulation** by:
        - Hiding private variables from direct access.
        - Providing a **controlled interface** to modify or view data.
        - Allowing **validation** or **transformation** before data modification.
    
    ### **Example: Encapsulation in Action**
    
    ```java
    class BankAccount {
        private double balance;
    
        // Getter
        public double getBalance() {
            return balance;
        }
    
        // Setter with control logic
        public void setBalance(double balance) {
            if (balance >= 0)
                this.balance = balance;
            else
                System.out.println("Invalid amount. Balance cannot be negative.");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            BankAccount account = new BankAccount();
            account.setBalance(1000.50);  // valid
            System.out.println("Balance: " + account.getBalance());
            account.setBalance(-500);     // invalid
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Balance: 1000.5
    Invalid amount. Balance cannot be negative.
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    Encapsulation ensures that the variable `balance` cannot be directly modified — only through validation inside the setter.
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Validation in Setters**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Setters often include **validation checks** to ensure that data assigned to fields is **valid, meaningful, and consistent**.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Employee {
        private int age;
        private double salary;
    
        public void setAge(int age) {
            if (age > 18 && age < 65)
                this.age = age;
            else
                System.out.println("Age must be between 18 and 65.");
        }
    
        public void setSalary(double salary) {
            if (salary > 0)
                this.salary = salary;
            else
                System.out.println("Salary must be positive.");
        }
    
        public int getAge() { return age; }
        public double getSalary() { return salary; }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Employee e = new Employee();
            e.setAge(25);
            e.setSalary(-2000);
            System.out.println("Age: " + e.getAge() + ", Salary: " + e.getSalary());
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Salary must be positive.
    Age: 25, Salary: 0.0
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    Validation ensures that invalid data (like negative salary) is not accepted or stored.
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Read-Only and Write-Only Properties**
    
    ### **A. Read-Only Property**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **read-only** property allows data to be **read** but **not modified** from outside the class.
    
    It has a **getter** but no **setter**.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Student {
        private final String rollNumber; // immutable field
    
        Student(String rollNumber) {
            this.rollNumber = rollNumber;
        }
    
        // Only getter
        public String getRollNumber() {
            return rollNumber;
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Student s = new Student("S101");
            System.out.println("Roll No: " + s.getRollNumber());
            // s.setRollNumber("S102"); ❌ No setter available
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Roll No: S101
    
    ```
    
    **Use Case:**
    
    Read-only properties are useful for immutable identifiers like roll numbers, IDs, or constants.
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. Write-Only Property**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **write-only** property allows data to be **written (set)** but **not read**.
    
    It has a **setter** but no **getter**.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class SecureData {
        private String password;
    
        // Only setter
        public void setPassword(String password) {
            if (password.length() >= 8)
                this.password = password;
            else
                System.out.println("Password must be at least 8 characters long.");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            SecureData data = new SecureData();
            data.setPassword("admin123"); // ✅ allowed
            // System.out.println(data.password); ❌ cannot access or read password
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Use Case:**
    
    Write-only fields are common for **sensitive data** such as passwords, PINs, or cryptographic keys.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    1. During compilation:
        - Getters and setters are treated as **regular methods**.
        - The **JVM** invokes them like any other method call.
    2. Encapsulation ensures that **fields remain private** and are only accessed through the **method interface**.
    3. The **JIT (Just-In-Time)** compiler may **inline simple getter/setter calls** for performance optimization.
    4. Reflection or serialization can still access private data, but **encapsulation boundaries** remain in normal usage.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Real-world Analogy**
    
    Think of **getters and setters** like a **secure ATM system**:
    
    - You can **deposit** money using the interface (setter).
    - You can **check balance** using another interface (getter).
    - You **cannot open the ATM and directly change internal values** — that’s encapsulation.
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Differences Between Direct Access and Getter/Setter**
    
    | Aspect | Direct Field Access | Getter/Setter |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | Encapsulation | Broken | Preserved |
    | Validation | Not possible | Can be implemented |
    | Flexibility | Hard to modify later | Easy to extend |
    | Control | None | Full control |
    | Common Use | For constants or local vars | For instance variables |
    
    ---
    
    ## **11. Important Points**
    
    - Getters and setters are a core part of **JavaBeans** conventions.
    - Always **validate inputs** in setters to ensure data integrity.
    - Use **getters only** for **read-only** properties, and **setters only** for **write-only** data.
    - Keep fields **private** and expose them through **public accessor methods**.
    - Use **`this`** keyword to refer to the current object’s variable inside setters.
    - In modern Java frameworks (e.g., Spring, Hibernate), getters and setters are used extensively for **dependency injection** and **data binding**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **12. Summary**
    
    - **Getters and setters** implement **encapsulation** by controlling access to private fields.
    - **Setters** can include **validation logic** to ensure data consistency.
    - **Read-only** properties have only **getters**, while **write-only** have only **setters**.
    - They provide a **controlled interface** between class internals and the outside world.
    - Proper use of getters and setters leads to **robust**, **maintainable**, and **secure** object-oriented design in Java.
- **Java Beans Convention**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **JavaBean** is a **reusable software component** that follows specific naming, structural, and accessibility conventions defined by the **JavaBeans specification**.
    
    It encapsulates multiple objects into a single object (the bean) that can be **easily manipulated**, **configured**, or **persisted**.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - A **JavaBean** is simply a **Java class** that follows particular conventions for **property naming**, **constructors**, and **serialization**.
    - Beans are widely used in **frameworks**, **GUI builders**, **enterprise applications**, and **component-based architectures** like **Spring**, **EJB**, and **JavaFX**.
    - These conventions ensure **interoperability** among tools, IDEs, and frameworks that rely on **reflection** and **introspection**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. JavaBean Conventions Overview**
    
    A class is considered a **valid JavaBean** if it meets the following conventions:
    
    | Convention | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Property Naming** | Private fields with public getter/setter methods following standard naming rules. |
    | **Default Constructor** | A public no-argument constructor must be present. |
    | **Serializable** | Implements `java.io.Serializable` to allow object persistence or transmission. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. JavaBean Structure Syntax**
    
    ```java
    import java.io.Serializable;
    
    public class BeanName implements Serializable {
        private dataType propertyName;
    
        // Default (no-argument) constructor
        public BeanName() { }
    
        // Getter method
        public dataType getPropertyName() {
            return propertyName;
        }
    
        // Setter method
        public void setPropertyName(dataType propertyName) {
            this.propertyName = propertyName;
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Property Naming Convention**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **property** in a JavaBean is a **private field** that can be accessed via a **pair of public getter and setter methods**.
    
    The naming of these methods follows a specific **camelCase convention** defined by the JavaBeans API.
    
    ### **Rules**
    
    1. The property name should be **camelCase** (e.g., `studentName`, `accountBalance`).
    2. Getter method:
        - Prefix `get` with the capitalized property name.
        - Example: `getStudentName()` corresponds to property `studentName`.
    3. Setter method:
        - Prefix `set` with the capitalized property name.
        - Example: `setStudentName(String name)`.
    4. For boolean properties:
        - The getter can use either `is` or `get`.
        - Example: `isActive()` or `getActive()`.
    5. No extra arguments should be in the getter; the setter should have exactly one argument.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    public class Employee {
        private String name;
        private int age;
        private boolean active;
    
        // Getter and Setter for name
        public String getName() {
            return name;
        }
        public void setName(String name) {
            this.name = name;
        }
    
        // Getter and Setter for age
        public int getAge() {
            return age;
        }
        public void setAge(int age) {
            this.age = age;
        }
    
        // Boolean getter uses 'is'
        public boolean isActive() {
            return active;
        }
        public void setActive(boolean active) {
            this.active = active;
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Property Mappings:**
    
    | Property | Getter | Setter |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | `name` | `getName()` | `setName(String)` |
    | `age` | `getAge()` | `setAge(int)` |
    | `active` | `isActive()` | `setActive(boolean)` |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Introspection**
    
    - JavaBeans use the **Introspector API** to detect properties via getter/setter naming.
    - Example:
        
        ```java
        BeanInfo info = Introspector.getBeanInfo(Employee.class);
        PropertyDescriptor[] props = info.getPropertyDescriptors();
        
        ```
        
    - Tools like **Spring**, **JSP (via EL)**, and **NetBeans GUI Builder** use introspection to discover bean properties automatically.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Default Constructor Requirement**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **JavaBean must have a public no-argument (default) constructor** so that tools and frameworks can **instantiate the bean dynamically** (e.g., via reflection or deserialization).
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - Frameworks like **Spring**, **JSP**, and **Hibernate** create bean instances **at runtime** without requiring specific parameters.
    - If only parameterized constructors exist, reflection-based instantiation fails.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    public class Student {
        private String name;
        private int age;
    
        // Default constructor
        public Student() { }
    
        // Parameterized constructor
        public Student(String name, int age) {
            this.name = name;
            this.age = age;
        }
    
        // Getters and Setters
        public String getName() { return name; }
        public void setName(String name) { this.name = name; }
    
        public int getAge() { return age; }
        public void setAge(int age) { this.age = age; }
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Why It’s Important:**
    
    - Allows **automatic instantiation** by tools like IDEs or frameworks.
    - Enables **object reconstruction** during **deserialization**.
    
    **Without a Default Constructor:**
    
    ```java
    Student s = Student.class.getDeclaredConstructor().newInstance();
    // ❌ Throws NoSuchMethodException if no default constructor exists
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Serializable Implementation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **JavaBean must implement `java.io.Serializable`** so its state can be **saved, transmitted, or persisted** across different systems.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - Serialization converts an object into a **byte stream** for storage or transmission (e.g., across networks or sessions).
    - It allows JavaBeans to be used in **distributed applications**, **web frameworks**, and **persistent storage** mechanisms.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    import java.io.Serializable;
    
    public class Product implements Serializable {
        private static final long serialVersionUID = 1L;
    
        private String name;
        private double price;
    
        public Product() { }
    
        public String getName() { return name; }
        public void setName(String name) { this.name = name; }
    
        public double getPrice() { return price; }
        public void setPrice(double price) { this.price = price; }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example: Serialization and Deserialization**
    
    ```java
    import java.io.*;
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Product p = new Product();
            p.setName("Laptop");
            p.setPrice(85000.00);
    
            try {
                // Serialize
                ObjectOutputStream out = new ObjectOutputStream(new FileOutputStream("product.ser"));
                out.writeObject(p);
                out.close();
    
                // Deserialize
                ObjectInputStream in = new ObjectInputStream(new FileInputStream("product.ser"));
                Product saved = (Product) in.readObject();
                in.close();
    
                System.out.println("Deserialized Product: " + saved.getName() + " - " + saved.getPrice());
            } catch (Exception e) {
                e.printStackTrace();
            }
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Deserialized Product: Laptop - 85000.0
    
    ```
    
    ### **Notes**
    
    - Always include a **serialVersionUID** to ensure version compatibility during deserialization.
    - Fields declared as `transient` are **not serialized**.
    - Non-serializable fields can cause `NotSerializableException`.
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    | Step | Behavior |
    | --- | --- |
    | Compilation | Compiler checks for naming convention violations and missing constructors. |
    | Introspection | JavaBeans API scans class methods and identifies properties by getter/setter pairs. |
    | Serialization | JVM converts the object to a byte stream using the `Serializable` interface. |
    | Deserialization | JVM reconstructs the bean object using the **default constructor** and restores fields. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Real-world Analogy**
    
    Think of a **JavaBean** as a **configurable component in a toolkit** — like a button or text field in a GUI builder:
    
    - It must have a **standard interface** (getters/setters) so tools can modify its properties.
    - It must be **easily instantiable** (via default constructor).
    - It must be **saveable or transferable** (via serialization).
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Differences Between JavaBeans and Plain Old Java Objects (POJOs)**
    
    | Aspect | JavaBean | POJO |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | Default Constructor | Required | Optional |
    | Serializable | Required | Optional |
    | Property Naming | Must follow getter/setter convention | No strict naming rules |
    | Access Control | Private fields with getters/setters | Any access level allowed |
    | Framework Support | Automatically recognized by IDEs and frameworks | Used freely without specific requirements |
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Important Points**
    
    - **JavaBeans** support **introspection** and **event handling**, making them suitable for component-based frameworks.
    - The **default constructor** ensures easy instantiation by reflection.
    - The **Serializable interface** enables persistent storage and network transmission.
    - Follow consistent **getter/setter naming conventions** for IDE and framework compatibility.
    - Many frameworks like **Spring**, **Hibernate**, and **JSP EL** depend on these conventions.
    - JavaBeans are **not threadsafe** by default — synchronization must be handled explicitly if needed.
    
    ---
    
    ## **11. Summary**
    
    - A **JavaBean** is a **standard Java class** that follows specific design conventions for reusability and interoperability.
    - It must:
        - Have **private fields** accessed via **getters/setters**.
        - Include a **public no-argument constructor**.
        - Implement **`Serializable`** for persistence.
    - These conventions enable tools and frameworks to **automatically recognize, instantiate, and manage** bean objects.
    - Following the JavaBeans standard ensures consistency, **encapsulation**, and **compatibility** across Java applications and platforms.
- **Immutable Objects** (**NEW**)
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    An **immutable object** in Java is an object whose **state (data fields) cannot be changed** once it is created.
    
    All fields of an immutable object are **final**, and no method can modify its internal data after construction.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - Immutable objects represent **constant data** — once initialized, their fields never change.
    - Instead of modifying the existing object, any operation that seems to “modify” it actually **creates a new object** with updated data.
    - Immutability is one of the **core principles of safe concurrent programming**, ensuring that multiple threads can **share objects safely without synchronization**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Rules for Creating Immutable Classes**
    
    To create an **immutable class** in Java, the following **rules** must be strictly followed:
    
    | Rule | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | **1. Declare the class as `final`** | Prevents subclassing, which could allow modification of fields. |
    | **2. Make all fields `private` and `final`** | Ensures that fields cannot be directly accessed or reassigned. |
    | **3. Initialize all fields through the constructor** | Guarantees that object data is set only once during creation. |
    | **4. Do not provide setter methods** | Prevents modification of data after construction. |
    | **5. Perform deep or defensive copying of mutable fields** | Ensures that external references cannot alter internal data. |
    | **6. Return copies of mutable objects in getters** | Avoids exposing mutable internal state to callers. |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax Example: Basic Immutable Class**
    
    ```java
    public final class Student {
        private final String name;
        private final int rollNumber;
    
        // Constructor
        public Student(String name, int rollNumber) {
            this.name = name;
            this.rollNumber = rollNumber;
        }
    
        // Getters (no setters)
        public String getName() {
            return name;
        }
    
        public int getRollNumber() {
            return rollNumber;
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Key Characteristics:**
    
    - Class declared `final`
    - Fields are `private` and `final`
    - No setter methods provided
    - Data set only once in constructor
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Defensive Copying in Constructors and Getters**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Defensive copying** is the process of **creating copies** of mutable objects when:
    
    - They are **passed to the constructor** (to protect internal state), or
    - They are **returned from getters** (to prevent external modification).
    
    This ensures the class remains truly immutable even when containing **mutable fields** like arrays, `Date`, or custom objects.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Without Defensive Copying (Mutable Exposure)**
    
    ```java
    import java.util.Date;
    
    public final class Employee {
        private final String name;
        private final Date joiningDate; // Mutable object
    
        public Employee(String name, Date joiningDate) {
            this.name = name;
            this.joiningDate = joiningDate; // ❌ Direct assignment (unsafe)
        }
    
        public Date getJoiningDate() {
            return joiningDate; // ❌ Exposes internal mutable object
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ```java
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Date date = new Date();
            Employee e = new Employee("John", date);
    
            System.out.println("Before change: " + e.getJoiningDate());
            date.setTime(0); // External modification
            System.out.println("After change: " + e.getJoiningDate()); // ❌ Internal state changed
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Output:**
    
    ```
    Before change: Fri Nov 09 15:00:00 IST 2025
    After change: Thu Jan 01 05:30:00 IST 1970
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    Since the internal `Date` reference was exposed, external modification changed the internal state — breaking immutability.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: With Defensive Copying (Safe Design)**
    
    ```java
    import java.util.Date;
    
    public final class Employee {
        private final String name;
        private final Date joiningDate;
    
        public Employee(String name, Date joiningDate) {
            this.name = name;
            // Defensive copy in constructor
            this.joiningDate = new Date(joiningDate.getTime());
        }
    
        public String getName() {
            return name;
        }
    
        public Date getJoiningDate() {
            // Defensive copy in getter
            return new Date(joiningDate.getTime());
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ```java
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Date date = new Date();
            Employee e = new Employee("John", date);
    
            System.out.println("Before change: " + e.getJoiningDate());
            date.setTime(0); // Change original date
            System.out.println("After external change: " + e.getJoiningDate());
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Output:**
    
    ```
    Before change: Fri Nov 09 15:00:00 IST 2025
    After external change: Fri Nov 09 15:00:00 IST 2025
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    The object remains unchanged because copies were used — preserving immutability.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Benefits for Thread Safety and Caching**
    
    ### **A. Thread Safety**
    
    - Immutable objects are **automatically thread-safe** because their state cannot change after creation.
    - No synchronization is needed, as multiple threads can safely read the same immutable object.
    - Eliminates risks of **data inconsistency** or **race conditions**.
    
    **Example:**
    
    ```java
    final class Coordinates {
        private final int x, y;
    
        Coordinates(int x, int y) {
            this.x = x; this.y = y;
        }
    
        public int getX() { return x; }
        public int getY() { return y; }
    }
    
    ```
    
    Multiple threads can access a `Coordinates` object safely without locks.
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. Caching**
    
    - Immutable objects can be **safely shared** and **cached** because their state never changes.
    - Frequently used objects can be stored in **memory pools**, reducing object creation overhead.
    
    **Examples of caching in Java:**
    
    1. **String pool** – Maintains unique immutable string instances.
    2. **Wrapper classes caching** (`Integer.valueOf()`, `Boolean.valueOf()`) reuse immutable objects.
    3. **Collections** such as `List.of()`, `Map.of()` return immutable collections for safe caching.
    
    **Example: Integer Caching**
    
    ```java
    Integer a = 100;
    Integer b = 100;
    System.out.println(a == b);  // true (cached)
    
    Integer c = 200;
    Integer d = 200;
    System.out.println(c == d);  // false (outside cache range)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. String Class as Prime Example**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The **`String`** class in Java is the most commonly used example of an **immutable class**.
    
    Once a `String` object is created, its **content cannot be changed**.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - All `String` fields are **private and final**.
    - There are **no setter methods**.
    - Any operation like concatenation or substring **creates a new String object** instead of modifying the original.
    
    ### **Example:**
    
    ```java
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            String s1 = "Java";
            String s2 = s1.concat(" Programming");
    
            System.out.println("s1: " + s1);
            System.out.println("s2: " + s2);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    s1: Java
    s2: Java Programming
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - The original string `s1` remains unchanged.
    - A new string `s2` is created in memory.
    
    ---
    
    ### **String Immutability Benefits**
    
    | Benefit | Explanation |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Thread Safety** | Strings can be shared safely between threads without synchronization. |
    | **Security** | Prevents unauthorized modification (e.g., class loaders, network URLs). |
    | **Caching and Pooling** | String literals are stored in the **String constant pool** for reuse. |
    | **Hash Code Stability** | Since values never change, `hashCode()` can be cached for performance in hash-based collections. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    1. **All fields marked `final`** are initialized once during object creation.
    2. The **JVM guarantees visibility** of final fields to all threads after construction.
    3. **Defensive copies** ensure that mutable references are never leaked.
    4. The **String pool** uses immutability to store and reuse identical string objects efficiently.
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    Imagine a **sealed passport**:
    
    - Once issued, its data (name, date of birth) **cannot be altered**.
    - If you need an update, a **new passport** is issued instead.
    - This ensures **trust**, **consistency**, and **security** — just like immutable objects.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Differences Between Mutable and Immutable Objects**
    
    | Aspect | Mutable Object | Immutable Object |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | State Modification | Can be changed after creation | Cannot be changed after creation |
    | Thread Safety | Requires synchronization | Inherently thread-safe |
    | Object Identity | Same object reused | New object created for changes |
    | Memory Usage | May change internally | May create multiple copies |
    | Example | `StringBuilder`, `ArrayList`, `Date` | `String`, `Integer`, Custom Immutable Classes |
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - Always make **fields `final` and `private`**.
    - Avoid returning **mutable references** from getters.
    - Use **defensive copying** when accepting or returning mutable objects.
    - Immutability leads to **safe concurrency** and **predictable behavior**.
    - Immutability is central to **functional programming** paradigms in Java (e.g., streams, lambdas).
    - Many Java standard classes (`String`, `Integer`, `LocalDate`, `BigDecimal`) are **immutable by design**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    - **Immutable objects** cannot have their state changed after creation.
    - To create an immutable class:
        - Use `final` fields and class.
        - No setters, private constructors.
        - Perform **defensive copying**.
    - They are inherently **thread-safe** and ideal for **caching** and **concurrent applications**.
    - The **String** class is the **classic immutable object**, showcasing the benefits of safety, performance, and reusability.
    - Immutability promotes **clarity**, **predictability**, and **robustness** in Java software design.

### 2.4 Inheritance

- **Inheritance Concepts**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Inheritance** in Java is an **object-oriented programming (OOP)** mechanism that allows one class (the **subclass** or **child class**) to **acquire the properties and behaviors** (fields and methods) of another class (the **superclass** or **parent class**).
    
    It promotes **code reusability**, **hierarchical classification**, and establishes an **IS-A relationship** between classes.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - The class being inherited from is called the **superclass**.
    - The class that inherits from it is called the **subclass**.
    - Inheritance is implemented using the **`extends`** keyword.
    - A subclass can:
        - Access **non-private members** of its superclass.
        - **Add new fields and methods**.
        - **Override methods** to provide specialized behavior.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class Superclass {
        // fields and methods
    }
    
    class Subclass extends Superclass {
        // additional fields and methods
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    | Component | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | `class Superclass` | Defines the base or parent class. |
    | `extends` | Keyword used to establish inheritance. |
    | `class Subclass` | Derived class that inherits from the superclass. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Rules and Notes**
    
    1. Java supports **single inheritance only** — one class can extend **only one superclass**.
    2. Inheritance can be **multilevel** (a class inherits another subclass).
    3. **Private members** of the superclass are **not inherited** directly.
    4. The subclass inherits:
        - All **non-private instance variables and methods**.
        - All **public and protected members** of the superclass.
    5. The **constructor** of the superclass is **not inherited**, but it can be **called using `super()`**.
    6. The `extends` keyword is used for **class inheritance**, while `implements` is used for **interfaces**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Example: Basic Inheritance**
    
    ```java
    class Animal {
        void eat() {
            System.out.println("Animal eats food");
        }
    }
    
    class Dog extends Animal {
        void bark() {
            System.out.println("Dog barks");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Dog d = new Dog();
            d.eat();  // Inherited method
            d.bark(); // Subclass method
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Animal eats food
    Dog barks
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - `Dog` inherits from `Animal`.
    - The `Dog` object can access both `eat()` (from `Animal`) and `bark()` (from `Dog`).
    - This demonstrates **code reuse** through inheritance.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. The `extends` Keyword**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The `extends` keyword is used to **create a subclass** that inherits from a **superclass**.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class Subclass extends Superclass {
        // subclass body
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Key Points**
    
    - It establishes an **IS-A relationship** between the classes.
    - It allows the subclass to **reuse**, **override**, or **extend** the functionality of the superclass.
    - It also allows **upcasting**, where a subclass object is referred to by a superclass reference.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Vehicle {
        void start() {
            System.out.println("Vehicle started");
        }
    }
    
    class Car extends Vehicle {
        void drive() {
            System.out.println("Car is driving");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Car c = new Car();
            c.start();  // Access superclass method
            c.drive();  // Access subclass method
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Vehicle started
    Car is driving
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Superclass and Subclass Relationship**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The **superclass-subclass relationship** defines a **hierarchical link** where the subclass inherits the **structure and behavior** of its superclass.
    
    ### **Hierarchy Example**
    
    ```java
    class Person {
        String name;
        void speak() { System.out.println("Person speaks"); }
    }
    
    class Employee extends Person {
        int employeeId;
        void work() { System.out.println("Employee works"); }
    }
    
    class Manager extends Employee {
        void manage() { System.out.println("Manager manages team"); }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Manager m = new Manager();
            m.speak();   // From Person
            m.work();    // From Employee
            m.manage();  // From Manager
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Person speaks
    Employee works
    Manager manages team
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - `Manager` inherits from `Employee`, which inherits from `Person`.
    - This is **multilevel inheritance** — each subclass adds more specialized behavior.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Accessing Superclass Members**
    
    - Subclass can access superclass members directly if they are **public** or **protected**.
    - The `super` keyword is used to:
        - Call **superclass methods** that are overridden.
        - Access **superclass variables**.
        - Invoke the **superclass constructor**.
    
    **Example:**
    
    ```java
    class Parent {
        String name = "Parent";
    
        void display() {
            System.out.println("Display from Parent");
        }
    }
    
    class Child extends Parent {
        String name = "Child";
    
        void show() {
            System.out.println("Superclass name: " + super.name);
            super.display();
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Child c = new Child();
            c.show();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Superclass name: Parent
    Display from Parent
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    The `super` keyword ensures access to superclass members even if they are **hidden or overridden** in the subclass.
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. IS-A Relationship**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The **IS-A relationship** represents a **hierarchical link** between two classes established via **inheritance**, indicating that the subclass **is a type of** the superclass.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - It means a subclass can be treated as an instance of its superclass.
    - For example:
        - `Dog IS-A Animal`
        - `Car IS-A Vehicle`
        - `Manager IS-A Employee`
    
    This relationship supports **polymorphism** — allowing a subclass object to be referenced by a superclass variable.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Demonstrating IS-A Relationship**
    
    ```java
    class Animal {
        void makeSound() { System.out.println("Animal makes sound"); }
    }
    
    class Dog extends Animal {
        void makeSound() { System.out.println("Dog barks"); }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Animal a = new Dog(); // Upcasting
            a.makeSound();        // Calls overridden method in Dog
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Dog barks
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - `Dog IS-A Animal`, so `Animal a = new Dog();` is valid.
    - Even though `a` is of type `Animal`, at runtime it executes `Dog`’s `makeSound()` — an example of **runtime polymorphism**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Real-World Analogy**
    
    Think of an **inheritance hierarchy** like a **taxonomy**:
    
    - **Animal** → **Mammal** → **Dog**
    - A **Dog** is a **Mammal**, and a **Mammal** is an **Animal**.
    - Thus, a **Dog IS-A Animal**, establishing the “is-a” relationship.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    | Phase | Behavior |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Compilation** | The compiler checks inheritance hierarchy and ensures superclass members are visible to the subclass. |
    | **Class Loading** | JVM loads superclass before subclass to ensure proper initialization order. |
    | **Object Creation** | When subclass object is created, **superclass constructors** are executed first (from top to bottom). |
    | **Method Resolution** | The JVM uses **dynamic binding** to decide which overridden method to execute at runtime. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Benefits of Inheritance**
    
    | Benefit | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Code Reusability** | Common functionality in the superclass is reused by all subclasses. |
    | **Extensibility** | Subclasses can extend or modify inherited behavior. |
    | **Maintainability** | Reduces redundancy, improving code organization and maintenance. |
    | **Polymorphism** | Enables one interface to be used for different types of objects. |
    | **Hierarchy Representation** | Models real-world relationships using structured class hierarchies. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Important Points**
    
    - **Constructors are not inherited** but can be **invoked** using `super()`.
    - **Private members** are **not accessible** to subclasses directly.
    - **Protected members** are accessible to subclasses even across packages.
    - Java does **not support multiple inheritance** with classes (use interfaces instead).
    - The **`super` keyword** is essential for accessing hidden or overridden superclass elements.
    - Always use inheritance when there is a **clear IS-A relationship**, not merely for code reuse.
    
    ---
    
    ## **11. Summary**
    
    - Inheritance allows one class (**subclass**) to **acquire the properties and behavior** of another (**superclass**).
    - Implemented using the **`extends` keyword**.
    - Establishes an **IS-A relationship**, enabling **polymorphism** and **code reuse**.
    - The **superclass-subclass hierarchy** defines a logical structure where the subclass can **override**, **extend**, or **reuse** inherited functionality.
    - It is a cornerstone of **Object-Oriented Programming**, promoting **reusability**, **scalability**, and **maintainability** of Java applications.
- **Method Overriding**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Method overriding** in Java occurs when a **subclass** provides a **specific implementation** for a method that is already defined in its **superclass**.
    
    The method in the subclass must have the **same name**, **return type (or covariant type)**, and **parameters** as the method in the parent class.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - Overriding is a key feature of **runtime polymorphism**.
    - It allows a subclass to **modify or extend** the behavior of a method inherited from the parent class.
    - The method to be executed is **determined at runtime** based on the **object type**, not the reference type.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class Superclass {
        void display() {
            System.out.println("Display in Superclass");
        }
    }
    
    class Subclass extends Superclass {
        @Override
        void display() {
            System.out.println("Display in Subclass");
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Rules for Method Overriding**
    
    | Rule | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | **1. Same Method Signature** | The method must have the **same name**, **return type**, and **parameter list** as the superclass method. |
    | **2. Inheritance** | Overriding occurs only between **superclass and subclass**. |
    | **3. Access Modifier** | Cannot reduce visibility (e.g., `public` → `protected`), but can increase it. |
    | **4. Static Methods** | Cannot be overridden — they are **hidden** instead (method hiding). |
    | **5. Final Methods** | Cannot be overridden. |
    | **6. Constructors** | Cannot be overridden (not inherited). |
    | **7. Private Methods** | Cannot be overridden — they are **not visible** to subclasses. |
    | **8. Exception Handling** | The overriding method cannot throw **broader checked exceptions** than the parent method. |
    | **9. Return Type** | Must be **same or covariant** (subtype of parent’s return type). |
    | **10. Annotations** | It is good practice to use the **`@Override` annotation**. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Example: Basic Method Overriding**
    
    ```java
    class Animal {
        void makeSound() {
            System.out.println("Animal makes sound");
        }
    }
    
    class Dog extends Animal {
        @Override
        void makeSound() {
            System.out.println("Dog barks");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Animal a = new Dog();  // Upcasting
            a.makeSound();         // Executes Dog's version
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Dog barks
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - The `makeSound()` method is overridden in `Dog`.
    - The call `a.makeSound()` executes `Dog`’s version because the object type (`Dog`) determines the method at runtime.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. The @Override Annotation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The `@Override` annotation is used to **explicitly indicate** that a method in a subclass is **intended to override** a method in its superclass.
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Improves **readability** and **clarity** of the code.
    - Helps the **compiler detect mistakes** (e.g., method signature mismatch).
    - Prevents accidental **overloading instead of overriding**.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Parent {
        void show() {
            System.out.println("Parent show()");
        }
    }
    
    class Child extends Parent {
        @Override
        void show() {  // Compiler ensures correct overriding
            System.out.println("Child show()");
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Invalid Example (Compilation Error)**
    
    ```java
    class Parent {
        void show() { }
    }
    
    class Child extends Parent {
        @Override
        void show(int x) { }  // ❌ Not overriding (method signature different)
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Error:**
    
    ```
    Method does not override or implement a method from a supertype
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    `@Override` detects the mismatch and prevents logical mistakes in code.
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Covariant Return Types**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **covariant return type** allows an **overriding method** to return a **subclass type** of the original method’s return type.
    
    Introduced in **Java 5**, it provides **type flexibility** while maintaining polymorphism.
    
    ### **Example: Covariant Return Type**
    
    ```java
    class Animal { }
    
    class Dog extends Animal { }
    
    class Parent {
        Animal getAnimal() {
            return new Animal();
        }
    }
    
    class Child extends Parent {
        @Override
        Dog getAnimal() {
            return new Dog();
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Parent p = new Child();
            Animal a = p.getAnimal();  // Returns Dog object
            System.out.println("Returned: " + a.getClass().getSimpleName());
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Returned: Dog
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - The method in `Child` returns a **subtype (`Dog`)** of the return type in `Parent` (`Animal`).
    - This is valid overriding due to **covariant return types**.
    
    ### **Benefits of Covariant Return**
    
    - Eliminates unnecessary casting.
    - Increases code clarity and flexibility.
    - Supports polymorphic hierarchies more naturally.
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Access Modifiers in Overriding**
    
    ### **Rule:**
    
    A subclass **cannot reduce** the visibility of the method it overrides.
    
    | Superclass Modifier | Allowed Modifiers in Subclass |
    | --- | --- |
    | `public` | `public` |
    | `protected` | `protected`, `public` |
    | *default* (package-private) | `default`, `protected`, `public` (if same package) |
    | `private` | Not inherited → cannot be overridden |
    
    ### **Example: Access Modifier Rule**
    
    ```java
    class Parent {
        protected void display() {
            System.out.println("Parent display()");
        }
    }
    
    class Child extends Parent {
        @Override
        public void display() { // ✅ Increased visibility allowed
            System.out.println("Child display()");
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Invalid Example (Compilation Error):**
    
    ```java
    class Parent {
        public void show() { }
    }
    
    class Child extends Parent {
        @Override
        private void show() { }  // ❌ Error: Cannot reduce visibility
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Error:**
    
    ```
    Cannot reduce the visibility of the inherited method from Parent
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Example: Method Overriding with `super`**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The `super` keyword can be used inside a subclass to **call the superclass version** of an overridden method.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Vehicle {
        void start() {
            System.out.println("Vehicle starting...");
        }
    }
    
    class Car extends Vehicle {
        @Override
        void start() {
            super.start();  // calls superclass method
            System.out.println("Car engine started.");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Car c = new Car();
            c.start();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Vehicle starting...
    Car engine started.
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    `super.start()` calls the parent’s implementation before adding additional subclass-specific behavior.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    | Stage | Behavior |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Compile Time** | Compiler checks for proper overriding rules (signature match, access, exceptions). |
    | **Runtime** | JVM performs **dynamic method dispatch** — determines which version (superclass or subclass) to execute based on the actual object type. |
    | **Method Table (vtable)** | JVM maintains a table of method references; overridden methods replace superclass entries for dynamic binding. |
    
    **Dynamic Method Dispatch Example:**
    
    ```java
    Parent p = new Child();
    p.display();  // Executes Child's version (runtime resolution)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Real-world Analogy**
    
    Consider a **base class `Printer`** and a **subclass `ColorPrinter`**:
    
    - A **Printer** knows how to print black-and-white pages.
    - A **ColorPrinter** overrides that method to print in color.
    - When you use a `Printer` reference to refer to a `ColorPrinter`, it still prints in color — the **actual object** decides the behavior at runtime.
    
    ---
    
    ## **11. Differences Between Overloading and Overriding**
    
    | Aspect | Method Overloading | Method Overriding |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Definition** | Same method name, different parameters (within same class) | Same method name, same parameters (in subclass) |
    | **Binding Type** | Compile-time (static polymorphism) | Runtime (dynamic polymorphism) |
    | **Return Type** | Can differ | Must be same or covariant |
    | **Access Modifier** | Independent | Cannot reduce visibility |
    | **Inheritance Required** | No | Yes |
    | **Annotation Used** | Optional | `@Override` recommended |
    | **Purpose** | To increase method flexibility | To modify inherited behavior |
    
    ---
    
    ## **12. Important Points**
    
    - Overriding enables **runtime polymorphism** — a key OOP feature.
    - The **object type**, not the reference type, determines the executed method.
    - Use `@Override` to ensure correctness.
    - **Final**, **static**, and **private** methods cannot be overridden.
    - **Covariant return types** increase flexibility and readability.
    - The **`super`** keyword allows invoking the superclass version of the overridden method.
    - **Access modifiers** in overriding cannot reduce visibility.
    
    ---
    
    ## **13. Summary**
    
    - **Method overriding** allows a subclass to **customize or replace** the behavior of its superclass methods.
    - It enables **dynamic method dispatch**, achieving **runtime polymorphism**.
    - The **`@Override` annotation**, **covariant return types**, and **access control rules** ensure type safety and maintainability.
    - Properly used, overriding provides **extensibility**, **reusability**, and **flexibility**, making it a cornerstone of **object-oriented design** in Java.
- **The super Keyword**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The **`super` keyword** in Java is a **reference variable** used to refer to the **immediate superclass** of the current class.
    
    It allows access to:
    
    1. **Superclass methods** (that may be overridden in the subclass)
    2. **Superclass constructors**
    3. **Superclass fields** (if hidden by subclass variables)
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - Every subclass object implicitly contains a reference to its **parent class** object.
    - The `super` keyword is used when:
        - You want to **invoke a superclass version** of an overridden method.
        - You want to **call a superclass constructor** (explicitly).
        - You need to **access a hidden field** from the superclass.
    
    The `super` keyword helps maintain **inheritance integrity** by giving controlled access to parent features.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Syntax**
    
    ```java
    super.memberName;          // Access superclass field or method
    super.methodName();        // Call superclass method
    super();                   // Call superclass constructor (must be first statement)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Accessing Superclass Methods**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A subclass can call a **method of its superclass** (especially if it’s overridden) using the `super` keyword.
    
    This is useful when the subclass wants to **extend** the functionality of the superclass method rather than completely replace it.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Accessing Overridden Method**
    
    ```java
    class Parent {
        void display() {
            System.out.println("Display method in Parent");
        }
    }
    
    class Child extends Parent {
        @Override
        void display() {
            System.out.println("Display method in Child");
            super.display();  // Call Parent's version
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Child c = new Child();
            c.display();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Display method in Child
    Display method in Parent
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - The subclass `Child` overrides the `display()` method.
    - Inside the overridden method, `super.display()` calls the **parent class method** to execute its version.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Accessing Superclass Fields**
    
    ```java
    class Vehicle {
        int speed = 60;
    }
    
    class Car extends Vehicle {
        int speed = 100;
    
        void showSpeed() {
            System.out.println("Car speed: " + speed);
            System.out.println("Vehicle speed: " + super.speed);
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Car c = new Car();
            c.showSpeed();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Car speed: 100
    Vehicle speed: 60
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - `super.speed` explicitly accesses the **parent class field** when the subclass has a field with the same name.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Accessing Superclass Constructors**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The `super()` statement is used to **call a constructor** of the **immediate superclass**.
    
    It ensures that the parent class is properly **initialized** before the subclass adds its own initialization logic.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    super();               // Calls superclass default constructor
    super(arguments);      // Calls superclass parameterized constructor
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Rules**
    
    1. The `super()` call **must be the first statement** inside a subclass constructor.
    2. If not explicitly called, the compiler automatically inserts a **`super()`** (default constructor call).
    3. `super()` can be used only in **constructors**, not in methods.
    4. Only **one `super()`** call is allowed per constructor.
    5. If the superclass doesn’t have a **default constructor**, the subclass **must call a parameterized constructor** explicitly.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Implicit `super()` Call**
    
    ```java
    class Parent {
        Parent() {
            System.out.println("Parent constructor called");
        }
    }
    
    class Child extends Parent {
        Child() {
            System.out.println("Child constructor called");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            new Child();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Parent constructor called
    Child constructor called
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    The compiler automatically inserts `super()` at the beginning of the `Child` constructor, ensuring the `Parent` class is constructed first.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Explicit `super()` Call**
    
    ```java
    class Parent {
        Parent(String msg) {
            System.out.println("Parent constructor: " + msg);
        }
    }
    
    class Child extends Parent {
        Child() {
            super("Hello from Child"); // Explicit call to Parent constructor
            System.out.println("Child constructor executed");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            new Child();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Parent constructor: Hello from Child
    Child constructor executed
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    The `super("Hello from Child")` explicitly calls the **parameterized constructor** of the parent class.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Constructor Chaining**
    
    ```java
    class A {
        A() {
            System.out.println("Constructor of A");
        }
    }
    
    class B extends A {
        B() {
            super(); // Calls constructor of A
            System.out.println("Constructor of B");
        }
    }
    
    class C extends B {
        C() {
            super(); // Calls constructor of B
            System.out.println("Constructor of C");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            new C();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Constructor of A
    Constructor of B
    Constructor of C
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - Constructors execute from **top (superclass)** to **bottom (subclass)**.
    - This process is known as **constructor chaining**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. The `super()` Constructor Call**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    `super()` is a special form of the `super` keyword used to **explicitly invoke a superclass constructor**.
    
    It ensures proper **initialization order** in inheritance hierarchies.
    
    ### **Rules Recap**
    
    - Always the **first statement** in a subclass constructor.
    - Calls **superclass’s constructor** (default or parameterized).
    - **Cannot** be used in regular methods.
    - **Only one** `super()` per constructor.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: With Multiple Constructors**
    
    ```java
    class Vehicle {
        Vehicle() {
            System.out.println("Default Vehicle Constructor");
        }
    
        Vehicle(String type) {
            System.out.println("Vehicle Type: " + type);
        }
    }
    
    class Car extends Vehicle {
        Car() {
            super("Car"); // Call specific parent constructor
            System.out.println("Car constructor called");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            new Car();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Vehicle Type: Car
    Car constructor called
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    `super("Car")` calls the **parameterized constructor** of `Vehicle` instead of the default one.
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    | Phase | Behavior |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Compilation** | Ensures that if `super()` is not explicitly called, the compiler inserts an implicit `super()` to call the superclass’s default constructor. |
    | **Object Creation** | JVM first allocates memory for the subclass object, then invokes the **superclass constructor chain** before initializing subclass-specific fields. |
    | **Execution Order** | Superclass constructors → Subclass constructors → Subclass methods. |
    
    **Execution Sequence Example:**
    
    1. Memory allocated for subclass object.
    2. Superclass constructor called (`super()` automatically inserted).
    3. Subclass constructor executes.
    4. Object reference returned.
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    Imagine an **Employee hierarchy**:
    
    - When you create a **Manager** object (subclass), the **Employee** (superclass) portion must be constructed first.
    - You can call specific **Employee constructors** (like one setting `name` or `id`) using `super()` inside the `Manager` constructor.
    - Similarly, if a `Manager` reuses some `Employee` methods but adds extra functionality, it can call `super.calculateSalary()` inside its own `calculateSalary()`.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Differences Between `super` and `this`**
    
    | Feature | `super` | `this` |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | Refers To | Immediate superclass object | Current object |
    | Used For | Accessing superclass members | Accessing current class members |
    | Constructor Call | Calls superclass constructor (`super()`) | Calls another constructor in the same class (`this()`) |
    | Use in Methods | Access overridden methods or hidden variables | Call current class fields or methods |
    | Must Be First Statement | Only when used in constructor | Only when used in constructor |
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - The `super` keyword is available **only inside subclass constructors or methods**.
    - The compiler automatically inserts a **`super()`** call if no explicit constructor call is made.
    - `super()` and `this()` **cannot appear together** in the same constructor (only one call allowed).
    - It helps in **constructor chaining** and **method extension** without breaking encapsulation.
    - Always use `super` when accessing hidden or overridden superclass members for clarity and maintainability.
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    - **`super`** is a reference to the **immediate superclass** of the current class.
    - It is used to:
        - Access **superclass methods or fields**.
        - Call **superclass constructors** (`super()` or `super(args)`).
    - The **`super()`** call ensures **constructor chaining** — superclass constructors run before subclass ones.
    - It plays a vital role in **inheritance**, **overriding**, and **object initialization order**.
    - Correct use of `super` ensures **clean, reusable, and maintainable** inheritance-based designs in Java.
- **Types of Inheritance**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Inheritance** is an **object-oriented programming (OOP)** concept where one class (the **subclass** or **child class**) acquires the **fields and methods** of another class (the **superclass** or **parent class**).
    
    In Java, inheritance helps in:
    
    - **Code reusability** (reuse existing logic)
    - **Extensibility** (extend behavior)
    - **Hierarchical classification** (build real-world relationships)
    - Establishing the **IS-A relationship**
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - The relationship between superclass and subclass is established using the **`extends`** keyword.
    - Java supports different types of inheritance structures:
        1. **Single Inheritance**
        2. **Multilevel Inheritance**
        3. **Hierarchical Inheritance**
        4. **Multiple Inheritance (via interfaces)**
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Types of Inheritance in Java**
    
    Java supports **class-based** and **interface-based** inheritance structures.
    
    It does **not support multiple inheritance with classes** to prevent ambiguity (Diamond Problem).
    
    ---
    
    ### **Overview Diagram**
    
    ```
              Object
                 │
            ┌────┼────┐
            │         │
          Class A   Interface
            │          │
            ├────┐     │
            │    │     │
          Class B ...  Class C (implements multiple interfaces)
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Single Inheritance**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Single Inheritance** is when a subclass inherits from a **single superclass**.
    
    This is the **simplest form** of inheritance.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class Parent {
        // Parent class members
    }
    
    class Child extends Parent {
        // Child class members
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Vehicle {
        void start() {
            System.out.println("Vehicle started");
        }
    }
    
    class Car extends Vehicle {
        void drive() {
            System.out.println("Car is driving");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Car c = new Car();
            c.start();  // Inherited from Vehicle
            c.drive();  // Defined in Car
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Vehicle started
    Car is driving
    
    ```
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - The `Car` class inherits from `Vehicle`.
    - It can access both its own methods and inherited ones.
    
    ### **Use Case**
    
    - When one class **directly extends** another to **reuse functionality**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Multilevel Inheritance**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Multilevel Inheritance** occurs when a class is derived from another **subclass**, forming a **chain of inheritance**.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class A { }
    class B extends A { }
    class C extends B { }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Animal {
        void eat() {
            System.out.println("Animal eats food");
        }
    }
    
    class Mammal extends Animal {
        void breathe() {
            System.out.println("Mammal breathes air");
        }
    }
    
    class Dog extends Mammal {
        void bark() {
            System.out.println("Dog barks");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Dog d = new Dog();
            d.eat();     // From Animal
            d.breathe(); // From Mammal
            d.bark();    // From Dog
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Animal eats food
    Mammal breathes air
    Dog barks
    
    ```
    
    ### **Explanation**
