    - The `Dog` class inherits properties from both `Mammal` and `Animal`.
    - This forms an **inheritance chain**:
        
        `Animal → Mammal → Dog`
        
    
    ### **Use Case**
    
    - When a system models **progressive specialization** (e.g., `LivingThing → Animal → Mammal → Human`).
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Hierarchical Inheritance**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Hierarchical Inheritance** occurs when **multiple subclasses** inherit from the **same superclass**.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class SuperClass { }
    class SubClass1 extends SuperClass { }
    class SubClass2 extends SuperClass { }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Shape {
        void draw() {
            System.out.println("Drawing a shape");
        }
    }
    
    class Circle extends Shape {
        void area() {
            System.out.println("Area = π * r * r");
        }
    }
    
    class Rectangle extends Shape {
        void area() {
            System.out.println("Area = length * breadth");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Circle c = new Circle();
            c.draw();
            c.area();
    
            Rectangle r = new Rectangle();
            r.draw();
            r.area();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Drawing a shape
    Area = π * r * r
    Drawing a shape
    Area = length * breadth
    
    ```
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - Both `Circle` and `Rectangle` inherit from `Shape`.
    - Each subclass provides its own specialized implementation.
    
    ### **Use Case**
    
    - When you have **multiple specialized subclasses** of a **common base class** (e.g., `Employee → Manager`, `Employee → Developer`).
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Multiple Inheritance (Through Interfaces)**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Java does **not support multiple inheritance through classes** (to avoid ambiguity and the **Diamond Problem**).
    
    However, it supports **multiple inheritance through interfaces**, since interfaces only contain **method declarations** (no conflicting state).
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    interface Interface1 { }
    interface Interface2 { }
    class Subclass implements Interface1, Interface2 { }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    interface Printable {
        void print();
    }
    
    interface Showable {
        void show();
    }
    
    class Demo implements Printable, Showable {
        public void print() {
            System.out.println("Printing something...");
        }
    
        public void show() {
            System.out.println("Showing something...");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Demo d = new Demo();
            d.print();
            d.show();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Printing something...
    Showing something...
    
    ```
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - `Demo` implements both `Printable` and `Showable`.
    - Both interface methods are defined by the class, resolving potential conflicts.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Multiple Inheritance with Default Methods**
    
    When two interfaces have the **same default method**, the implementing class must **override** it explicitly.
    
    ```java
    interface A {
        default void show() {
            System.out.println("Interface A");
        }
    }
    
    interface B {
        default void show() {
            System.out.println("Interface B");
        }
    }
    
    class C implements A, B {
        // Resolve conflict by overriding
        public void show() {
            System.out.println("Overridden method from A and B");
            A.super.show(); // Optionally call A’s version
            B.super.show(); // Optionally call B’s version
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            new C().show();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Overridden method from A and B
    Interface A
    Interface B
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - Both interfaces provide a default `show()` method.
    - Class `C` resolves ambiguity by providing its own implementation.
    - Optional calls to specific interface versions using `InterfaceName.super.methodName()`.
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Why Multiple Inheritance with Classes is Not Allowed**
    
    ### **Problem: Diamond Ambiguity**
    
    ### **Example (Hypothetical)**
    
    ```java
    class A {
        void display() { System.out.println("A"); }
    }
    
    class B extends A {
        void display() { System.out.println("B"); }
    }
    
    class C extends A {
        void display() { System.out.println("C"); }
    }
    
    // ❌ Not allowed: Which display() should D inherit?
    class D extends B, C { }
    
    ```
    
    **Ambiguity:**
    
    If both `B` and `C` define `display()`, the JVM cannot determine **which version** to inherit in `D`.
    
    Hence, Java avoids **multiple inheritance through classes**, allowing it only via **interfaces**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    | Type | Behavior |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Single** | Simple inheritance tree; superclass loaded before subclass. |
    | **Multilevel** | Constructor chaining happens top-down (from parent to child). |
    | **Hierarchical** | Each subclass inherits independently from the same superclass. |
    | **Multiple (Interfaces)** | Compiler merges all interface declarations; implementing class provides concrete implementations. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Inheritance Type | Real-world Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Single** | A *Manager* inherits properties from an *Employee*. |
    | **Multilevel** | A *Manager* inherits from *Employee*, which inherits from *Person*. |
    | **Hierarchical** | Both *Manager* and *Developer* inherit from *Employee*. |
    | **Multiple (Interfaces)** | A *SmartPhone* implements *Camera*, *MusicPlayer*, and *GPS* functionalities simultaneously. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Differences Between Class and Interface Inheritance**
    
    | Aspect | Class Inheritance (`extends`) | Interface Inheritance (`implements`) |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | Keyword | `extends` | `implements` |
    | Multiple Inheritance | ❌ Not allowed | ✅ Allowed |
    | Members | Variables + Methods | Abstract + Default + Static methods |
    | State | Can maintain state (fields) | No instance state |
    | Purpose | Code reuse | Define capabilities or contracts |
    
    ---
    
    ## **11. Important Points**
    
    - Java supports **single, multilevel, and hierarchical** inheritance using classes.
    - **Multiple inheritance** is supported **only through interfaces**.
    - Every Java class implicitly inherits from the **`Object` class** (topmost parent).
    - The **`super` keyword** can be used to invoke superclass members in all inheritance types.
    - Proper inheritance design avoids redundancy and improves **maintainability**.
    - **Constructor chaining** always starts from the **topmost superclass**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **12. Summary**
    
    - **Inheritance** enables a class to acquire the properties and behavior of another class.
    - **Types:**
        - **Single Inheritance** – One subclass inherits from one superclass.
        - **Multilevel Inheritance** – A chain of inheritance (A → B → C).
        - **Hierarchical Inheritance** – Multiple subclasses inherit from one superclass.
        - **Multiple Inheritance (via interfaces)** – A class implements multiple interfaces.
    - Java avoids multiple inheritance with classes to prevent ambiguity but achieves it **safely through interfaces**.
    - Inheritance forms the foundation of **polymorphism**, **encapsulation**, and **code reuse** in Java’s OOP architecture.
- **Object Class** (**ENHANCED**)
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    In Java, the **`Object` class** is the **root superclass** of all classes.
    
    Every class in Java **implicitly extends** the `java.lang.Object` class — either directly or indirectly — making it the **ultimate ancestor** of all Java classes.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - Since every class inherits from `Object`, it provides a **common set of methods** that all objects share.
    - These methods include fundamental operations like **comparison**, **hashing**, **string representation**, **cloning**, and **finalization**.
    - The `Object` class resides in the `java.lang` package and does not need to be explicitly imported.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Hierarchy Example**
    
    ```
    java.lang.Object
           │
       java.util.Date
           │
       java.sql.Timestamp
    
    ```
    
    Even user-defined classes follow this hierarchy:
    
    ```java
    class Student { }
    // Internally treated as:
    class Student extends Object { }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Object Class Overview**
    
    | **Method** | **Purpose** |
    | --- | --- |
    | `toString()` | Returns a string representation of the object |
    | `equals(Object obj)` | Compares two objects for equality |
    | `hashCode()` | Returns an integer hash code for the object |
    | `getClass()` | Returns runtime class information |
    | `clone()` | Creates and returns a copy of the object |
    | `finalize()` | Called by GC before object destruction *(deprecated)* |
    | `wait(), notify(), notifyAll()` | Used for thread synchronization |
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Root of All Classes**
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - The `Object` class is the **root** of the Java class hierarchy.
    - All classes **inherit its methods**, even if not explicitly mentioned.
    - This allows **universal method availability** across all Java objects (e.g., `equals`, `hashCode`, `toString`).
    - It also allows **polymorphic behavior** — since any object can be treated as an instance of `Object`.
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Object obj1 = new String("Java");
            Object obj2 = new Integer(10);
    
            System.out.println(obj1.toString()); // Calls String.toString()
            System.out.println(obj2.toString()); // Calls Integer.toString()
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Java
    10
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    Although both are `Object` references, the actual method called depends on the runtime type — demonstrating **dynamic method dispatch**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Important Methods in Object Class**
    
    ---
    
    ### **A. toString()**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The `toString()` method returns a **string representation** of the object.
    
    The default implementation in `Object` returns the **class name** followed by **@** and the object’s **hash code** in hexadecimal form.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    public String toString();
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example: Default Implementation**
    
    ```java
    class Student {
        int id = 1;
        String name = "Alice";
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Student s = new Student();
            System.out.println(s.toString());
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Student@4617c264
    
    ```
    
    ### **Overriding toString()**
    
    ```java
    class Student {
        int id;
        String name;
        Student(int id, String name) {
            this.id = id;
            this.name = name;
        }
    
        @Override
        public String toString() {
            return "Student[id=" + id + ", name=" + name + "]";
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Student s = new Student(1, "Alice");
            System.out.println(s);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Student[id=1, name=Alice]
    
    ```
    
    **Purpose:** Improves readability for logging, debugging, and printing object details.
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. equals()**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The `equals()` method checks whether two objects are **logically equal**.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    public boolean equals(Object obj);
    
    ```
    
    ### **Default Behavior**
    
    By default, `equals()` in `Object` performs **reference comparison** (checks if both references point to the same object).
    
    ### **Overriding equals()**
    
    To compare contents, override this method.
    
    ```java
    class Student {
        int id;
        String name;
    
        Student(int id, String name) {
            this.id = id;
            this.name = name;
        }
    
        @Override
        public boolean equals(Object obj) {
            if (this == obj) return true; // Same reference
            if (obj == null || getClass() != obj.getClass()) return false;
    
            Student s = (Student) obj;
            return id == s.id && name.equals(s.name);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Student s1 = new Student(1, "Alice");
            Student s2 = new Student(1, "Alice");
    
            System.out.println(s1.equals(s2));
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    true
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **C. hashCode()**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The `hashCode()` method returns an **integer hash code** for the object, used primarily in **hash-based collections** (e.g., `HashMap`, `HashSet`).
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    public int hashCode();
    
    ```
    
    ### **Default Behavior**
    
    By default, `Object.hashCode()` returns a **memory-based hash value**, which differs for different objects.
    
    ### **Overriding hashCode()**
    
    When `equals()` is overridden, `hashCode()` **must also be overridden** to maintain consistency.
    
    ```java
    @Override
    public int hashCode() {
        return Objects.hash(id, name);
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. equals() and hashCode() Contract**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The **equals-hashCode contract** defines a **mandatory relationship** between these two methods, ensuring consistent behavior in collections like `HashMap` and `HashSet`.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Contract Rules**
    
    1. **If two objects are equal according to `equals()`, they must have the same `hashCode()`.**
    2. **If two objects have the same `hashCode()`, they may or may not be equal.**
    3. **During execution**, multiple calls to `hashCode()` for the same object must return the same value.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Why It Matters**
    
    Collections like `HashMap` and `HashSet` use both methods internally:
    
    - `hashCode()` determines the **bucket** (group of entries).
    - `equals()` determines the **exact match** inside that bucket.
    
    Incorrect implementation leads to:
    
    - Duplicate entries in sets.
    - Missing keys or retrieval failures in hash maps.
    - Poor performance (increased collision handling).
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Proper Implementation**
    
    ```java
    import java.util.Objects;
    
    class Employee {
        int id;
        String name;
    
        Employee(int id, String name) {
            this.id = id;
            this.name = name;
        }
    
        @Override
        public boolean equals(Object obj) {
            if (this == obj) return true;
            if (!(obj instanceof Employee)) return false;
            Employee e = (Employee) obj;
            return id == e.id && Objects.equals(name, e.name);
        }
    
        @Override
        public int hashCode() {
            return Objects.hash(id, name);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Impact on HashMap**
    
    ```java
    import java.util.*;
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Map<Employee, String> map = new HashMap<>();
            map.put(new Employee(1, "John"), "Developer");
            map.put(new Employee(1, "John"), "Tester");
    
            System.out.println("Map size: " + map.size());
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Map size: 1
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    Because `equals()` and `hashCode()` are properly overridden, duplicate keys are detected and replaced, ensuring correct behavior.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Null-Safe Implementation**
    
    Use `Objects.equals()` and `Objects.hash()` for **null safety**:
    
    ```java
    @Override
    public boolean equals(Object obj) {
        if (this == obj) return true;
        if (!(obj instanceof Employee)) return false;
        Employee e = (Employee) obj;
        return id == e.id && Objects.equals(name, e.name);
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. clone() Method and Cloneable Interface**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The `clone()` method is used to **create a copy of an object**.
    
    It’s declared in the `Object` class and must be explicitly overridden.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    protected Object clone() throws CloneNotSupportedException;
    
    ```
    
    ### **Important Points**
    
    - The class must **implement `Cloneable`**; otherwise, `CloneNotSupportedException` is thrown.
    - The **default clone()** performs **shallow copying**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **A. Shallow Copy Example**
    
    ```java
    class Address {
        String city;
        Address(String city) { this.city = city; }
    }
    
    class Person implements Cloneable {
        String name;
        Address address;
    
        Person(String name, Address address) {
            this.name = name;
            this.address = address;
        }
    
        @Override
        protected Object clone() throws CloneNotSupportedException {
            return super.clone(); // Shallow copy
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) throws CloneNotSupportedException {
            Address addr = new Address("Delhi");
            Person p1 = new Person("Alice", addr);
            Person p2 = (Person) p1.clone();
    
            p2.address.city = "Mumbai";
    
            System.out.println(p1.address.city); // Affected → "Mumbai"
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Mumbai
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    Shallow copy duplicates object references, not nested objects.
    
    Both `p1` and `p2` share the same `Address` reference.
    
    ---
    
    ### **B. Deep Copy Example**
    
    ```java
    class Person implements Cloneable {
        String name;
        Address address;
    
        Person(String name, Address address) {
            this.name = name;
            this.address = address;
        }
    
        @Override
        protected Object clone() throws CloneNotSupportedException {
            Person cloned = (Person) super.clone();
            cloned.address = new Address(address.city); // Deep copy
            return cloned;
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    Now `p1` and `p2` have **independent copies**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Why Cloneable is Considered a Broken Pattern**
    
    1. **Design flaw** — Cloneable doesn’t define the `clone()` method itself.
    2. **Inconsistent semantics** — Shallow vs deep copy behavior is confusing.
    3. **Violates encapsulation** — Bypasses constructors.
    4. **Better alternatives** — Use:
        - **Copy constructors**
        - **Static factory methods**
        - **Serialization-based cloning**
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. finalize() Method (Deprecated)**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The `finalize()` method was used as a **cleanup mechanism** before an object is garbage collected.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    @Deprecated
    protected void finalize() throws Throwable;
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Demo {
        @Override
        protected void finalize() throws Throwable {
            System.out.println("Finalize called before GC");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Demo d = new Demo();
            d = null;
            System.gc();  // Request GC (no guarantee)
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Possible Output**
    
    ```
    Finalize called before GC
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Deprecation Warning**
    
    As of **Java 9**, `finalize()` is **deprecated** because:
    
    - It’s **unreliable** (GC timing not guaranteed).
    - Causes **performance overhead**.
    - May lead to **resource leaks** if misused.
    
    **Preferred Alternatives:**
    
    - Use **`try-with-resources`** or **`AutoCloseable`** for cleanup.
    - Example:
        
        ```java
        try (FileInputStream fis = new FileInputStream("data.txt")) {
            // Work with resource
        } // Automatically closed
        
        ```
        
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    | Operation | Behavior |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Inheritance** | Every class implicitly inherits `Object` methods. |
    | **Method Override** | `toString()`, `equals()`, `hashCode()`, and `clone()` are often overridden. |
    | **Garbage Collection** | Calls `finalize()` (if present) before reclaiming memory — now deprecated. |
    | **hashCode()** | Used internally by hash-based collections for bucket placement. |
    | **clone()** | Performs field-by-field copy using native memory duplication. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - `Object` is the **root of all Java classes**.
    - Methods like `equals()`, `toString()`, and `hashCode()` should be **overridden consistently**.
    - Follow the **equals-hashCode contract** to avoid logical errors in collections.
    - Prefer **copy constructors** over `clone()` for object duplication.
    - Avoid `finalize()` — use **try-with-resources** or **cleaner APIs** for cleanup.
    - Every object inherits these methods, ensuring a **uniform behavior model** across Java.
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    - The **`Object` class** is the **universal superclass** in Java.
    - It defines essential methods for **identity**, **comparison**, **cloning**, and **resource cleanup**.
    - **Key methods:**
        - `toString()` → Human-readable object info
        - `equals()` + `hashCode()` → Object equality & hashing contract
        - `clone()` → Object duplication (use cautiously)
        - `finalize()` → Deprecated cleanup method
    - Proper overriding of these methods ensures **predictable**, **efficient**, and **reliable** object behavior across all Java applications.

### 2.5 Polymorphism

- **Compile-time Polymorphism**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Compile-time polymorphism**, also known as **static polymorphism** or **early binding**, is a form of polymorphism in which the **method call is resolved at compile time**.
    
    In Java, this is primarily achieved through **method overloading**.
    
    Unlike runtime polymorphism (method overriding), compile-time polymorphism does not depend on the **object type at runtime** — it depends on the **method signature at compile time**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - The Java compiler determines **which method version to call** based on:
        - The **method name**,
        - The **number of parameters**,
        - The **types of parameters**, and
        - The **order of parameters**.
    - The process of choosing the appropriate method is called **method resolution**.
    - This provides flexibility to use the **same method name** for different data types or operations.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Method Overloading**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Method overloading** occurs when **multiple methods** in the same class have the **same name** but **different parameter lists** (different number, type, or order of parameters).
    
    It allows methods to perform **similar operations** with different types or numbers of inputs.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class ClassName {
        void methodName(int a) { }
        void methodName(int a, int b) { }
        void methodName(double a) { }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Rules for Method Overloading**
    
    | Rule | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | **1. Same method name** | All methods must share the same name. |
    | **2. Different parameter list** | Methods must differ by number, type, or order of parameters. |
    | **3. Return type does not matter** | Changing only the return type does **not** constitute overloading. |
    | **4. Access modifiers can vary** | But overloading is based on method signature only. |
    | **5. Can occur in same or subclass** | Methods can be overloaded within a single class or between superclass and subclass. |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Method Overloading**
    
    ```java
    class Calculator {
        // Overloaded methods
        int add(int a, int b) {
            return a + b;
        }
    
        double add(double a, double b) {
            return a + b;
        }
    
        int add(int a, int b, int c) {
            return a + b + c;
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Calculator calc = new Calculator();
            System.out.println(calc.add(10, 20));        // int version
            System.out.println(calc.add(5.5, 4.5));      // double version
            System.out.println(calc.add(1, 2, 3));       // 3-parameter version
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    30
    10.0
    6
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    The compiler selects the appropriate method version based on **argument type and count**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Overloading by Parameter Order**
    
    ```java
    class Display {
        void show(String s, int n) {
            System.out.println("String: " + s + ", Int: " + n);
        }
    
        void show(int n, String s) {
            System.out.println("Int: " + n + ", String: " + s);
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Display d = new Display();
            d.show("Java", 11);
            d.show(11, "Java");
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    String: Java, Int: 11
    Int: 11, String: Java
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Invalid Case (Only Return Type Changed)**
    
    ```java
    class Test {
        int display() { return 10; }
        // ❌ Not allowed — same parameters, different return type
        double display() { return 20.0; }
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Compilation Error:**
    
    ```
    method display() is already defined in class Test
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Overloading with Type Promotion**
    
    When exact match is not found, Java **promotes smaller data types** automatically.
    
    | Promoted From | Promoted To |
    | --- | --- |
    | `byte` → `short` → `int` → `long` → `float` → `double` |  |
    | `char` → `int` |  |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Type Promotion**
    
    ```java
    class Demo {
        void show(int a) {
            System.out.println("int version");
        }
    
        void show(double a) {
            System.out.println("double version");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Demo d = new Demo();
            d.show(10);     // int version
            d.show(10.5f);  // float promoted to double
            d.show('A');    // char promoted to int
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    int version
    double version
    int version
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Operator Overloading (Limited in Java)**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Operator overloading** refers to giving **additional meanings** to operators (like `+`, `-`, `*`, etc.) for **user-defined types** (objects).
    
    In some languages (like C++), operators can be explicitly overloaded.
    
    **Java does not support operator overloading** (except for a few limited cases).
    
    ---
    
    ### **Reason**
    
    To **maintain simplicity, clarity, and safety** in Java.
    
    Allowing arbitrary operator overloading could lead to:
    
    - Confusing and unreadable code.
    - Ambiguous semantics.
    - Security and performance issues.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Supported Operator Overloading in Java**
    
    Java performs **limited, built-in operator overloading** for a few cases only.
    
    | Operator | Overloaded For | Example | Behavior |
    | --- | --- | --- | --- |
    | `+` | `String` concatenation | `"Java" + " Rocks"` | Concatenates strings |
    | `+` | Numeric addition | `5 + 3` | Adds numbers |
    | `+=` | Strings and numbers | `s += "World"` | Appends to string |
    | `==`, `!=` | Object references | `obj1 == obj2` | Compares references, not values |
    | `+` (with mixed types) | Implicit conversion | `"Sum: " + 10` | Converts number to string then concatenates |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: String Concatenation (Built-in Overloading)**
    
    ```java
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            String s1 = "Java";
            String s2 = "Programming";
            System.out.println(s1 + " " + s2);  // String concatenation
            System.out.println("Result: " + (10 + 20)); // Arithmetic inside parentheses
            System.out.println(10 + 20 + " Result");    // Numeric addition before concatenation
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Java Programming
    Result: 30
    30 Result
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    The `+` operator behaves differently for strings and numbers — a form of **built-in operator overloading**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Why Java Disallows Custom Operator Overloading**
    
    | Reason | Explanation |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Readability** | Avoids confusion caused by overloaded operators behaving differently. |
    | **Simplicity** | Keeps language simpler and predictable. |
    | **Maintainability** | Prevents misuse where operators perform unexpected actions. |
    | **Consistency** | Ensures uniform behavior across all classes. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    | Phase | Behavior |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Compilation** | Compiler performs **method resolution** (binds method call to specific signature). |
    | **Execution** | No runtime resolution — method binding is **already done at compile time**. |
    | **Overloaded Methods** | Stored in the **method table** (symbol table) with distinct signatures. |
    | **Operator Overloading** | Handled internally by compiler rules (only for built-in types and strings). |
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Real-world Analogy**
    
    Think of a **universal “calculate()” function** that works differently depending on inputs:
    
    - `calculate(int a, int b)` → Adds two numbers.
    - `calculate(double a, double b)` → Adds decimals.
    - `calculate(String a, String b)` → Concatenates text.
    
    This is similar to method overloading — **same name, different forms** (polymorphism in action).
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Differences Between Compile-time and Runtime Polymorphism**
    
    | Feature | Compile-time Polymorphism | Runtime Polymorphism |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Also Known As** | Static binding / Early binding | Dynamic binding / Late binding |
    | **Achieved By** | Method Overloading | Method Overriding |
    | **Binding Time** | Compile-time | Runtime |
    | **Involves** | Same class | Superclass–Subclass |
    | **Return Type** | Can differ | Must be same or covariant |
    | **Performance** | Faster (no runtime lookup) | Slightly slower (runtime method resolution) |
    | **Example** | `sum(int, int)` and `sum(double, double)` | `display()` overridden in subclass |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Important Points**
    
    - Compile-time polymorphism = **Method Overloading** + limited **Operator Overloading**.
    - The compiler decides which method to call **based on the method signature**.
    - Overloading increases **code readability** and **reusability**.
    - **Return type alone cannot differentiate** overloaded methods.
    - **Operator overloading** is **restricted** in Java to avoid complexity.
    - The **`+` operator** is the only example of intentional operator overloading for strings.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Summary**
    
    - **Compile-time polymorphism** means **method binding occurs during compilation**.
    - It is primarily achieved through **method overloading**.
    - Overloading allows multiple methods with the same name but different parameter lists.
    - **Operator overloading** in Java is **limited and built-in** (mainly for string concatenation and primitive arithmetic).
    - This type of polymorphism enhances **flexibility**, **readability**, and **maintainability**, while keeping Java **safe and consistent**.
- **Runtime Polymorphism**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Runtime polymorphism**, also known as **dynamic polymorphism** or **late binding**, is a type of polymorphism in which the **method to be executed is determined at runtime**, not at compile time.
    
    It is primarily achieved through **method overriding** and **dynamic method dispatch** in Java.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - In runtime polymorphism, a **reference variable of the superclass** can refer to an **object of a subclass**.
    - The method that gets executed depends on the **actual object type** the reference variable points to, **not the reference type**.
    - The decision of which method to call is made **by the JVM at runtime**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Method Overriding**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Method overriding** occurs when a subclass provides a **specific implementation** of a method already defined in its superclass, with the **same name, parameters, and return type** (or covariant type).
    
    ### **Key Requirements**
    
    1. The method must exist in both superclass and subclass.
    2. The subclass method must have the **same method signature**.
    3. It must not have a **narrower access modifier**.
    4. **Static, final, or private** methods cannot be overridden.
    5. Overriding supports **runtime polymorphism** because method calls are resolved **at runtime**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Method Overriding**
    
    ```java
    class Animal {
        void sound() {
            System.out.println("Animal makes a sound");
        }
    }
    
    class Dog extends Animal {
        @Override
        void sound() {
            System.out.println("Dog barks");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Animal a = new Dog(); // Upcasting
            a.sound();            // Calls overridden method in Dog
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Dog barks
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    Although the reference type is `Animal`, the actual object is `Dog`.
    
    The JVM determines at runtime which version of `sound()` to execute — this is **runtime polymorphism**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Dynamic Method Dispatch**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Dynamic method dispatch** is the **mechanism** through which a **method call to an overridden method** is resolved **at runtime** rather than compile time.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - When a superclass reference variable refers to a subclass object, the JVM uses **dynamic dispatch tables (v-tables)** to determine which method implementation to invoke.
    - It ensures the correct overridden method of the actual object type is executed, even when the reference is of the parent type.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Dynamic Method Dispatch**
    
    ```java
    class Shape {
        void draw() {
            System.out.println("Drawing a generic shape");
        }
    }
    
    class Circle extends Shape {
        @Override
        void draw() {
            System.out.println("Drawing a circle");
        }
    }
    
    class Rectangle extends Shape {
        @Override
        void draw() {
            System.out.println("Drawing a rectangle");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Shape s;
    
            s = new Circle();
            s.draw();   // Circle's draw() called
    
            s = new Rectangle();
            s.draw();   // Rectangle's draw() called
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Drawing a circle
    Drawing a rectangle
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    The same reference `Shape s` calls different methods depending on the actual object type (`Circle` or `Rectangle`) — the decision is made **at runtime**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Real-world Analogy**
    
    Imagine a **remote control (superclass reference)** that can operate different devices (TV, AC, Fan — subclass objects).
    
    When you press the **“turn on”** button, the actual action (starting TV, cooling room, or spinning blades) depends on which **device** the remote currently controls — decided **at runtime**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Virtual Method Invocation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    In Java, **all non-static, non-final methods** are **virtual methods**.
    
    This means that their **method calls are dynamically resolved** at runtime based on the actual object type, not the declared reference type.
    
    ---
    
    ### **How Virtual Methods Work**
    
    - Each class has a **virtual method table (v-table)** maintained by the JVM.
    - When a method is called on an object, the JVM looks up the method’s address in the object’s v-table and invokes it.
    - The v-table is built **at class loading time**, and **overridden methods** replace the superclass entries.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Virtual Method Invocation**
    
    ```java
    class Payment {
        void pay() {
            System.out.println("Processing generic payment");
        }
    }
    
    class CreditCardPayment extends Payment {
        @Override
        void pay() {
            System.out.println("Processing credit card payment");
        }
    }
    
    class UpiPayment extends Payment {
        @Override
        void pay() {
            System.out.println("Processing UPI payment");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Payment p1 = new CreditCardPayment();
            Payment p2 = new UpiPayment();
    
            p1.pay(); // Resolved at runtime → CreditCardPayment.pay()
            p2.pay(); // Resolved at runtime → UpiPayment.pay()
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Processing credit card payment
    Processing UPI payment
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    Both `p1` and `p2` are of type `Payment`, but the actual method invoked depends on their **runtime object type**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Non-virtual Methods**
    
    - **Static methods** (resolved at compile-time)
    - **Final methods** (cannot be overridden)
    - **Private methods** (not visible to subclasses)
    
    Hence, these are **not subject to dynamic dispatch**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    | Phase | Behavior |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Compile Time** | Compiler verifies method existence (based on reference type). It doesn’t decide which implementation to call. |
    | **Runtime** | JVM inspects the actual object type, retrieves the correct method address from the **v-table**, and executes it. |
    | **V-Table (Virtual Table)** | A per-class structure holding references to method implementations. Overridden methods replace superclass entries. |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Internal Steps of Dynamic Dispatch**
    
    1. The compiler generates a call to the superclass method (based on reference).
    2. The JVM at runtime checks the **actual object type**.
    3. The **v-table lookup** identifies the correct overridden method.
    4. The JVM executes that method on the object instance.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Visualization of v-table**
    
    | **Class** | **Method** |
    | --- | --- |
    | `Payment` | → `pay()` (Payment version) |
    | `CreditCardPayment` | → `pay()` (Overrides Payment’s version) |
    | `UpiPayment` | → `pay()` (Overrides Payment’s version) |
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Example: Overriding vs Hiding**
    
    ### **Static Methods → Method Hiding**
    
    ```java
    class A {
        static void show() { System.out.println("Static method in A"); }
    }
    
    class B extends A {
        static void show() { System.out.println("Static method in B"); }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            A obj = new B();
            obj.show(); // Calls A’s show() → method hiding (not overridden)
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Static method in A
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    Static methods are **class-level**, not instance-level.
    
    Hence, they are resolved **at compile-time**, not runtime.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Instance Methods → Overriding**
    
    ```java
    class A {
        void show() { System.out.println("Instance method in A"); }
    }
    
    class B extends A {
        @Override
        void show() { System.out.println("Instance method in B"); }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            A obj = new B();
            obj.show(); // Calls B’s show() → overridden at runtime
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Instance method in B
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    Non-static methods are resolved **dynamically** via virtual invocation — the core of runtime polymorphism.
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Advantages of Runtime Polymorphism**
    
    | **Advantage** | **Description** |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Code Reusability** | Same interface can represent multiple implementations. |
    | **Flexibility and Extensibility** | New classes can be added without changing existing code. |
    | **Scalability** | Allows systems to evolve with minimal modification. |
    | **Readability** | Simplifies code structure using abstraction and overriding. |
    | **Framework and API Design** | Enables frameworks like Spring, Hibernate, etc., to use dependency injection and dynamic binding. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Real-world Analogy**
    
    Think of **a universal charger (superclass)** and various **devices (subclasses)** like phones or tablets.
    
    The charger (reference) can plug into any device (object), but the **charging process (method)** differs for each device — determined **at runtime**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Differences Between Compile-time and Runtime Polymorphism**
    
    | **Aspect** | **Compile-time Polymorphism** | **Runtime Polymorphism** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Also Known As** | Static Binding / Early Binding | Dynamic Binding / Late Binding |
    | **Achieved Through** | Method Overloading | Method Overriding |
    | **Binding Time** | Compile-time | Runtime |
    | **Class Involvement** | Same class | Superclass–Subclass |
    | **Decision Basis** | Based on method signature | Based on object type |
    | **Performance** | Faster (no runtime lookup) | Slightly slower (v-table lookup) |
    | **Example** | `add(int, int)` vs `add(double, double)` | `draw()` overridden in subclasses |
    | **Virtual Table** | Not used | Used by JVM for method resolution |
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Important Points**
    
    - **Runtime polymorphism** depends on **method overriding** and **dynamic dispatch**.
    - Only **non-static, non-final, non-private** methods are subject to **virtual invocation**.
    - The **reference type** decides what methods can be called;
        
        the **object type** decides which implementation executes.
        
    - It enables **dynamic behavior**, making code **flexible** and **extensible**.
    - **JVM’s v-table** ensures that the correct overridden method executes at runtime.
    
    ---
    
    ## **11. Summary**
    
    - **Runtime polymorphism** allows method calls to be resolved **at runtime** rather than compile time.
    - Achieved through **method overriding** and **dynamic method dispatch**.
    - The **JVM** uses **virtual method invocation** to determine which method implementation to execute.
    - It provides **dynamic flexibility**, **extensibility**, and **object-oriented abstraction** — forming the foundation of Java’s **polymorphic design**.
- **Polymorphism in Practice**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Polymorphism** means *“many forms”* — the ability of a single reference to point to objects of **different types** and to behave differently based on the **actual object** it refers to.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - In Java, **polymorphism in practice** is achieved by **using reference variables of the superclass** to refer to **objects of its subclasses**.
    - The actual method executed depends on the **object type (runtime type)**, not the **reference type**.
    - This allows a single interface or reference to handle **multiple object types**, enabling **dynamic behavior** and **extensibility**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Animal {
        void makeSound() { System.out.println("Animal sound"); }
    }
    
    class Dog extends Animal {
        @Override
        void makeSound() { System.out.println("Dog barks"); }
    }
    
    class Cat extends Animal {
        @Override
        void makeSound() { System.out.println("Cat meows"); }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Animal a1 = new Dog();  // Upcasting
            Animal a2 = new Cat();  // Upcasting
    
            a1.makeSound();  // Dog barks
            a2.makeSound();  // Cat meows
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Dog barks
    Cat meows
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    Both `a1` and `a2` are of type `Animal`, but they behave differently based on their actual object type — demonstrating **runtime polymorphism** in practice.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Reference Variables and Object Types**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    In Java, **reference variables** determine **which methods or fields can be accessed**, while the **object type** determines **which implementation is executed at runtime**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    | Aspect | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Reference Type** | The declared type of the variable (decides accessible members). |
    | **Object Type** | The type of the actual object created (decides method execution at runtime). |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Parent {
        void show() { System.out.println("Parent show()"); }
    }
    
    class Child extends Parent {
        @Override
        void show() { System.out.println("Child show()"); }
        void onlyInChild() { System.out.println("Exclusive Child method"); }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Parent p = new Child();   // Reference type: Parent, Object type: Child
            p.show();                 // Calls Child's show() at runtime
            // p.onlyInChild();       // ❌ Compile-time error (not in Parent)
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Child show()
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - The **reference type (`Parent`)** decides what methods you *can call*.
    - The **object type (`Child`)** decides *which implementation* runs at runtime.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Key Takeaway**
    
    | Type | Determines |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Reference Type** | What methods *can be called* |
    | **Object Type** | What methods *will be executed* |
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Upcasting**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Upcasting** is the process of assigning a **subclass object to a superclass reference variable**.
    
    It is **always safe** and does not require explicit casting because every subclass **is-a** superclass (IS-A relationship).
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    SuperClass ref = new SubClass(); // Implicit upcasting
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Vehicle {
        void start() { System.out.println("Vehicle starting..."); }
    }
    
    class Car extends Vehicle {
        @Override
        void start() { System.out.println("Car starting..."); }
        void openRoof() { System.out.println("Opening car roof"); }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Vehicle v = new Car(); // Upcasting
            v.start();             // Calls Car's start()
            // v.openRoof();       // ❌ Not accessible through Vehicle reference
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Car starting...
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - The reference `v` of type `Vehicle` can only access methods defined in `Vehicle`.
    - However, at runtime, the `Car` version of `start()` executes — demonstrating **dynamic method dispatch**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Advantages of Upcasting**
    
    - Enables **polymorphic behavior**.
    - Simplifies **code generalization** (e.g., using parent references for lists of subclass objects).
    - Used extensively in **collections**, **interfaces**, and **frameworks**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Polymorphic Collection**
    
    ```java
    List<Animal> animals = new ArrayList<>();
    animals.add(new Dog());
    animals.add(new Cat());
    animals.add(new Animal());
    
    for (Animal a : animals) {
        a.makeSound(); // Each object responds differently
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    Dog barks
    Cat meows
    Animal sound
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    Upcasting allows storage of **heterogeneous subclass objects** in a single collection.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Downcasting**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Downcasting** is the reverse of upcasting — converting a **superclass reference** back into a **subclass reference**.
    
    It requires **explicit casting** and should be performed **only after verifying** the actual object type using the `instanceof` operator.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    SubClass sub = (SubClass) superRef; // Explicit downcasting
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Animal {
        void sound() { System.out.println("Generic sound"); }
    }
    
    class Dog extends Animal {
        void sound() { System.out.println("Dog barks"); }
        void fetch() { System.out.println("Dog fetches ball"); }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Animal a = new Dog();     // Upcasting
            a.sound();
    
            Dog d = (Dog) a;          // Downcasting
            d.fetch();                // Now subclass-specific method accessible
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Dog barks
    Dog fetches ball
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - The object is actually of type `Dog`, so downcasting is valid.
    - After downcasting, subclass-specific methods (`fetch()`) become accessible.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Invalid Downcasting (ClassCastException)**
    
    ```java
    Animal a = new Animal();
    Dog d = (Dog) a;  // ❌ Invalid: actual object is not Dog
    d.fetch();
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Exception in thread "main" java.lang.ClassCastException
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    Downcasting an object that is not actually an instance of the subclass causes a **runtime exception**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. The `instanceof` Operator**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The `instanceof` operator tests whether an **object reference** is an **instance of a specific class** (or subclass).
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    objectReference instanceof ClassName
    
    ```
    
    - Returns `true` if the object is an instance of that class (or subclass).
    - Returns `false` otherwise.
    - Prevents invalid downcasting by verifying the object type before casting.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Animal { }
    class Dog extends Animal { }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Animal a = new Dog();
    
            if (a instanceof Dog) {
                Dog d = (Dog) a;  // Safe downcast
                System.out.println("Downcasting successful!");
            } else {
                System.out.println("Not a Dog instance");
            }
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Downcasting successful!
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Using instanceof for Safety**
    
    ```java
    class Animal { }
    class Dog extends Animal { }
    class Cat extends Animal { }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Animal a = new Cat();
    
            if (a instanceof Dog) {
                Dog d = (Dog) a;  // Checked safely
                System.out.println("Dog instance");
            } else {
                System.out.println("Not a Dog instance, downcast skipped");
            }
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Not a Dog instance, downcast skipped
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    `instanceof` ensures that the program performs **safe downcasting**, avoiding runtime exceptions.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Note:**
    
    With **Java 16+**, pattern matching simplifies `instanceof` usage:
    
    ```java
    if (a instanceof Dog d) {
        d.fetch(); // Safe and concise
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    | Phase | Behavior |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Compilation** | The compiler checks whether the method exists in the **reference type**. |
    | **Runtime** | The JVM resolves method calls based on the **object’s actual type** (for overridden methods). |
    | **Upcasting** | Implicitly allowed; no type check needed. |
    | **Downcasting** | Verified at runtime; throws `ClassCastException` if invalid. |
    | **instanceof** | Evaluated at runtime; used to ensure type safety before downcasting. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    Imagine a **remote control (reference)** that can operate various devices (**objects**):
    
    - When the remote is connected to a TV, it acts like a TV controller.
    - When connected to an AC, it acts like an AC controller.
    - You can always “downcast” the generic remote to a specific type (TVRemote or ACRemote) **only if** it actually controls that device.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Differences Between Upcasting and Downcasting**
    
    | **Aspect** | **Upcasting** | **Downcasting** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Direction** | Subclass → Superclass | Superclass → Subclass |
    | **Type Conversion** | Implicit (safe) | Explicit (unsafe) |
    | **Access Level** | Only superclass members accessible | Subclass members accessible |
    | **Purpose** | Achieve polymorphism | Access subclass-specific features |
    | **Risk** | No risk | May throw `ClassCastException` |
    | **Example** | `Animal a = new Dog();` | `Dog d = (Dog) a;` |
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - **Upcasting** is always safe and implicit.
    - **Downcasting** must be done carefully and only after checking with `instanceof`.
    - The **reference type** decides accessible members; **object type** decides actual behavior.
    - **Overridden methods** are always executed from the actual object type (runtime decision).
    - The `instanceof` operator ensures **type safety** during runtime casting.
    - Polymorphism allows writing **generic, extensible, and reusable** code that works with different subclasses interchangeably.
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    - **Polymorphism in practice** uses **reference variables**, **upcasting**, **downcasting**, and **instanceof** to achieve dynamic behavior.
    - **Upcasting** enables a superclass reference to point to subclass objects — forming the basis for **runtime polymorphism**.
    - **Downcasting** allows access to subclass-specific methods but must be done **safely using `instanceof`**.
    - The **reference type** limits method accessibility, while the **object type** decides the actual behavior executed.
    - This flexibility is central to Java’s **object-oriented design**, enabling clean, extensible, and scalable applications.
- **Pattern Matching** (**NEW**)
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Pattern matching** in Java is a feature that simplifies **type checking and casting** operations in code.
    
    It allows you to **match data against a pattern** and, if the match succeeds, **extract components** (like fields or records) directly — without the need for explicit casting or manual field access.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    Traditionally, Java required **explicit type checks and casts** (e.g., using `instanceof` + casting).
    
    Pattern matching makes this process **more concise, readable, and safer**, as it **automatically performs type casts** when a condition is satisfied.
    
    Java introduces **pattern matching** in multiple phases:
    
    1. **Pattern Matching for `instanceof`** — introduced in **Java 14 (preview)**, finalized in **Java 16**.
    2. **Record Patterns** — introduced in **Java 19 (preview)**, enhanced in **Java 21**.
    
    These enhancements align Java with functional-style pattern matching found in languages like Kotlin and Scala — but while maintaining Java’s **strong typing and explicitness**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Pattern Matching for `instanceof` (Java 14+)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Traditional Approach (Before Java 14)**
    
    Before pattern matching, developers had to:
    
    1. Check type with `instanceof`.
    2. Perform **explicit casting** before accessing members.
    
    ```java
    if (obj instanceof String) {
        String s = (String) obj;    // Explicit cast
        System.out.println(s.toUpperCase());
    }
    
    ```
    
    This approach was **verbose and repetitive**, especially in nested conditions.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Modern Approach (Java 14+): Pattern Matching**
    
    **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    if (obj instanceof Type variableName) {
        // variableName is automatically cast to Type
    }
    
    ```
    
    - When the `instanceof` check succeeds, the variable defined in the pattern (`variableName`) becomes **available and safely typed** within the `if` block.
    - No explicit cast is needed — the compiler performs an **implicit smart cast**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Pattern Matching for `instanceof`**
    
    ```java
    public class Main {
        public static void printObject(Object obj) {
            if (obj instanceof String s) {
                System.out.println("String in uppercase: " + s.toUpperCase());
            } else if (obj instanceof Integer i) {
                System.out.println("Square of integer: " + (i * i));
            } else {
                System.out.println("Unknown type: " + obj);
            }
        }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            printObject("Java");
            printObject(10);
            printObject(3.14);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    String in uppercase: JAVA
    Square of integer: 100
    Unknown type: 3.14
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - The variable `s` is automatically treated as a `String` after `instanceof String s`.
    - Similarly, `i` is treated as an `Integer` without needing explicit casting.
    - This improves **type safety**, **conciseness**, and **readability**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Pattern Matching with Logical Conditions**
    
    You can **extend** pattern-matched variables within complex conditions:
    
    ```java
    if (obj instanceof String s && s.length() > 5) {
        System.out.println("Long string: " + s);
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Explanation:**
    
    - The variable `s` is valid within the logical condition (`&&`) only if the first part of the condition succeeds.
    - Java ensures **definite assignment safety**, meaning `s` is accessible only when it is guaranteed to be assigned.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Pattern Matching with `switch` (Java 17+)**
    
    Pattern matching can be used with `switch` expressions (preview feature in Java 17):
    
    ```java
    static String formatter(Object obj) {
        return switch (obj) {
            case String s -> "String: " + s.toLowerCase();
            case Integer i -> "Integer doubled: " + (i * 2);
            case null -> "Null value";
            default -> "Unknown type";
        };
    }
    
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        System.out.println(formatter("JAVA"));
        System.out.println(formatter(10));
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    String: java
    Integer doubled: 20
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    The `switch` expression uses **type patterns** to perform matching and automatically casts values for use in the corresponding branch.
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Record Patterns (Java 19+)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Record patterns** allow **deconstructing record objects** into their **components directly in pattern matching expressions**.
    
    They enable concise extraction of data from **record types**, especially in `if` statements and `switch` expressions.
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Simplify working with **data carrier classes (records)**.
    - Replace boilerplate code for **field extraction and validation**.
    - Combine neatly with **type patterns** for structured data processing.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Traditional Record Handling**
    
    ```java
    record Point(int x, int y) { }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void printPoint(Object obj) {
            if (obj instanceof Point p) {
                int x = p.x();
                int y = p.y();
                System.out.println("Point at (" + x + ", " + y + ")");
            }
        }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            printPoint(new Point(10, 20));
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    Point at (10, 20)
    
    ```
    
    This is functional but **still requires manual unpacking** of the record’s components (`x`, `y`).
    
    ---
    
    ### **Record Pattern Approach (Java 19+)**
    
    ```java
    record Point(int x, int y) { }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void printPoint(Object obj) {
            if (obj instanceof Point(int x, int y)) {
                System.out.println("Point at (" + x + ", " + y + ")");
            }
        }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            printPoint(new Point(10, 20));
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Point at (10, 20)
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - The `Point(int x, int y)` pattern automatically **deconstructs** the record into its components.
    - No explicit `p.x()` or `p.y()` calls needed.
    - Cleaner, safer, and eliminates redundant code.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Nested Record Patterns**
    
    Record patterns can be **nested** to deconstruct records containing other records.
    
    ```java
    record Point(int x, int y) { }
    record Line(Point start, Point end) { }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Object shape = new Line(new Point(1, 2), new Point(3, 4));
    
            if (shape instanceof Line(Point(int x1, int y1), Point(int x2, int y2))) {
                System.out.println("Line from (" + x1 + "," + y1 + ") to (" + x2 + "," + y2 + ")");
            }
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Line from (1,2) to (3,4)
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - Both `Point` records are deconstructed inside the `Line` pattern.
    - This creates **deep destructuring** of nested records, similar to tuple unpacking in functional languages.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Record Patterns in `switch` Expressions (Java 21+)**
    
    ```java
    record Circle(double radius) { }
    record Rectangle(double width, double height) { }
    
    static String describeShape(Object shape) {
        return switch (shape) {
            case Circle(double r) -> "Circle with radius " + r;
            case Rectangle(double w, double h) -> "Rectangle " + w + "x" + h;
            default -> "Unknown shape";
        };
    }
    
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        System.out.println(describeShape(new Circle(5)));
        System.out.println(describeShape(new Rectangle(4, 6)));
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Circle with radius 5.0
    Rectangle 4.0x6.0
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    Each record type is **matched and deconstructed directly** in the `switch`, without the need for getters.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    | **Aspect** | **Pattern Matching Behavior** |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Compile-time Type Safety** | Compiler ensures that extracted pattern variables are **type-safe** and valid only in their matched scope. |
    | **Runtime Execution** | JVM evaluates type at runtime (like `instanceof`), automatically performs safe casts, and binds extracted variables. |
    | **Scope Rules** | Pattern variables exist only within the **true branch** of the matching condition. |
    | **Null Handling** | Patterns automatically reject `null` values unless explicitly handled (e.g., `case null ->`). |
    | **Optimization** | Record and type patterns reduce redundant `getters`, `casts`, and branching logic. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Real-world Analogy**
    
    Think of **pattern matching** like **customs inspection**:
    
    - Each bag (object) has a **label (type)** and **contents (fields)**.
    - The inspector (`instanceof`) checks if the bag fits a pattern.
    - If it matches, the inspector automatically **unpacks the contents** (record fields) — no manual opening (casting) needed.
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Differences Between Classic and Pattern-based instanceof**
    
    | **Aspect** | **Classic `instanceof`** | **Pattern Matching `instanceof`** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Casting** | Manual casting required | Automatic casting by compiler |
    | **Variable Declaration** | Outside `if` block | Inside `if` block (pattern variable) |
    | **Readability** | Verbose | Concise and readable |
    | **Type Safety** | Developer-managed | Compiler-managed |
    | **Introduced In** | Java 1.0 | Java 14 (finalized in Java 16) |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Differences Between Type and Record Patterns**
    
    | **Aspect** | **Type Pattern** | **Record Pattern** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Introduced In** | Java 14+ | Java 19+ |
    | **Matches** | Object type (class/interface) | Record structure |
    | **Purpose** | Simplify casting | Simplify data decomposition |
    | **Example** | `if (obj instanceof String s)` | `if (obj instanceof Point(int x, int y))` |
    | **Use Case** | Type checking | Data extraction |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - Pattern matching **reduces boilerplate** in type-checking and field extraction.
    - Pattern variables are **scope-limited** and **type-safe**.
    - **Record patterns** allow direct **deconstruction of records** into components.
    - Works seamlessly with **switch expressions** and **nested patterns**.
    - Patterns **do not match `null`** unless explicitly handled.
    - Enhances **readability**, **safety**, and **functional-style expressiveness**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    - **Pattern matching** simplifies conditional type logic and object decomposition in Java.
    - **Type patterns (for instanceof)** remove the need for manual casting and enhance clarity.
    - **Record patterns** enable destructuring of record components concisely and safely.
    - Together, these features bring Java closer to modern functional paradigms while maintaining its **object-oriented and strongly-typed** nature.
    - They are key for **cleaner, safer, and more expressive** polymorphic code in modern Java (Java 14–21+).

### 2.6 Abstraction

- **Abstract Classes**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    An **abstract class** in Java is a class that is **declared using the `abstract` keyword** and is **intended to be subclassed** but **not instantiated directly**.
    
    It serves as a **base class** that provides a **partial implementation** for its subclasses, enforcing a **common structure** and **behavioral contract**.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - Abstract classes act as **templates** or **blueprints** for other classes.
    - They may contain:
        - **Abstract methods** (without implementation)
        - **Concrete methods** (with implementation)
        - **Constructors**, **fields**, and **static methods** like normal classes.
    - Any subclass inheriting from an abstract class must **implement all abstract methods**, unless the subclass itself is declared **abstract**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Key Features**
    
    | Feature | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Keyword** | Declared using `abstract` keyword. |
    | **Instantiation** | Cannot be instantiated directly. |
    | **Methods** | Can have both abstract and non-abstract (concrete) methods. |
    | **Constructors** | Can have constructors (used for initialization of subclass objects). |
    | **Inheritance** | Used to define a base class for related subclasses. |
    | **Purpose** | To enforce partial abstraction and code reuse. |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    abstract class Shape {
        abstract void draw(); // abstract method
    
        void display() {      // concrete method
            System.out.println("Displaying the shape");
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. The `abstract` Keyword**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The **`abstract` keyword** is used to:
    
    1. Declare a **class** as abstract — meaning it cannot be instantiated.
    2. Declare a **method** as abstract — meaning it has no implementation and must be overridden by subclasses.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    abstract class ClassName {
        abstract void methodName(); // abstract method
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    abstract class Animal {
        abstract void sound();  // abstract method
    
        void eat() {            // concrete method
            System.out.println("Animals eat food");
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - The `sound()` method provides a **contract** for all subclasses to implement.
    - The `eat()` method provides **common reusable behavior**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Rules for Using `abstract` Keyword**
    
    | Rule | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | A class declared `abstract` cannot be instantiated. | `new AbstractClass()` is not allowed. |
    | An abstract class **may or may not** contain abstract methods. | It can have only concrete methods if needed. |
    | A class containing at least one abstract method **must be declared abstract**. | Else, compile-time error occurs. |
    | Abstract methods **cannot have a body**. | Only method signature is defined. |
    | Constructors, static, and final methods **cannot be abstract**. | Abstract methods must be overridden, which static/final methods cannot be. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Abstract Methods**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    An **abstract method** is a method that is **declared without implementation** (no method body) and is **intended to be implemented** by subclasses.
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    abstract returnType methodName(parameterList);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Abstract Method**
    
    ```java
    abstract class Vehicle {
        abstract void start(); // abstract method
    }
    
    ```
    
    - The method `start()` has **no body** and must be implemented by any non-abstract subclass.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Subclass Implementation**
    
    ```java
    class Car extends Vehicle {
        @Override
        void start() {
            System.out.println("Car starts with ignition key");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Vehicle v = new Car();  // Upcasting
            v.start();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Car starts with ignition key
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    The subclass `Car` provides a **specific implementation** of the abstract method `start()` from its superclass `Vehicle`.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Rules for Abstract Methods**
    
    | Rule | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | Must be declared inside an abstract class or interface. | Cannot exist in a concrete class. |
    | Cannot be **private**, **final**, or **static**. | Because they must be overridden. |
    | Subclass must **override all abstract methods**. | Otherwise, subclass must also be abstract. |
    | Abstract methods define **common behavior** without implementation. | Implementation deferred to subclass. |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Multiple Abstract Methods**
    
    ```java
    abstract class Appliance {
        abstract void turnOn();
        abstract void turnOff();
    }
    
    ```
    
    - Each subclass must provide implementations for both methods.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Concrete Methods in Abstract Classes**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    An abstract class can also contain **concrete methods** — methods that **have implementation**.
    
    These methods allow **code reusability** and **shared functionality** across all subclasses.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    abstract class Bank {
        abstract void calculateInterest();
    
        void openAccount() {
            System.out.println("Account opened successfully");
        }
    }
    
    class HDFCBank extends Bank {
        void calculateInterest() {
            System.out.println("HDFC Bank Interest: 6.5%");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Bank b = new HDFCBank();
            b.openAccount();        // Concrete method from abstract class
            b.calculateInterest();  // Overridden abstract method
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Account opened successfully
    HDFC Bank Interest: 6.5%
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    The abstract class `Bank` defines both an **abstract method** (must be implemented) and a **concrete method** (inherited as-is).
    
    ---
    
    ### **Benefits of Concrete Methods in Abstract Classes**
    
    - Avoids code duplication.
    - Defines **common behavior** across all subclasses.
    - Subclasses can **reuse** or **override** them as needed.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Constructors in Abstract Classes**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Although abstract classes **cannot be instantiated directly**, they can still **have constructors**.
    
    These constructors are **called when a subclass object is created**, helping initialize common fields or perform setup tasks.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Constructor in Abstract Class**
    
    ```java
    abstract class Animal {
        Animal() {
            System.out.println("Animal constructor called");
        }
    
        abstract void sound();
    }
    
    class Dog extends Animal {
        Dog() {
            System.out.println("Dog constructor called");
        }
    
        void sound() {
            System.out.println("Dog barks");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Animal a = new Dog(); // Calls constructor chain
            a.sound();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Animal constructor called
    Dog constructor called
    Dog barks
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - When creating a `Dog` object, both constructors execute:
        1. **Superclass (Animal)** constructor → initializes common state.
        2. **Subclass (Dog)** constructor → initializes subclass-specific state.
    - Abstract class constructors **ensure consistent initialization** in inheritance chains.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Why Constructors Exist in Abstract Classes**
    
    - For **initializing common data** shared across subclasses.
    - To perform **logging, validation, or setup** required for derived classes.
    - Called implicitly through `super()` when a subclass object is created.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Rules**
    
    | Rule | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | Abstract class constructors cannot be called directly. | Only indirectly via subclass constructors. |
    | They are used to initialize **shared fields**. | Enables common setup logic. |
    | If subclass doesn’t explicitly call `super()`, it’s invoked automatically. | Ensures base class construction first. |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Parameterized Constructor in Abstract Class**
    
    ```java
    abstract class Shape {
        int sides;
    
        Shape(int sides) {
            this.sides = sides;
            System.out.println("Shape created with " + sides + " sides");
        }
    
        abstract void draw();
    }
    
    class Triangle extends Shape {
        Triangle() {
            super(3);
        }
    
        void draw() {
            System.out.println("Drawing a triangle");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Shape s = new Triangle();
            s.draw();
        }
    }
    ```
