    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Shape created with 3 sides
    Drawing a triangle
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    The abstract class `Shape`’s constructor is executed **first** through `super(3)` when creating a `Triangle`.
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    | **Phase** | **Behavior** |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Compile-time** | Compiler ensures abstract methods have no body, and subclasses implement them. |
    | **Instantiation** | Abstract class objects cannot be instantiated directly. |
    | **Runtime** | Subclass constructors invoke abstract class constructors using `super()`. |
    | **Method Dispatch** | Calls to abstract methods are resolved dynamically (runtime polymorphism). |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    Imagine an **abstract class `Appliance`** as a **blueprint for electrical devices**:
    
    - It defines **common properties** like `powerOn()` and `powerOff()` (concrete methods).
    - It leaves **specific behavior** (like `performFunction()`) abstract, to be implemented by subclasses like `WashingMachine` or `Refrigerator`.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Differences Between Abstract Class and Interface**
    
    | **Aspect** | **Abstract Class** | **Interface** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Keyword** | `abstract` | `interface` |
    | **Methods** | Can have abstract and concrete methods | Only abstract methods (before Java 8), default and static allowed after Java 8 |
    | **Variables** | Can have instance and static variables | All variables are implicitly `public static final` |
    | **Constructors** | Can have constructors | Cannot have constructors |
    | **Inheritance Type** | Single inheritance (class to class) | Multiple inheritance supported (class can implement multiple interfaces) |
    | **Purpose** | Partial abstraction and code reuse | Full abstraction and contract definition |
    | **Accessibility** | Can have any access modifier | Members are always `public` |
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - The **`abstract` keyword** can apply to both **classes** and **methods**.
    - An abstract class **cannot be instantiated**, but its constructor **is executed** during subclass creation.
    - Abstract methods **must be implemented** by the first concrete subclass.
    - Abstract classes can contain **fields, constructors, static, and final methods**.
    - **Final methods** in an abstract class cannot be overridden.
    - They support both **abstraction** and **inheritance**, forming the backbone for frameworks and template-based designs.
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    - **Abstract classes** provide a **base structure** that defines common behaviors while allowing subclasses to **specialize** certain functionalities.
    - They use the **`abstract` keyword** and can contain both **abstract and concrete methods**.
    - **Constructors** exist to initialize shared data when subclasses are created.
    - Abstract classes are used when classes share **common behavior** but **differ in implementation details**.
    - They combine **inheritance** and **abstraction**, forming a key pillar of Java’s **object-oriented design**.
- **Interfaces**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    An **interface** in Java is a **reference type** (similar to a class) that defines a **contract or blueprint** for classes.
    
    It contains **abstract methods** (methods without implementation) and **constants** that specify **what a class must do**, but **not how it does it**.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - Interfaces enable **abstraction** and **multiple inheritance of type**.
    - A class that implements an interface must **provide implementations** for all its methods.
    - Interfaces are used to define **capabilities** or **behavioral contracts** that multiple classes can share, even if they are unrelated by class hierarchy.
    - The keyword `interface` is used to declare an interface.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example Overview**
    
    ```java
    interface Animal {
        void sound(); // abstract method
        void eat();   // abstract method
    }
    
    ```
    
    Any class implementing `Animal` must define both `sound()` and `eat()`.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose of Interfaces**
    
    - Define **common behaviors** that can be implemented by unrelated classes.
    - Achieve **loose coupling** between components.
    - Provide a way to achieve **multiple inheritance** (since Java doesn’t allow multiple class inheritance).
    - Support **polymorphism** via interface references.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. The `interface` Keyword**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The `interface` keyword is used to declare an **interface type** in Java.
    
    An interface is similar to a class but can contain only:
    
    - **Constant declarations**
    - **Abstract method declarations** (implicitly `public abstract`)
    - (Since Java 8) **default** and **static** methods
    - (Since Java 9) **private** methods (for reuse within interface)
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    interface InterfaceName {
        // constant declarations
        // abstract method declarations
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    interface Vehicle {
        int MAX_SPEED = 120;  // constant declaration
    
        void start();         // abstract method
        void stop();          // abstract method
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - `MAX_SPEED` is a constant (implicitly `public static final`).
    - Methods `start()` and `stop()` are **implicitly abstract** and **public**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Rules for Interface Declaration**
    
    | Rule | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | Declared using the `interface` keyword | Similar to how a class is declared |
    | Cannot be instantiated directly | Must be implemented by a class |
    | Can extend other interfaces | Supports multiple inheritance between interfaces |
    | All interface methods are **public and abstract** by default | Even without modifiers |
    | Variables declared in an interface are **public, static, and final** | Automatically constants |
    | A class uses `implements` keyword to adopt an interface | Must implement all methods |
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Constant Declarations in Interfaces**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    All variables declared in an interface are **implicitly constants**, meaning they are:
    
    - `public`
    - `static`
    - `final`
    
    They must be **initialized at declaration time** because `final` variables cannot remain uninitialized.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    interface DatabaseConfig {
        String DB_NAME = "StudentDB";
        int MAX_CONNECTIONS = 10;
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Equivalent Form (After Compilation)**
    
    ```java
    public static final String DB_NAME = "StudentDB";
    public static final int MAX_CONNECTIONS = 10;
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Using Interface Constants**
    
    ```java
    interface AppConstants {
        String APP_NAME = "LibrarySystem";
        int VERSION = 3;
    }
    
    public class Main implements AppConstants {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            System.out.println("App: " + APP_NAME + ", Version: " + VERSION);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    App: LibrarySystem, Version: 3
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    Constants in interfaces can be accessed directly using the **interface name** or through the implementing class because they are **public static final**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Rules for Interface Constants**
    
    | Rule | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | Automatically `public static final` | No need to specify explicitly |
    | Must be initialized when declared | Cannot remain unassigned |
    | Shared across all implementing classes | No per-object storage |
    | Cannot be overridden | They are static and final |
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Method Declarations (Abstract Methods)**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Methods in an interface are **implicitly public and abstract** (until Java 8).
    
    They represent **behavioral contracts** that implementing classes must fulfill.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    interface InterfaceName {
        returnType methodName(parameters);
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Equivalent Form (After Compilation)**
    
    ```java
    public abstract returnType methodName(parameters);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    interface Machine {
        void start();
        void stop();
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Implementing Class**
    
    ```java
    class Car implements Machine {
        public void start() {
            System.out.println("Car starting...");
        }
        public void stop() {
            System.out.println("Car stopping...");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Machine m = new Car();
            m.start();
            m.stop();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Car starting...
    Car stopping...
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    The class `Car` **implements** all methods defined in the interface `Machine`.
    
    If even one method remains unimplemented, `Car` must be declared `abstract`.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Rules for Interface Methods**
    
    | Rule | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | All methods are implicitly **public** and **abstract** | No need to specify |
    | Must be implemented by the implementing class | Else class becomes abstract |
    | Cannot have a method body (before Java 8) | Default methods added in Java 8 |
    | Cannot be `final`, `static`, or `private` (except in Java 9+ private helpers) | Abstract nature conflicts with these modifiers |
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Interface Implementation (`implements` Keyword)**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A class **implements** an interface using the `implements` keyword.
    
    It provides **concrete implementations** for all abstract methods declared in the interface.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class ClassName implements InterfaceName {
        // Implementation of all interface methods
    }
    
    ```
    
    If a class implements **multiple interfaces**, they are separated by commas:
    
    ```java
    class ClassName implements Interface1, Interface2 {
        // Implement methods from both interfaces
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Single Interface Implementation**
    
    ```java
    interface Drawable {
        void draw();
    }
    
    class Circle implements Drawable {
        public void draw() {
            System.out.println("Drawing a Circle");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Drawable d = new Circle(); // upcasting
            d.draw();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Drawing a Circle
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - The `Circle` class **implements** the `Drawable` interface.
    - The reference type `Drawable` can refer to an instance of any class implementing it — enabling **polymorphism**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Multiple Interface Implementation**
    
    ```java
    interface Printable {
        void print();
    }
    
    interface Showable {
        void show();
    }
    
    class Document implements Printable, Showable {
        public void print() {
            System.out.println("Printing document...");
        }
        public void show() {
            System.out.println("Showing document...");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Document doc = new Document();
            doc.print();
            doc.show();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Printing document...
    Showing document...
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - A single class can implement **multiple interfaces**, combining different functionalities.
    - This is how Java achieves **multiple inheritance of type** safely (without ambiguity).
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Polymorphism with Interfaces**
    
    ```java
    interface Payment {
        void pay();
    }
    
    class CreditCardPayment implements Payment {
        public void pay() {
            System.out.println("Payment made using Credit Card");
        }
    }
    
    class UpiPayment implements Payment {
        public void pay() {
            System.out.println("Payment made using UPI");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Payment p;
    
            p = new CreditCardPayment();
            p.pay();
    
            p = new UpiPayment();
            p.pay();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Payment made using Credit Card
    Payment made using UPI
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - The same interface reference (`Payment p`) refers to different implementations dynamically.
    - This demonstrates **runtime polymorphism** via interfaces.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Rules for Implementing Interfaces**
    
    | Rule | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | The implementing class must **implement all abstract methods** | Else declared `abstract` |
    | Use the `implements` keyword | To connect class and interface |
    | Can implement **multiple interfaces** | Separated by commas |
    | Can extend a class **and** implement interfaces simultaneously | `class X extends Y implements A, B` |
    | Interface methods must be declared `public` in implementation | Otherwise, compilation error |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Class Extending a Class and Implementing Interfaces**
    
    ```java
    class Person {
        void walk() {
            System.out.println("Person walking...");
        }
    }
    
    interface Speaker {
        void speak();
    }
    
    class Teacher extends Person implements Speaker {
        public void speak() {
            System.out.println("Teacher speaking...");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Teacher t = new Teacher();
            t.walk();   // from Person
            t.speak();  // from Speaker
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Person walking...
    Teacher speaking...
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    | **Stage** | **Behavior** |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Compilation** | Compiler ensures all interface methods are implemented. |
    | **Inheritance** | Classes inherit method declarations from interfaces, not implementations. |
    | **Bytecode** | The interface methods generate abstract method entries in the class file. |
    | **Runtime** | JVM invokes actual implementations using **dynamic dispatch** (for polymorphic calls). |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    Imagine an **interface `PaymentGateway`** that defines methods like `authorize()` and `process()`.
    
    Different payment systems — **PayPal**, **Stripe**, **GooglePay** — implement this interface differently.
    
    Each system processes payments in its own way, but all adhere to the **same contract**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Differences Between Abstract Classes and Interfaces**
    
    | **Aspect** | **Abstract Class** | **Interface** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Keyword** | `abstract` | `interface` |
    | **Methods** | Can have abstract + concrete methods | Only abstract (before Java 8), plus default/static (Java 8+) |
    | **Variables** | Instance and static variables allowed | Only `public static final` constants |
    | **Constructors** | Can have constructors | Cannot have constructors |
    | **Inheritance** | Single inheritance only | Multiple inheritance supported |
    | **Access Modifiers** | Can use any modifier | Methods are implicitly `public` |
    | **Purpose** | Code reuse + abstraction | Contract enforcement + multiple type inheritance |
    | **Usage** | When base class shares common logic | When unrelated classes must share behavior |
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - Interfaces provide **full abstraction** (no implementation details).
    - Methods are **implicitly public and abstract**, variables are **public static final**.
    - A class must **implement all interface methods** or be declared **abstract**.
    - Multiple interfaces can be implemented by a single class.
    - Interfaces enable **type-based polymorphism** and **loose coupling**.
    - **Upcasting** is possible: interface reference → implementing class object.
    - They are a cornerstone of Java frameworks (e.g., JDBC, Servlet API, Spring, etc.).
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    - An **interface** defines a **contract** that implementing classes must follow.
    - Declared with the **`interface` keyword**, it can contain **constants** and **abstract methods**.
    - Implementation is done using the **`implements` keyword**.
    - Interfaces enable **multiple inheritance**, **abstraction**, and **runtime polymorphism** in Java.
    - They are essential for designing **flexible**, **maintainable**, and **scalable** object-oriented systems.
- **Interface Evolution**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Overview**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Interface evolution** refers to the progressive enhancement of Java interfaces across different versions of the language — primarily to **improve flexibility**, **backward compatibility**, and **code reuse**.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    Originally, interfaces in Java (prior to **Java 8**) could only contain:
    
    - **Public abstract methods**
    - **Public static final constants**
    
    This design was simple but **rigid**.
    
    Any addition of a new method to an existing interface would **break all implementing classes**, since they would be forced to implement it.
    
    To overcome this limitation, Java introduced new features in subsequent versions:
    
    - **Java 8:** `default` and `static` methods
    - **Java 9:** `private` methods
    - **Java 17+:** `sealed` interfaces (to control inheritance)
    
    These features allow interfaces to **evolve safely** without breaking existing implementations.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Default Methods (Java 8)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **default method** in an interface is a method that provides a **concrete implementation** using the `default` keyword.
    
    It allows new methods to be added to existing interfaces **without forcing subclasses to implement them**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    interface InterfaceName {
        default void methodName() {
            // method body
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Maintain **backward compatibility** when adding new methods to interfaces.
    - Provide **default behavior** that implementing classes can **override** if needed.
    - Facilitate **code reuse** inside interfaces.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Default Method in Interface**
    
    ```java
    interface Vehicle {
        void start(); // abstract method
    
        default void honk() {
            System.out.println("Honking...");
        }
    }
    
    class Car implements Vehicle {
        public void start() {
            System.out.println("Car starting...");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Car c = new Car();
            c.start();
            c.honk(); // default method
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Car starting...
    Honking...
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - The interface `Vehicle` defines a `default` method `honk()`.
    - The class `Car` inherits it without the need to implement it.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Overriding a Default Method**
    
    Implementing classes can **override** a default method if a different behavior is required.
    
    ```java
    class Bike implements Vehicle {
        public void start() {
            System.out.println("Bike starting...");
        }
    
        @Override
        public void honk() {
            System.out.println("Bike horn sound...");
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Resolving Multiple Default Methods (Ambiguity Problem)**
    
    If a class implements **multiple interfaces** that define the same default method, Java requires the class to **override it explicitly** to resolve ambiguity.
    
    ```java
    interface A {
        default void greet() {
            System.out.println("Hello from A");
        }
    }
    
    interface B {
        default void greet() {
            System.out.println("Hello from B");
        }
    }
    
    class C implements A, B {
        @Override
        public void greet() {
            System.out.println("Hello from C (resolved)");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            new C().greet();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Hello from C (resolved)
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    Java enforces **explicit resolution** of method conflicts to preserve clarity and prevent the “Diamond Problem.”
    
    ---
    
    ### **Accessing Super Interface Default Method**
    
    You can use the syntax `InterfaceName.super.methodName()` to call a specific interface’s default method.
    
    ```java
    class C implements A, B {
        @Override
        public void greet() {
            A.super.greet(); // calling A's default method
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Key Points About Default Methods**
    
    | Aspect | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Keyword** | Declared using `default` |
    | **Inheritance** | Automatically inherited by implementing classes |
    | **Overriding** | Can be overridden in subclass |
    | **Static Context** | Cannot be called via interface reference (`InterfaceName.super.method()`) |
    | **Purpose** | Adds backward-compatible behavior in evolving APIs |
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Static Methods in Interfaces (Java 8)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Static methods** in interfaces are methods declared with the `static` keyword that belong **to the interface itself**, not to any instance or implementing class.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    interface InterfaceName {
        static void methodName() {
            // static implementation
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    interface MathUtils {
        static int add(int a, int b) {
            return a + b;
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            int sum = MathUtils.add(5, 10); // called using interface name
            System.out.println("Sum = " + sum);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Sum = 15
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - The method `add()` belongs to the interface, not to any implementing class.
    - It is accessed via the **interface name**, not through an instance.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Rules for Static Methods**
    
    | Rule | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | Declared using `static` keyword | Like in classes |
    | Belongs to interface, not object | Accessed via interface name |
    | Cannot be overridden | Subclasses can hide, not override |
    | Useful for | Utility, helper, and factory methods |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Utility Interface**
    
    ```java
    interface Converter {
        static double inchesToCm(double inches) {
            return inches * 2.54;
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            System.out.println(Converter.inchesToCm(12)); // Output: 30.48
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose of Static Methods**
    
    - Allows defining **utility or helper methods** inside the interface itself.
    - Reduces dependency on external utility classes.
    - Keeps related behavior encapsulated within the interface definition.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Private Methods in Interfaces (Java 9)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Private methods** were introduced in **Java 9** to **avoid code duplication** inside interfaces.
    
    They help **default and static methods** share common logic without exposing it to implementing classes.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    interface InterfaceName {
        private void helperMethod() {
            // reusable internal logic
        }
    
        default void operation() {
            helperMethod(); // called inside default method
        }
    
        static void staticOperation() {
            new InterfaceName() {}.helperMethod(); // or another static private helper
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Private Method Usage**
    
    ```java
    interface Logger {
        private void log(String msg) {
            System.out.println("[LOG]: " + msg);
        }
    
        default void info(String msg) {
            log("INFO: " + msg);
        }
    
        default void error(String msg) {
            log("ERROR: " + msg);
        }
    }
    
    class Application implements Logger {
        // inherits info() and error() with shared logic
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Application app = new Application();
            app.info("Application started");
            app.error("An error occurred");
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    [LOG]: INFO: Application started
    [LOG]: ERROR: An error occurred
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - The private method `log()` is shared internally by the `info()` and `error()` default methods.
    - It **cannot** be accessed from outside the interface or by implementing classes.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Rules for Private Methods**
    
    | Rule | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | Declared with `private` modifier | Like in classes |
    | Access | Only accessible within the interface |
    | Used by | Default or static methods for code reuse |
    | Cannot be abstract, final, or static (unless explicitly declared static) | Restricted for helper use only |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Private Static Method**
    
    ```java
    interface Utils {
        private static void helper() {
            System.out.println("Private static helper method");
        }
    
        static void display() {
            helper(); // called internally
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Utils.display(); // Output: Private static helper method
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Sealed Interfaces (Java 17+)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **sealed interface** restricts which other classes or interfaces can **implement** or **extend** it.
    
    Introduced in **Java 17 (finalized)**, sealed interfaces provide **controlled extensibility** while maintaining **type safety**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    public sealed interface InterfaceName permits Subclass1, Subclass2 {
        // abstract or default methods
    }
    
    ```
    
    - The `permits` clause lists all **allowed subclasses** (implementors).
    - Each permitted subclass must declare one of the following:
        - `final` → cannot be extended further.
        - `sealed` → can be extended, but with its own restricted permits list.
        - `non-sealed` → allows unrestricted extension.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Sealed Interface**
    
    ```java
    sealed interface Shape permits Circle, Rectangle, Triangle { }
    
    final class Circle implements Shape { }
    final class Rectangle implements Shape { }
    non-sealed class Triangle implements Shape { } // open for extension
    
    ```
    
    ### **Explanation:**
    
    - Only `Circle`, `Rectangle`, and `Triangle` can implement `Shape`.
    - `Triangle` is declared `non-sealed`, allowing further subclasses.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Benefits**
    
    - **Controlled inheritance** — prevents unauthorized extensions.
    - **Enhanced maintainability** — developers know all possible implementors at compile time.
    - **Improved pattern matching** — compilers can exhaustively check switch expressions.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Pattern Matching with Sealed Interface**
    
    ```java
    sealed interface Vehicle permits Car, Bike, Truck { }
    
    final class Car implements Vehicle { }
    final class Bike implements Vehicle { }
    final class Truck implements Vehicle { }
    
    public class Main {
        static String identify(Vehicle v) {
            return switch (v) {
                case Car c -> "Car detected";
                case Bike b -> "Bike detected";
                case Truck t -> "Truck detected";
            };
        }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            System.out.println(identify(new Bike()));
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Bike detected
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    The compiler knows all subclasses of `Vehicle`, ensuring **exhaustive pattern checking** in `switch`.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Rules for Sealed Interfaces**
    
    | Rule | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | Declared using `sealed` keyword | Restricts extension |
    | Must include `permits` clause | Lists permitted implementors |
    | Implementors must use `final`, `sealed`, or `non-sealed` | Defines extension behavior |
    | Works with classes and interfaces | Unified sealing model |
    | Supports pattern matching | Ensures completeness and safety |
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    | Feature | Compile-time Behavior | Runtime Behavior |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Default methods** | Checked for conflicts | Resolved using virtual dispatch |
    | **Static methods** | Belong to interface, not instance | Invoked using interface name |
    | **Private methods** | Not visible outside interface | Used internally by JVM for reuse |
    | **Sealed interfaces** | Compiler enforces permitted subclasses | JVM validates sealed hierarchy integrity |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    - **Default methods:** Like adding new rules in a game without breaking old versions.
    - **Static methods:** Like utility tools in the rulebook for players to use anytime.
    - **Private methods:** Internal instructions only visible to the rulebook’s authors.
    - **Sealed interfaces:** Like limiting who can become a certified referee — only a few authorized people.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - **Default methods** introduced backward-compatible evolution.
    - **Static methods** make interfaces self-contained with utility logic.
    - **Private methods** reduce redundancy within interfaces.
    - **Sealed interfaces** add type safety and controlled inheritance.
    - These enhancements made interfaces **richer**, **more maintainable**, and **easier to evolve** without breaking existing codebases.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    | **Feature** | **Introduced In** | **Purpose** | **Example Keyword(s)** |
    | --- | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Default Methods** | Java 8 | Add new behavior without breaking existing code | `default` |
    | **Static Methods** | Java 8 | Provide utility methods inside interfaces | `static` |
    | **Private Methods** | Java 9 | Share internal logic between methods | `private` |
    | **Sealed Interfaces** | Java 17 | Restrict interface inheritance | `sealed`, `permits`, `non-sealed`, `final` |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Interface evolution has transformed interfaces from **purely abstract contracts** into **flexible, partially implementable, and controllable constructs**, allowing modern Java to combine **abstraction**, **encapsulation**, and **type safety** seamlessly.
    
- **Functional Interfaces**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **functional interface** in Java is an interface that contains **exactly one abstract method**.
    
    Such interfaces are also called **SAM interfaces** (Single Abstract Method interfaces).
    
    They are the foundation of **lambda expressions** and **method references** introduced in **Java 8**.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - Functional interfaces provide a **target type** for **lambda expressions**.
    - The single abstract method represents the **behavior** that can be passed as an argument or executed dynamically.
    - Functional interfaces may still contain:
        - **Default methods**
        - **Static methods**
        - **Private methods**
            
            …but only **one abstract method** is allowed.
            
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    @FunctionalInterface
    interface Greeting {
        void sayHello(String name);
    }
    
    ```
    
    Any class, lambda expression, or method reference that implements this interface must provide a body for the `sayHello()` method.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example Usage with Lambda**
    
    ```java
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Greeting g = (name) -> System.out.println("Hello, " + name + "!");
            g.sayHello("Java");
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Hello, Java!
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    The lambda expression `(name) -> System.out.println("Hello, " + name + "!")` provides the implementation of the single abstract method `sayHello()`.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Single Abstract Method (SAM) Interfaces**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **Single Abstract Method (SAM)** interface is an interface that defines **only one abstract method**, which serves as the **functional contract**.
    
    ### **Characteristics**
    
    | Property | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Abstract Method Count** | Exactly one |
    | **Other Members** | Can include `default`, `static`, or `private` methods |
    | **Target Type for Lambdas** | Lambda expressions map to SAM interfaces |
    | **Used In** | Functional programming, stream operations, event handling, callbacks |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: SAM Interface**
    
    ```java
    @FunctionalInterface
    interface Calculator {
        int add(int a, int b);
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Lambda Expression Using SAM**
    
    ```java
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Calculator c = (a, b) -> a + b;
            System.out.println("Sum = " + c.add(10, 20));
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Sum = 30
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: SAM Interface with Default Method**
    
    ```java
    @FunctionalInterface
    interface Printer {
        void print(String msg);
    
        default void log(String msg) {
            System.out.println("Logging: " + msg);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - Still valid because it contains only **one abstract method** (`print()`).
    - The `default` method does not affect the functional nature of the interface.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Invalid Example (Not a Functional Interface)**
    
    ```java
    interface InvalidFunctionalInterface {
        void m1();
        void m2();  // ❌ More than one abstract method
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Compilation Error:**
    
    ```
    Multiple non-overriding abstract methods found in interface
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. The `@FunctionalInterface` Annotation**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The **`@FunctionalInterface`** annotation is used to **explicitly mark** an interface as a **functional interface**.
    
    It helps the compiler enforce the rule that the interface must contain **exactly one abstract method**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    @FunctionalInterface
    interface InterfaceName {
        void singleMethod();
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - Provides **compile-time validation**.
    - Ensures the interface remains functional even when modified in the future.
    - Enhances **code readability and intent** — clearly communicates that the interface is meant for functional programming.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    @FunctionalInterface
    interface Operation {
        int perform(int a, int b);
    }
    
    ```
    
    If you accidentally add another abstract method:
    
    ```java
    @FunctionalInterface
    interface Operation {
        int perform(int a, int b);
        int subtract(int a, int b);  // ❌ Error
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Compilation Error:**
    
    ```
    Unexpected @FunctionalInterface annotation
    Operation is not a functional interface
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Notes**
    
    | Rule | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | Optional Annotation | `@FunctionalInterface` is optional but recommended. |
    | Compiler Check | Enforces single abstract method constraint. |
    | Backward Compatibility | Even pre-Java 8 interfaces (like `Runnable`) qualify as functional interfaces. |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Existing Functional Interfaces Before Java 8**
    
    | Interface | Abstract Method | Description |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | `Runnable` | `void run()` | Used for threads and concurrent execution. |
    | `Comparable<T>` | `int compareTo(T o)` | Used for ordering and sorting. |
    | `ActionListener` (AWT) | `void actionPerformed(ActionEvent e)` | Used for GUI event handling. |
    
    All these are **functional interfaces** even though they existed before Java 8.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Built-in Functional Interfaces (Java 8 java.util.function Package)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Overview**
    
    Java 8 introduced the **`java.util.function`** package containing **predefined functional interfaces** to simplify functional programming and lambda usage.
    
    These interfaces are generic and widely used in **Streams API**, **Collections**, and **functional-style operations**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common Built-in Functional Interfaces**
    
    | **Interface** | **Abstract Method** | **Description** | **Example** |
    | --- | --- | --- | --- |
    | **`Predicate<T>`** | `boolean test(T t)` | Tests a condition and returns boolean | Filter logic in streams |
    | **`Function<T, R>`** | `R apply(T t)` | Takes input and returns a result | Transformation functions |
    | **`Consumer<T>`** | `void accept(T t)` | Performs an action without returning a result | Logging, printing |
    | **`Supplier<T>`** | `T get()` | Supplies a value (no input) | Factory or data supplier |
    | **`UnaryOperator<T>`** | `T apply(T t)` | Same type input and output | Increment, modify operations |
    | **`BinaryOperator<T>`** | `T apply(T t1, T t2)` | Combines two values of same type | Summation, concatenation |
    | **`BiFunction<T, U, R>`** | `R apply(T t, U u)` | Takes two arguments, returns result | Combine two different types |
    | **`BiConsumer<T, U>`** | `void accept(T t, U u)` | Consumes two inputs | Printing key-value pairs |
    | **`BiPredicate<T, U>`** | `boolean test(T t, U u)` | Tests condition with two inputs | Comparisons, validations |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example 1: Predicate**
    
    ```java
    import java.util.function.Predicate;
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Predicate<Integer> isEven = n -> n % 2 == 0;
            System.out.println(isEven.test(10)); // true
            System.out.println(isEven.test(7));  // false
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example 2: Function**
    
    ```java
    import java.util.function.Function;
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Function<String, Integer> strLength = s -> s.length();
            System.out.println(strLength.apply("Java")); // 4
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example 3: Consumer**
    
    ```java
    import java.util.function.Consumer;
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Consumer<String> printer = msg -> System.out.println("Message: " + msg);
            printer.accept("Functional Interfaces are powerful!");
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example 4: Supplier**
    
    ```java
    import java.util.function.Supplier;
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Supplier<Double> randomValue = () -> Math.random();
            System.out.println("Random number: " + randomValue.get());
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example 5: BinaryOperator**
    
    ```java
    import java.util.function.BinaryOperator;
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            BinaryOperator<Integer> add = (a, b) -> a + b;
            System.out.println(add.apply(10, 20)); // 30
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Custom Functional Interface Example**
    
    ```java
    @FunctionalInterface
    interface StringOperation {
        String operate(String s);
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            StringOperation upperCase = str -> str.toUpperCase();
            System.out.println(upperCase.operate("java functional interfaces"));
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    JAVA FUNCTIONAL INTERFACES
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    The lambda `(str -> str.toUpperCase())` directly implements the single abstract method `operate()`.
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    | **Phase** | **Behavior** |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Compilation** | Compiler ensures only one abstract method exists when `@FunctionalInterface` is present. |
    | **Bytecode** | Lambda expressions are translated into synthetic methods using `invokedynamic` instruction. |
    | **Runtime** | The JVM dynamically creates instances of the functional interface at execution time. |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Behind the Scenes (Lambda Translation)**
    
    - Java uses **`invokedynamic`** to dynamically link lambdas.
    - The JVM generates **lightweight function objects** at runtime.
    - No anonymous class is created in bytecode (unlike pre-Java 8 approach).
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    A **functional interface** is like a **single-button remote**:
    
    - You press the button, and an action happens (the behavior).
    - The button can be wired to **different devices** (lambdas or methods), each performing its own operation — but the **trigger remains the same**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Differences Between Functional Interface and Normal Interface**
    
    | **Aspect** | **Functional Interface** | **Normal Interface** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Abstract Methods** | Exactly one | One or more |
    | **Annotation** | Marked with `@FunctionalInterface` (optional) | Not required |
    | **Purpose** | Functional programming (lambda target) | Structural design |
    | **Default/Static Methods** | Allowed | Allowed |
    | **Usage** | Used with lambdas, streams, callbacks | General interface contracts |
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - Must contain **only one abstract method**.
    - May include **default**, **static**, or **private** methods.
    - Marking with `@FunctionalInterface` ensures **compile-time validation**.
    - Lambdas and method references can implement functional interfaces.
    - Built-in functional interfaces reside in `java.util.function`.
    - Enable **functional programming**, **stream operations**, and **event handling** in modern Java.
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    - A **functional interface** is an interface with **exactly one abstract method (SAM)**.
    - The **`@FunctionalInterface` annotation** enforces and documents its intent.
    - They are the foundation for **lambda expressions** and **method references**.
    - **Built-in functional interfaces** (like `Predicate`, `Function`, `Consumer`, `Supplier`, etc.) simplify common programming patterns.
    - Functional interfaces enable **cleaner**, **concise**, and **expressive** functional-style programming in Java.

### 2.7 Advanced OOP Concepts

- **Composition vs Inheritance**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Composition** and **Inheritance** are two fundamental techniques for achieving **code reuse** and **object relationships** in Object-Oriented Programming (OOP).
    
    - **Inheritance (IS-A relationship)**:
        
        A mechanism where one class **inherits properties and behaviors** of another class.
        
        Example: `Dog` *is a* `Animal`.
        
    - **Composition (HAS-A relationship)**:
        
        A mechanism where one class **contains an instance** of another class as a field, representing **ownership** or **usage**.
        
        Example: `Car` *has a* `Engine`.
        
    
    ---
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - **Inheritance** represents a **hierarchical** relationship between classes (extends).
    - **Composition** represents a **part-whole** or **containment** relationship (uses or owns another object).
    - Both allow reuse of existing code, but **composition provides greater flexibility** and **looser coupling**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Simple Illustration**
    
    | Relationship Type | Example | Description |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **IS-A** (Inheritance) | `Dog extends Animal` | Dog *is a kind of* Animal |
    | **HAS-A** (Composition) | `Car has Engine` | Car *has an* Engine as a component |
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. HAS-A Relationship (Composition)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **HAS-A relationship** defines **composition** — when a class **contains** another class as a member variable.
    
    It implies **ownership**, meaning the containing class is **composed of** other objects.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class ClassA {
        // ClassB object as a field → Composition
        private ClassB objB = new ClassB();
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Composition (HAS-A Relationship)**
    
    ```java
    class Engine {
        void start() {
            System.out.println("Engine starts...");
        }
    }
    
    class Car {
        private Engine engine = new Engine();  // Car HAS-A Engine
    
        void drive() {
            engine.start();
            System.out.println("Car is moving...");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Car car = new Car();
            car.drive();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Engine starts...
    Car is moving...
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - `Car` contains an instance of `Engine`.
    - This represents a **HAS-A** relationship (Composition).
    - `Car` delegates the task of starting to its internal `Engine` object.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Types of Composition**
    
    | Type | Description | Example |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Composition** | Strong ownership; part cannot exist without whole. | `Car` and `Engine` |
    | **Aggregation** | Weak ownership; part can exist independently. | `Department` and `Teacher` |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Aggregation**
    
    ```java
    class Department {
        String name;
        Department(String name) {
            this.name = name;
        }
    }
    
    class Teacher {
        String name;
        Department dept; // Aggregation
    
        Teacher(String name, Department dept) {
            this.name = name;
            this.dept = dept;
        }
    
        void display() {
            System.out.println(name + " teaches in " + dept.name + " department");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Department d = new Department("Computer Science");
            Teacher t = new Teacher("John", d);
            t.display();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    John teaches in Computer Science department
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    The `Department` can exist even without the `Teacher`, hence this is **aggregation** — a **weaker form of composition**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Key Characteristics of Composition**
    
    - The **composed object** (contained object) is typically **created and destroyed** along with the owner.
    - Provides **better encapsulation** (internal implementation hidden).
    - Promotes **code reuse without inheritance**.
    - Avoids tight coupling of class hierarchies.
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. IS-A Relationship (Inheritance)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    An **IS-A relationship** represents **inheritance**, where one class **extends another** and inherits its fields and methods.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class Parent { }
    class Child extends Parent { }  // Child IS-A Parent
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Inheritance (IS-A Relationship)**
    
    ```java
    class Animal {
        void eat() {
            System.out.println("Animal eats food");
        }
    }
    
    class Dog extends Animal {
        void bark() {
            System.out.println("Dog barks");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Dog d = new Dog();
            d.eat();  // Inherited from Animal
            d.bark(); // Defined in Dog
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Animal eats food
    Dog barks
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - `Dog` inherits behavior from `Animal`.
    - Represents an **IS-A** relationship — `Dog is an Animal`.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Key Characteristics of Inheritance**
    
    - Promotes **code reuse through hierarchy**.
    - Enables **runtime polymorphism** (via method overriding).
    - Introduces **tight coupling** between parent and child classes.
    - Changes in base class may **impact all subclasses**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Composition vs Inheritance**
    
    | **Aspect** | **Composition (HAS-A)** | **Inheritance (IS-A)** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Definition** | One class contains another class | One class extends another |
    | **Keyword** | Uses object reference | Uses `extends` keyword |
    | **Relationship Type** | HAS-A relationship | IS-A relationship |
    | **Coupling** | Loose coupling | Tight coupling |
    | **Reusability** | Achieved by using existing classes as fields | Achieved by extending base classes |
    | **Flexibility** | High – can change behavior at runtime by replacing components | Low – fixed at compile time |
    | **Change Impact** | Changes in contained class have limited impact | Changes in base class affect all subclasses |
    | **Encapsulation** | Preserved (composed object is private) | Can be broken (subclass can access base class members) |
    | **Multiple Reuse** | Can use multiple classes together | Java does not support multiple inheritance (classes) |
    | **Example** | `Car has Engine` | `Dog is Animal` |
    | **Design Type** | “Uses-a” relationship | “Is-a” relationship |
    | **Favorability** | Preferred for flexible design | Preferred for hierarchy modeling |
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. When to Use Composition Over Inheritance**
    
    ---
    
    ### **1. Prefer Composition When:**
    
    - You want to **reuse behavior without inheriting implementation**.
    - You need **loose coupling** between classes.
    - The relationship is **not hierarchical** or **doesn’t logically fit** “is-a”.
    - You want to **change behavior at runtime** (delegation possible).
    - You need to **combine multiple behaviors** flexibly.
    - You want to **avoid the diamond problem** and class fragility.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Composition for Flexibility**
    
    ```java
    interface PaymentProcessor {
        void pay(double amount);
    }
    
    class CreditCardPayment implements PaymentProcessor {
        public void pay(double amount) {
            System.out.println("Paid $" + amount + " using Credit Card");
        }
    }
    
    class UpiPayment implements PaymentProcessor {
        public void pay(double amount) {
            System.out.println("Paid $" + amount + " using UPI");
        }
    }
    
    class Checkout {
        private PaymentProcessor processor; // Composition
    
        Checkout(PaymentProcessor processor) {
            this.processor = processor;
        }
    
        void processOrder(double amount) {
            processor.pay(amount); // Delegation
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Checkout c1 = new Checkout(new CreditCardPayment());
            c1.processOrder(250.0);
    
            Checkout c2 = new Checkout(new UpiPayment()); // swap behavior dynamically
            c2.processOrder(500.0);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Paid $250.0 using Credit Card
    Paid $500.0 using UPI
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - The `Checkout` class doesn’t depend on a specific payment type.
    - Using **composition**, the behavior can be **changed dynamically** at runtime — unlike inheritance.
    
    ---
    
    ### **2. Use Inheritance When:**
    
    - The subclass **is a specialized type** of the parent class.
    - The relationship clearly expresses an **IS-A hierarchy**.
    - You need **polymorphism** or **method overriding**.
    - Base class is **stable** and unlikely to change frequently.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: When Inheritance Makes Sense**
    
    ```java
    abstract class Shape {
        abstract double area();
    }
    
    class Circle extends Shape {
        double radius;
        Circle(double radius) { this.radius = radius; }
        double area() { return Math.PI * radius * radius; }
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - A `Circle` **is a type of** `Shape`.
    - This is a valid **IS-A** relationship suited for inheritance.
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Advantages of Composition Over Inheritance**
    
    | **Advantage** | **Description** |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Encapsulation** | Internal implementation can be hidden. |
    | **Flexibility** | Behavior can be changed at runtime by swapping components. |
    | **Reusability** | Can reuse code without forming rigid hierarchies. |
    | **Maintenance** | Easier to modify without breaking other classes. |
    | **Avoids Fragile Base Class Problem** | Subclasses are not dependent on base class implementation details. |
    | **Supports Dynamic Behavior** | Enables delegation and strategy patterns. |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Design Principle**
    
    > Favor composition over inheritance — Effective Java, Item 18 (Joshua Bloch)
    > 
    
    This principle suggests:
    
    - Use inheritance only when there is a **clear IS-A** relationship.
    - Otherwise, prefer **composition** for **flexible, maintainable, and modular design**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Concept | Real-world Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Inheritance (IS-A)** | A **Car** is a **Vehicle** — inherits vehicle properties. |
    | **Composition (HAS-A)** | A **Car** has an **Engine**, **Seats**, and **Wheels** — composed of parts. |
    
    In real-world systems, **composition dominates**, since most objects **contain** other objects rather than **extend** them.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    | **Aspect** | **Inheritance** | **Composition** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Memory Layout** | Subclass contains fields of superclass | Containing object has reference to another object |
    | **Method Lookup** | Uses v-table for virtual method dispatch | Delegates method calls explicitly |
    | **Instantiation** | Subclass constructors call superclass constructors | Contained objects created separately |
    | **Destruction** | Subclass destroyed along with superclass | Contained objects destroyed with container (if owned) |
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - **Composition = HAS-A** (contains or uses another class).
    - **Inheritance = IS-A** (extends another class).
    - Composition promotes **loose coupling** and **flexibility**.
    - Inheritance is **tightly coupled** and should be used cautiously.
    - Composition is used heavily in **design patterns** (e.g., Strategy, Decorator, Adapter).
    - Prefer composition unless there’s a **strong IS-A relationship**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    | **Feature** | **Composition (HAS-A)** | **Inheritance (IS-A)** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Keyword Used** | Object Reference | `extends` |
    | **Relationship** | HAS-A | IS-A |
    | **Coupling** | Loose | Tight |
    | **Flexibility** | High | Low |
    | **Reusability** | Through delegation | Through inheritance |
    | **Polymorphism** | Supported via interfaces | Supported via overriding |
    | **Design Preference** | Favor composition over inheritance | Use when IS-A fits naturally |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    - Use **inheritance** when modeling a clear **hierarchical relationship**.
    - Use **composition** to achieve **modular, extensible, and maintainable** designs.
    - Following the “**Favor composition over inheritance**” principle leads to **cleaner and more robust object-oriented systems**.
- **Final Keyword**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The **`final` keyword** in Java is a **non-access modifier** used to restrict **modification or inheritance** of variables, methods, or classes.
    
    It can be applied to:
    
    - **Variables** → to make them **constants** (value cannot change)
    - **Methods** → to prevent **overriding**
    - **Classes** → to prevent **inheritance**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    The word *“final”* means **unchangeable** or **constant**.
    
    It is used to impose restrictions at different levels in a program:
    
    - **final variable** → can’t be reassigned after initialization.
    - **final method** → can’t be overridden in subclass.
    - **final class** → can’t be extended (no subclass can be created).
    
    This provides **safety, immutability, and design control**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    final dataType variableName = value;       // final variable
    final returnType methodName() { ... }      // final method
    final class ClassName { ... }              // final class
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Final Variables (Constants)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **final variable** is a variable whose **value cannot be changed** once assigned.
    
    It represents a **constant** in Java.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    final int MAX_SPEED = 120;
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Final Variable**
    
    ```java
    public class Car {
        final int MAX_SPEED = 200; // constant variable
    
        void display() {
            System.out.println("Maximum speed: " + MAX_SPEED);
            // MAX_SPEED = 250; ❌ Error: cannot assign a value to final variable
        }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Car c = new Car();
            c.display();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Maximum speed: 200
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    Once assigned, the value of a `final` variable cannot be modified. Attempting to reassign it results in a **compile-time error**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Initialization of Final Variables**
    
    Final variables must be initialized **exactly once**, and that can happen in three ways:
    
    ### 1. **At Declaration**
    
    ```java
    final double PI = 3.14159;
    
    ```
    
    ### 2. **In a Constructor**
    
    ```java
    class Student {
        final int rollNo;
    
        Student(int rollNo) {
            this.rollNo = rollNo; // initialized inside constructor
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### 3. **In an Instance Initialization Block**
    
    ```java
    class Config {
        final int TIMEOUT;
        {
            TIMEOUT = 5000; // initialized in block
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Blank Final Variable**
    
    A **blank final variable** is a final variable that is **declared but not initialized** at the point of declaration.
    
    It **must** be initialized in:
    
    - A **constructor**, or
    - An **instance initialization block**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Static Final Variables**
    
    A `static final` variable acts as a **class-level constant** shared across all instances.
    
    ```java
    class MathUtil {
        static final double PI = 3.14159;
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Access:**
    
    `MathUtil.PI`
    
    ---
    
    ### **Rules for Final Variables**
    
    | Rule | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | Must be initialized once | Cannot remain unassigned |
    | Cannot be reassigned | Attempt causes compile-time error |
    | Can be static or instance level | Both allowed |
    | Can hold object references | But object’s fields can still change |
    | Compile-time constants must be `static final` | Used in expressions or switch statements |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Final Reference Variable**
    
    ```java
    class Student {
        int age = 20;
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            final Student s = new Student();
            s.age = 25;        // ✅ allowed (modifying object content)
            // s = new Student(); ❌ not allowed (reassigning reference)
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    The **reference** `s` cannot change, but the **object’s internal state** can be modified.
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Final Methods (Cannot Be Overridden)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **final method** is a method that **cannot be overridden** by subclasses.
    
    This prevents altering the base class behavior, ensuring **method consistency** across all derived classes.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class ClassName {
        final void methodName() {
            // method body
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Final Method**
    
    ```java
    class Vehicle {
        final void start() {
            System.out.println("Vehicle starting...");
        }
    }
    
    class Car extends Vehicle {
        // ❌ Cannot override final method
        // void start() { System.out.println("Car starting..."); }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Car c = new Car();
            c.start();  // Calls Vehicle's version
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Vehicle starting...
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    The method `start()` in `Vehicle` is `final`, hence `Car` cannot override it.
    
    This ensures the start behavior remains **consistent** across all vehicles.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Reasons for Using Final Methods**
    
    - To **prevent overriding** important or security-critical behavior.
    - To **enforce consistent functionality** across all subclasses.
    - To **improve performance** (compiler can optimize calls).
    
    ---
    
    ### **Rules for Final Methods**
    
    | Rule | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | Declared with `final` keyword | Prevents overriding |
    | Inherited by subclasses | But cannot be changed |
    | Can be overloaded | Overloading is allowed |
    | Cannot be abstract | Contradicts purpose of being final |
    | Can be private | Private methods are implicitly final (cannot be overridden) |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Final Method Overloading**
    
    ```java
    class MathUtil {
        final void display(int x) {
            System.out.println("Value: " + x);
        }
    
        void display(double x) {  // ✅ overloading allowed
            System.out.println("Double value: " + x);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Final Classes (Cannot Be Inherited)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **final class** is a class that **cannot be extended or subclassed**.
    
    It prevents inheritance and is often used to:
    
    - **Secure implementation logic**
    - **Ensure immutability**
    - **Prevent misuse through subclassing**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    final class ClassName {
        // class body
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Final Class**
    
    ```java
    final class Account {
        void displayBalance() {
            System.out.println("Balance: $1000");
        }
    }
    
    // ❌ Error: cannot inherit from final class
    // class SavingsAccount extends Account { }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Account acc = new Account();
            acc.displayBalance();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Balance: $1000
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    `Account` is a **final class**, so no other class can extend it.
    
    It ensures that its implementation remains **unchanged**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Real-world Example**
    
    In the Java API, several important classes are declared `final`, for example:
    
    - `java.lang.String`
    - `java.lang.Math`
    - `java.lang.Integer`
    - `java.lang.System`
    
    These classes are final to ensure:
    
    - **Security** (can’t be modified through inheritance)
    - **Performance** (compiler optimizations)
    - **Immutability**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Rules for Final Classes**
    
    | Rule | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | Declared using `final` keyword | Prevents inheritance |
    | Cannot be subclassed | Compilation error on attempt |
    | Can contain final or non-final methods | All members allowed |
    | Can be instantiated | Like normal classes |
    | Common use cases | Immutable or secure utility classes |
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    | **Context** | **Behavior** |
    | --- | --- |
    | **final variable** | Compiler ensures assignment happens only once. Constant values may be inlined at compile time. |
    | **final method** | Added to class’s method table but excluded from override entries. |
    | **final class** | JVM disallows subclass bytecode from extending final classes. |
    | **Optimization** | Compiler and JIT may optimize final members for faster performance since they’re predictable. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Concept | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **final variable** | Like a **constant**—once written, it cannot be changed (e.g., your birth date). |
    | **final method** | Like a **sealed instruction**—cannot be redefined (e.g., engine start mechanism fixed by manufacturer). |
    | **final class** | Like a **sealed device**—cannot be opened or modified (e.g., a sealed smartphone). |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Differences Between Final, Finally, and Finalize**
    
    | **Keyword/Method** | **Category** | **Purpose** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **final** | Modifier | Prevents modification of variables, methods, or classes |
    | **finally** | Block | Ensures execution of code (usually cleanup) in exception handling |
    | **finalize()** | Method (deprecated) | Called by Garbage Collector before object destruction |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - **final variables** → constants; must be initialized once.
    - **final methods** → cannot be overridden but can be overloaded.
    - **final classes** → cannot be inherited or extended.
    - A **final class’s methods** are implicitly final (since no subclass can override them).
    - Declaring methods or classes as final can improve **security**, **performance**, and **reliability**.
    - Overuse of `final` can reduce flexibility — use it only when immutability or restriction is truly needed.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    | **Final Applied To** | **Meaning** | **Effect** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Variable** | Constant | Value cannot change after initialization |
    | **Method** | Fixed behavior | Cannot be overridden by subclass |
    | **Class** | Sealed type | Cannot be inherited |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    The `final` keyword in Java is used to **create constants**, **lock down behavior**, and **restrict inheritance**.
    
    It plays a key role in ensuring **immutability, design safety, and program consistency** — fundamental principles for building robust and secure Java applications.
    
- **Static Members**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The **`static` keyword** in Java is a **non-access modifier** that makes a member (variable, method, block, or nested class) belong to the **class itself**, rather than to any specific instance of the class.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - Normally, each object has its own copy of instance variables and methods.
    - But **static members** are **shared by all instances** of the class.
    - They are **loaded once** when the class is first loaded into memory by the **JVM’s ClassLoader**.
    - Hence, static members are ideal for **common data** and **utility behaviors** that are **independent of individual objects**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class ClassName {
        static dataType variableName;
        static returnType methodName() { ... }
        static { ... } // static block
        static class NestedClass { ... } // static nested class
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example Overview**
    
    ```java
    class Demo {
        static int count = 0;       // static variable
        static void display() { }   // static method
        static { }                  // static block
        static class Helper { }     // static nested class
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Static Variables (Class Variables)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **static variable** (also called a **class variable**) is a variable that belongs to the **class**, not to any specific object.
    
    There is **only one copy** of a static variable shared among all instances.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class ClassName {
        static dataType variableName = value;
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Static Variable**
    
    ```java
    class Counter {
        static int count = 0;  // static variable
    
        Counter() {
            count++;
            System.out.println("Object created. Count = " + count);
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            new Counter();
            new Counter();
            new Counter();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Object created. Count = 1
    Object created. Count = 2
    Object created. Count = 3
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - `count` belongs to the **class**, not to each object.
    - Each new `Counter` object increments the **same shared** static variable.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Accessing Static Variables**
    
    Static variables can be accessed in two ways:
    
    ```java
    ClassName.variableName; // Preferred way
    objectName.variableName; // Legal, but not recommended
    
    ```
    
    Example:
    
    ```java
    System.out.println(Counter.count);
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **When to Use Static Variables**
    
    - When a variable’s value is **common for all objects**.
    - For **counters, configuration constants, or caching shared data**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Constant Using Static Final**
    
    ```java
    class MathUtil {
        static final double PI = 3.14159;
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Access:**
    
    `MathUtil.PI`
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Static Methods**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **static method** belongs to the **class** rather than any instance of the class.
    
    It can be called **without creating an object**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class ClassName {
        static returnType methodName(parameters) {
            // body
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Static Method**
    
    ```java
    class MathUtil {
        static int add(int a, int b) {
            return a + b;
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            int result = MathUtil.add(10, 20); // no object creation
            System.out.println("Sum: " + result);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Sum: 30
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - The method `add()` is static, so it can be invoked using the class name.
    - No `MathUtil` object is required.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Rules for Static Methods**
    
    | Rule | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | Can access **only static members** | Cannot access instance variables or methods directly |
    | Can be called using **class name** | No object required |
    | Cannot use `this` or `super` | Because they belong to instances |
    | Can be overloaded | But not overridden (cannot have runtime polymorphism) |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Access Rules**
    
    ```java
    class Demo {
        int x = 10;
        static int y = 20;
    
        static void show() {
            // System.out.println(x); ❌ Error - cannot access instance variable
            System.out.println(y);   // ✅ allowed
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Static Method Overloading**
    
    ```java
    class Display {
        static void show() { System.out.println("No parameters"); }
        static void show(String msg) { System.out.println(msg); }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Display.show();
            Display.show("Static method overloading works!");
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    No parameters
    Static method overloading works!
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common Use Cases**
    
    - **Utility methods** (e.g., `Math.max()`, `Arrays.sort()`).
    - **Factory methods** (e.g., `Integer.valueOf()`).
    - **Helper or logging functions**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Static Blocks**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **static block** (or **static initialization block**) is used to **initialize static variables** or **execute code once** when the class is loaded into memory.
    
    It runs **automatically before the main method** or before any object creation.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class ClassName {
        static {
            // initialization code
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Static Block Execution**
    
    ```java
    class Example {
        static int num;
        static String name;
    
        static {
            num = 10;
            name = "Java";
            System.out.println("Static block executed");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            System.out.println("Main method executed");
            System.out.println("Number: " + Example.num);
            System.out.println("Name: " + Example.name);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Static block executed
    Main method executed
    Number: 10
    Name: Java
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - The **static block** executes **once** when the class is loaded into memory.
    - It initializes static members before they are used.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Cases of Static Blocks**
    
    - Initialize **static variables** with complex logic.
    - Perform **one-time setup operations** (e.g., loading configuration files).
    - Execute **logging or database initialization** before main program starts.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Multiple Static Blocks**
    
    You can define multiple static blocks in a class.
    
    They execute **in the order they appear** in the source code.
    
    ```java
    class Test {
        static {
            System.out.println("Block 1");
        }
        static {
            System.out.println("Block 2");
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Output**
    
    ```
    Block 1
    Block 2
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Static Blocks and Class Loading**
    
    - Executed **once per class** when loaded by **JVM ClassLoader**.
    - Not executed again, even if multiple objects are created.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Static Nested Classes**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **static nested class** is a class defined **inside another class** with the `static` keyword.
    
    It can be instantiated **without an object of the outer class**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class Outer {
        static class Nested {
            // body
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Static Nested Class**
    
    ```java
    class Outer {
        static int outerVar = 100;
    
        static class Inner {
            void display() {
                System.out.println("Accessing outer static variable: " + outerVar);
            }
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Outer.Inner obj = new Outer.Inner(); // no Outer object needed
            obj.display();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Accessing outer static variable: 100
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - The `Inner` class is **static**, so it does not require an `Outer` instance.
    - It can directly access **static members** of the outer class.
    
    ---
