    ### **Characteristics of Static Nested Classes**
    
    | Property | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Associated with class, not instance** | No need to create outer class object |
    | **Can access only static members of outer class** | Instance members not accessible directly |
    | **Can have static and non-static members** | Like any top-level class |
    | **Used for logical grouping of classes** | Often used for helper or builder classes |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Practical Use Case**
    
    ```java
    class Database {
        static class Config {
            static final String URL = "jdbc:mysql://localhost:3306/test";
            static final String USER = "root";
            static final String PASSWORD = "admin";
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            System.out.println("Connecting to: " + Database.Config.URL);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Connecting to: jdbc:mysql://localhost:3306/test
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    The static nested class `Config` holds constants logically related to `Database`.
    
    This improves organization and encapsulation.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Difference Between Static Nested and Inner Classes**
    
    | **Aspect** | **Static Nested Class** | **Inner Class (Non-static)** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | Belongs To | Outer class (static context) | Outer class instance |
    | Object Requirement | No outer object needed | Requires outer object |
    | Can Access | Only static members of outer class | Both static and instance members |
    | Example Access | `Outer.Inner obj = new Outer.Inner();` | `Outer.Inner obj = outerObj.new Inner();` |
    | Use Case | Utility/helper grouping | Tight coupling with outer object |
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    | **Static Member** | **Behavior During Execution** |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Static Variables** | Allocated once per class; stored in **method area**. |
    | **Static Methods** | Associated with class, not objects; stored in method area. |
    | **Static Blocks** | Executed once when class is loaded by ClassLoader. |
    | **Static Nested Class** | Compiled as a separate `.class` file with `$` notation (e.g., `Outer$Inner.class`). |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | **Static Member** | **Analogy** |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Static Variable** | Shared information board (visible to all employees) |
    | **Static Method** | Utility function (like a company-wide helpline number) |
    | **Static Block** | One-time setup operation (like initializing systems before office starts) |
    | **Static Nested Class** | Department inside a company – logically belongs to the company but works independently |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Important Points**
    
    - Static members belong to the **class**, not individual objects.
    - Loaded **once** when the class is first used.
    - **Static variables** are shared, **static methods** are accessible without objects.
    - **Static blocks** execute during class loading.
    - **Static nested classes** can access only static members of their enclosing class.
    - `this` and `super` keywords cannot be used inside static contexts.
    - Overuse of static members reduces **object orientation** — use only when necessary.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Summary**
    
    | **Static Member** | **Purpose** | **Key Property** | **Access Requirement** |
    | --- | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Static Variable** | Shared data | Single copy for class | Access via `ClassName.variable` |
    | **Static Method** | Common behavior | Independent of objects | Access via `ClassName.method()` |
    | **Static Block** | One-time initialization | Runs once at class load time | Auto-executed |
    | **Static Nested Class** | Logical grouping | No outer object needed | Access via `Outer.Nested` |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Static members are **class-level entities** that enable **shared data**, **utility methods**, and **one-time initialization**.
    
    They are crucial for designing **memory-efficient**, **organized**, and **utility-driven** Java programs while maintaining clear separation between **instance-level** and **class-level** behavior.
    
- **Nested Classes**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **nested class** is a class defined **inside another class**.
    
    It helps to logically group classes that are **closely associated**, increases **encapsulation**, and makes the code **more readable** and **organized**.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    In Java, nested classes are used when one class is **useful only to another class** and should not be exposed outside unnecessarily.
    
    They act as **helper or supportive components** inside the outer class.
    
    There are **two major types** of nested classes:
    
    1. **Static nested classes**
    2. **Non-static nested classes (Inner classes)**
        - Member inner classes
        - Local classes
        - Anonymous classes
    
    ---
    
    ### **Basic Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class OuterClass {
        class InnerClass {
            // code
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Advantages of Using Nested Classes**
    
    - Logical grouping of related classes.
    - Improved encapsulation (can hide inner details).
    - Easier code maintenance.
    - Enhanced readability and modularity.
    - Inner classes can access private members of the outer class.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Static Nested Classes**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **static nested class** is a nested class declared with the `static` keyword.
    
    It **does not require an instance** of the outer class to be created and behaves like a **regular top-level class**, except that it is **scoped within** the outer class.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class Outer {
        static class Nested {
            // code
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Static Nested Class**
    
    ```java
    class Outer {
        static int outerValue = 100;
    
        static class Inner {
            void display() {
                System.out.println("Outer value: " + outerValue);
            }
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Outer.Inner obj = new Outer.Inner(); // no outer object required
            obj.display();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Outer value: 100
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Key Points**
    
    | Property | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | Belongs to | Outer class, not instance |
    | Access to outer members | Can access only **static members** of outer class |
    | Instantiation | Without outer class object |
    | File structure | Compiled as `Outer$Inner.class` |
    | Use case | Helper or utility class related to outer class |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Real-World Usage**
    
    ```java
    class Database {
        static class Config {
            static String URL = "jdbc:mysql://localhost/test";
            static String USER = "root";
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            System.out.println("Connecting to: " + Database.Config.URL);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Connecting to: jdbc:mysql://localhost/test
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Inner Classes (Non-static Nested Classes)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    An **inner class** (also called a **non-static nested class**) is associated with an **instance** of the outer class.
    
    It **can access all members** (including private ones) of its outer class.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class Outer {
        class Inner {
            // code
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Member Inner Class**
    
    ```java
    class Outer {
        private String message = "Hello from Outer class!";
    
        class Inner {
            void display() {
                System.out.println(message); // can access private member
            }
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Outer outer = new Outer();
            Outer.Inner inner = outer.new Inner(); // requires outer instance
            inner.display();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Hello from Outer class!
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Accessing Inner Class Members**
    
    Inner class is tied to an instance of the outer class:
    
    ```java
    Outer.Inner innerObject = new Outer().new Inner();
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Key Points**
    
    | Property | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | Associated with | Instance of outer class |
    | Access | Can access both static and non-static members of outer class |
    | Requires outer object | Yes |
    | Instantiation | `Outer.Inner inner = outerObj.new Inner();` |
    | Use case | Encapsulating helper logic tied to outer object |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Accessing Outer Members**
    
    ```java
    class Company {
        private String name = "TechSoft";
    
        class Employee {
            void show() {
                System.out.println("Company name: " + name);
            }
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Local Classes**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **local class** is a class declared **within a method**, **constructor**, or **block**.
    
    It is **local to the scope** in which it is defined and cannot be accessed outside of it.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class Outer {
        void outerMethod() {
            class Local {
                void display() {
                    System.out.println("Inside local class");
                }
            }
            Local obj = new Local();
            obj.display();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Local Inner Class**
    
    ```java
    public class Outer {
        void greeting() {
            class Local {
                void sayHello() {
                    System.out.println("Hello from Local Inner Class!");
                }
            }
    
            Local local = new Local();
            local.sayHello();
        }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Outer o = new Outer();
            o.greeting();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Hello from Local Inner Class!
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Rules for Local Classes**
    
    | Rule | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | Scope | Exists only within the method/block it is declared |
    | Access to outer members | Can access outer class members |
    | Access to local variables | Can access only **final or effectively final** local variables |
    | Instantiation | Only within the same block or method |
    | Modifiers allowed | Cannot be declared `static`, but can be `abstract` or `final` |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Accessing Method Variables**
    
    ```java
    class Outer {
        void calculate(final int num) {
            class Square {
                void printSquare() {
                    System.out.println("Square: " + (num * num));
                }
            }
            new Square().printSquare();
        }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            new Outer().calculate(5);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Square: 25
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    The local class `Square` can access `num` because it is effectively final.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Anonymous Classes**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    An **anonymous class** is a **local class without a name**, created **on the fly** for **instantiating objects with specific behavior**.
    
    They are typically used to **override methods** of a class or interface **inline**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    InterfaceOrClass obj = new InterfaceOrClass() {
        // method implementation
    };
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Anonymous Class Implementing Interface**
    
    ```java
    interface Greeting {
        void sayHello();
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Greeting g = new Greeting() {
                public void sayHello() {
                    System.out.println("Hello from Anonymous Class!");
                }
            };
    
            g.sayHello();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Hello from Anonymous Class!
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Anonymous Class Extending a Class**
    
    ```java
    class Animal {
        void sound() {
            System.out.println("Animal sound");
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Animal dog = new Animal() {
                void sound() {
                    System.out.println("Dog barks");
                }
            };
    
            dog.sound();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Dog barks
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    Here, the anonymous subclass of `Animal` overrides the `sound()` method at runtime.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Anonymous Class as Argument**
    
    ```java
    abstract class Processor {
        abstract void process();
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            execute(new Processor() {
                void process() {
                    System.out.println("Processing anonymously...");
                }
            });
        }
    
        static void execute(Processor p) {
            p.process();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Processing anonymously...
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Key Points**
    
    | Property | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | Name | No explicit name |
    | Declaration | Done at object creation |
    | Common Use | For interface or abstract class implementation |
    | Scope | Within the expression it is created |
    | Compilation | Compiler creates a synthetic class like `Outer$1.class` |
    | Limitation | Cannot have constructors or static declarations |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Anonymous Class with Runnable**
    
    ```java
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Thread t = new Thread(new Runnable() {
                public void run() {
                    System.out.println("Thread running using anonymous class!");
                }
            });
            t.start();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Thread running using anonymous class!
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Comparison of All Nested Class Types**
    
    | **Type** | **Static** | **Associated With** | **Access to Outer Members** | **Instantiation** | **Use Case** |
    | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Static Nested Class** | Yes | Outer class | Only static members | `new Outer.Nested()` | Helper classes |
    | **Inner (Member) Class** | No | Outer instance | All members (including private) | `outer.new Inner()` | Tightly coupled helper |
    | **Local Class** | No | Method/block | Outer and final local vars | Inside method | Temporary, short-lived helper |
    | **Anonymous Class** | No | Expression | Outer and final local vars | Inline creation | Event handling, single use behavior |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    | **Nested Type** | **Compiled As** | **Loaded By** | **Access Rights** |
    | --- | --- | --- | --- |
    | Static Nested Class | `Outer$Inner.class` | ClassLoader (once per class) | Can access only static members |
    | Inner Class | `Outer$Inner.class` | Created with outer instance | Accesses outer instance directly |
    | Local Class | `Outer$1Local.class` | Scoped to method | Accesses final local vars |
    | Anonymous Class | `Outer$1.class`, `Outer$2.class`, etc. | Created dynamically | Accesses enclosing context |
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | **Nested Type** | **Analogy** |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Static Nested Class** | A **manual** that belongs to a company, but not to any specific employee. |
    | **Inner Class** | An **ID card** that belongs specifically to an employee (dependent on instance). |
    | **Local Class** | A **temporary pass** used only inside a department meeting. |
    | **Anonymous Class** | A **visitor badge** created instantly for a one-time guest entry. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Important Points**
    
    - Nested classes improve **encapsulation** and **code organization**.
    - **Static nested classes** behave like independent top-level classes.
    - **Inner classes** require an outer instance and have full access to its members.
    - **Local classes** are limited to the scope of a method or block.
    - **Anonymous classes** provide **concise, one-time implementations**.
    - Anonymous and local classes can access only **final or effectively final** variables.
    - Overuse of nested classes can reduce code readability — use only for **logical grouping**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Summary**
    
    | **Nested Class Type** | **Static Modifier** | **Outer Object Required** | **Access to Outer Members** | **Common Usage** |
    | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Static Nested Class** | Yes | No | Only static members | Utility or grouping |
    | **Inner (Member) Class** | No | Yes | All members | Encapsulated helper |
    | **Local Class** | No | Yes (inside method) | Outer + final locals | Temporary inner logic |
    | **Anonymous Class** | No | Implicitly yes | Outer + final locals | Inline single-use behavior |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Nested classes in Java allow developers to build **modular, encapsulated, and maintainable** code structures.
    
    They support **tight integration** between outer and inner classes while offering varying levels of **independence and scope**, depending on the type of nesting used.
    

### 2.8 Initialization and Object Lifecycle

- **Initialization Blocks**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Initialization Blocks** in Java are **special code blocks** used to initialize data **automatically** when a class or object is loaded or created.
    
    They are executed **before constructors or main method**, depending on their type.
    
    There are **two main types** of initialization blocks:
    
    1. **Static Initialization Blocks (SIB)** — executed once when the class is loaded.
    2. **Instance Initialization Blocks (IIB)** — executed every time an object is created, before the constructor.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Purpose**
    
    - To perform **common initialization logic** shared by multiple constructors.
    - To **initialize static or instance variables** that require complex setup.
    - To execute **setup code** that must run at class loading or object creation time.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Static Initialization Blocks (SIB)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    A **Static Initialization Block** (also called **static block**) is used to **initialize static variables** or perform **one-time setup** operations.
    
    It is executed **once** when the class is **loaded into memory** by the **JVM ClassLoader**, before any object creation or main method execution.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class ClassName {
        static {
            // static initialization code
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Static Initialization Block**
    
    ```java
    class DatabaseConfig {
        static String DB_URL;
        static String USER;
    
        // Static Initialization Block
        static {
            System.out.println("Static block executed");
            DB_URL = "jdbc:mysql://localhost:3306/test";
            USER = "admin";
        }
    
        public static void displayConfig() {
            System.out.println("DB: " + DB_URL + ", User: " + USER);
        }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            System.out.println("Main method started");
            DatabaseConfig.displayConfig();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Static block executed
    Main method started
    DB: jdbc:mysql://localhost:3306/test, User: admin
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Execution Timing (Class Loading)**
    
    - A static block executes **once per class**, when the **JVM loads the class into memory**.
    - It executes **before the main method** and before **any static member** is accessed.
    - The **order of execution**:
        1. Class loaded by ClassLoader.
        2. Static variables initialized (default values).
        3. **Static blocks executed (in order of declaration).**
        4. Main method or object creation begins.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Demonstrating Execution Timing**
    
    ```java
    class Test {
        static {
            System.out.println("Static Block 1");
        }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            System.out.println("Main Method");
        }
    
        static {
            System.out.println("Static Block 2");
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Static Block 1
    Static Block 2
    Main Method
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Multiple Static Blocks Order**
    
    - You can define **multiple static blocks** in a class.
    - They are executed **in the order they appear** in the source file.
    
    | Declaration Order | Execution Order |
    | --- | --- |
    | Top to Bottom | Sequential |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Multiple Static Blocks**
    
    ```java
    class MultiBlockExample {
        static {
            System.out.println("Static Block 1");
        }
        static {
            System.out.println("Static Block 2");
        }
        static {
            System.out.println("Static Block 3");
        }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            System.out.println("Main Method");
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Static Block 1
    Static Block 2
    Static Block 3
    Main Method
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Cases of Static Blocks**
    
    - Initializing **static variables** that need complex setup (e.g., reading config files).
    - Performing **one-time tasks** like registering drivers or connecting to a database.
    - Logging or pre-loading resources before main execution.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Restrictions**
    
    | Rule | Description |
    | --- | --- |
    | Can only access static members | Instance members cannot be used |
    | Runs only once per class load | Even if multiple objects are created |
    | Cannot use `this` or `super` | No instance context exists |
    | Runs automatically | No explicit call required |
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Instance Initialization Blocks (IIB)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    An **Instance Initialization Block (IIB)** is a non-static block that is executed **each time an object is created**, **before the constructor** runs.
    
    It is used to **initialize instance variables** or execute common setup code shared across multiple constructors.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Syntax**
    
    ```java
    class ClassName {
        {
            // instance initialization code
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Instance Initialization Block**
    
    ```java
    class Person {
        String name;
        int age;
    
        // Instance Initialization Block
        {
            System.out.println("Instance Initialization Block executed");
            age = 18; // default value for all persons
        }
    
        Person(String name) {
            this.name = name;
            System.out.println("Constructor executed for " + name);
        }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            new Person("Alice");
            new Person("Bob");
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Instance Initialization Block executed
    Constructor executed for Alice
    Instance Initialization Block executed
    Constructor executed for Bob
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - The IIB runs **every time** a new object is created.
    - It executes **before the constructor**, setting up default values or common logic.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Execution Order with Constructors**
    
    | **Execution Order** | **Step Description** |
    | --- | --- |
    | 1 | Memory allocated for object |
    | 2 | Instance variables initialized with default values |
    | 3 | **Instance Initialization Block executes** |
    | 4 | Constructor executes |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Execution Flow**
    
    ```java
    class Example {
        {
            System.out.println("Instance Block 1");
        }
    
        Example() {
            System.out.println("Constructor");
        }
    
        {
            System.out.println("Instance Block 2");
        }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            new Example();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Instance Block 1
    Instance Block 2
    Constructor
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    Multiple IIBs execute **in the order they appear**, **before** the constructor.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Using IIB with Inheritance**
    
    If a subclass and superclass both have initialization blocks:
    
    1. Superclass IIB executes first.
    2. Then subclass IIB.
    3. Finally, subclass constructor.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: IIB with Inheritance**
    
    ```java
    class Parent {
        {
            System.out.println("Parent IIB");
        }
    }
    
    class Child extends Parent {
        {
            System.out.println("Child IIB");
        }
    
        Child() {
            System.out.println("Child Constructor");
        }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            new Child();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Parent IIB
    Child IIB
    Child Constructor
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Use Cases vs Constructors**
    
    | **Aspect** | **Instance Initialization Block (IIB)** | **Constructor** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Purpose** | Common code for all constructors | Object-specific initialization |
    | **Execution Time** | Before constructor | After IIB |
    | **Reusability** | Shared among all constructors | Specific to each constructor |
    | **Access** | Can access both static and instance members | Can access all members |
    | **Overloading** | Cannot be overloaded | Can be overloaded |
    | **Usage** | When multiple constructors share common setup code | For distinct initialization logic |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Common Setup Using IIB**
    
    ```java
    class Employee {
        String name;
        int id;
        static int counter = 1000;
    
        {
            id = ++counter; // auto-assign unique ID
            System.out.println("Employee object initializing...");
        }
    
        Employee(String name) {
            this.name = name;
            System.out.println("Employee " + name + " created with ID: " + id);
        }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            new Employee("John");
            new Employee("Sara");
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Employee object initializing...
    Employee John created with ID: 1001
    Employee object initializing...
    Employee Sara created with ID: 1002
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    The IIB provides a **common initialization task** (auto-ID assignment) for all constructors, avoiding duplication.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Differences Between Static and Instance Initialization Blocks**
    
    | **Aspect** | **Static Initialization Block (SIB)** | **Instance Initialization Block (IIB)** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Keyword** | Declared with `static` | No keyword |
    | **Execution Time** | When class is loaded | Every time object is created |
    | **Execution Frequency** | Once per class | Once per object |
    | **Access Level** | Can access only static members | Can access both static and instance members |
    | **Use Case** | Initialize static variables | Initialize instance variables |
    | **Order of Execution** | Before main method | Before constructor |
    | **Associated With** | Class | Object |
    | **Inheritance** | Not inherited | Inherited and executed before subclass constructor |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Combined Example: Static and Instance Blocks**
    
    ```java
    class Demo {
        static {
            System.out.println("Static Block");
        }
    
        {
            System.out.println("Instance Block");
        }
    
        Demo() {
            System.out.println("Constructor");
        }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            System.out.println("Main Method");
            new Demo();
            new Demo();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Static Block
    Main Method
    Instance Block
    Constructor
    Instance Block
    Constructor
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - **Static Block** executes once when class loads.
    - **Instance Block** executes before every constructor call.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    | **Phase** | **Static Initialization Block** | **Instance Initialization Block** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **When executed** | During class loading | During object creation |
    | **Storage Area** | Method area (class-level) | Heap (object-level) |
    | **Trigger** | ClassLoader | `new` keyword |
    | **Order Enforcement** | Top to bottom in source | Top to bottom in class definition |
    | **Optimization** | Pre-compiled into `<clinit>()` method | Compiled into constructors (`<init>()`) |
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | Concept | Analogy |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Static Block** | Powering on a machine once — runs setup before any operation. |
    | **Instance Block** | Initialization checklist every time a new device is started. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Important Points**
    
    - **Static blocks** execute **once** when class is loaded.
    - **Instance blocks** execute **before constructors** every time an object is created.
    - Multiple blocks execute in **declaration order**.
    - **IIBs** can access both static and instance data, while **SIBs** only static.
    - Both can coexist — static for class-level setup, instance for object setup.
    - IIBs help **reduce constructor code duplication**.
    - Static blocks are often used for **initializing constants**, **drivers**, or **framework components**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Summary**
    
    | **Block Type** | **Keyword** | **Execution Time** | **Access Scope** | **Execution Count** | **Use Case** |
    | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Static Initialization Block (SIB)** | `static` | At class loading | Only static members | Once per class | Static variable initialization |
    | **Instance Initialization Block (IIB)** | (none) | At object creation (before constructor) | Static + instance members | Once per object | Common instance setup |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Initialization blocks in Java are powerful mechanisms that allow **automatic code execution** during **class loading** or **object creation**.
    
    They improve **readability**, **reduce redundancy**, and provide **controlled initialization** for both static and instance-level data.
    
- **Initialization Sequence**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    The **initialization sequence** in Java defines the **exact order** in which **static blocks**, **instance blocks**, and **constructors** execute during **class loading** and **object creation**.
    
    It ensures that both **class-level** (static) and **object-level** (instance) data are properly initialized before being used.
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    When a Java program runs:
    
    1. Classes are **loaded into memory** by the **JVM’s ClassLoader**.
    2. **Static initialization** occurs once per class (static blocks/variables).
    3. When an object is created, **instance initialization** occurs (instance blocks/variables and constructors).
    4. If inheritance is involved, **parent classes** are initialized **before** child classes.
    
    This sequence guarantees **deterministic and consistent initialization** across all Java classes.
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Complete Execution Order**
    
    ---
    
    ### **General Rule**
    
    When a Java program starts, the **initialization order** follows this sequence:
    
    1. **Static initialization of Parent class**
        
        (Static variables and static blocks of parent class)
        
    2. **Static initialization of Child class**
        
        (Static variables and static blocks of child class)
        
    3. **Instance initialization of Parent class**
        
        (Instance variables and instance blocks)
        
    4. **Constructor of Parent class**
    5. **Instance initialization of Child class**
        
        (Instance variables and instance blocks)
        
    6. **Constructor of Child class**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Simplified Flow**
    
    | **Step** | **Initialization Level** | **What Executes** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | 1 | Parent Class (Static) | Static variables and static blocks |
    | 2 | Child Class (Static) | Static variables and static blocks |
    | 3 | Parent Class (Instance) | Instance variables and instance blocks |
    | 4 | Parent Class Constructor | Constructor body |
    | 5 | Child Class (Instance) | Instance variables and instance blocks |
    | 6 | Child Class Constructor | Constructor body |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Mnemonic Order**
    
    > Static → Instance → Constructor
    > 
    > 
    > (First Parent, then Child)
    > 
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Static Initialization Phase**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    When the class is loaded by the JVM (before any object is created), all **static variables** and **static blocks** are executed **once per class** in **top-to-bottom order**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Static Initialization**
    
    ```java
    class Parent {
        static { System.out.println("Parent Static Block"); }
    }
    
    class Child extends Parent {
        static { System.out.println("Child Static Block"); }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            System.out.println("Main Method");
            new Child();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Parent Static Block
    Child Static Block
    Main Method
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - Static blocks of **Parent** load first.
    - Then **Child** static blocks load.
    - Then the **main()** method executes.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Instance Initialization Phase**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    When an object is created using `new`, the following sequence occurs for **each class in the inheritance chain**:
    
    1. Instance variables are initialized.
    2. Instance initialization blocks execute.
    3. Constructor executes.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Instance Initialization**
    
    ```java
    class Parent {
        { System.out.println("Parent Instance Block"); }
    
        Parent() {
            System.out.println("Parent Constructor");
        }
    }
    
    class Child extends Parent {
        { System.out.println("Child Instance Block"); }
    
        Child() {
            System.out.println("Child Constructor");
        }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            new Child();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Parent Instance Block
    Parent Constructor
    Child Instance Block
    Child Constructor
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    1. Parent’s instance block runs first.
    2. Parent’s constructor executes.
    3. Child’s instance block executes.
    4. Child’s constructor runs last.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Complete Example (Static + Instance + Constructor)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Code Example**
    
    ```java
    class Parent {
        static { System.out.println("Parent Static Block"); }
        { System.out.println("Parent Instance Block"); }
    
        Parent() {
            System.out.println("Parent Constructor");
        }
    }
    
    class Child extends Parent {
        static { System.out.println("Child Static Block"); }
        { System.out.println("Child Instance Block"); }
    
        Child() {
            System.out.println("Child Constructor");
        }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            System.out.println("Main Method Start");
            new Child();
            System.out.println("Main Method End");
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Parent Static Block
    Child Static Block
    Main Method Start
    Parent Instance Block
    Parent Constructor
    Child Instance Block
    Child Constructor
    Main Method End
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Step-by-Step Explanation**
    
    | **Step** | **Phase** | **Execution Detail** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | 1 | Static | Parent’s static block executes first (class loaded). |
    | 2 | Static | Child’s static block executes (child class loaded). |
    | 3 | Runtime | Main method begins execution. |
    | 4 | Instance | Parent’s instance block executes. |
    | 5 | Constructor | Parent’s constructor executes. |
    | 6 | Instance | Child’s instance block executes. |
    | 7 | Constructor | Child’s constructor executes. |
    | 8 | Runtime | Main method ends. |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Key Rule**
    
    > Parent initialization always completes before Child initialization — both for static and instance phases.
    > 
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Static and Instance Execution Combined (Detailed Flow)**
    
    Let’s generalize for **object creation** in a program:
    
    | **Phase** | **Parent Class** | **Child Class** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Static Initialization (Class Loading)** | Static Variables + Static Blocks | Static Variables + Static Blocks |
    | **Instance Initialization (Object Creation)** | Instance Variables + Instance Blocks + Constructor | Instance Variables + Instance Blocks + Constructor |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Illustration**
    
    ```
    Class Loading:
     ├── Parent static → Child static
    Object Creation:
     ├── Parent instance → Parent constructor
     ├── Child instance → Child constructor
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Example: Multiple Objects Creation**
    
    When multiple objects are created, note that **static blocks run only once**, while **instance blocks and constructors run for each object**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example**
    
    ```java
    class Example {
        static { System.out.println("Static Block"); }
        { System.out.println("Instance Block"); }
        Example() { System.out.println("Constructor"); }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            new Example();
            new Example();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Static Block
    Instance Block
    Constructor
    Instance Block
    Constructor
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - Static block → executed only once when class loads.
    - Instance block + constructor → executed each time an object is created.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Parent–Child Initialization Example (Complete)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Code Example**
    
    ```java
    class A {
        static { System.out.println("A: Static Block"); }
        { System.out.println("A: Instance Block"); }
    
        A() {
            System.out.println("A: Constructor");
        }
    }
    
    class B extends A {
        static { System.out.println("B: Static Block"); }
        { System.out.println("B: Instance Block"); }
    
        B() {
            System.out.println("B: Constructor");
        }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            System.out.println("Main Start");
            new B();
            System.out.println("Main End");
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    A: Static Block
    B: Static Block
    Main Start
    A: Instance Block
    A: Constructor
    B: Instance Block
    B: Constructor
    Main End
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Stepwise Execution**
    
    1. **Class Loading**
        - `A` is loaded → executes static block.
        - `B` is loaded → executes static block.
    2. **Object Creation**
        - `A`’s instance block → `A`’s constructor.
        - `B`’s instance block → `B`’s constructor.
    3. **Main method continues** after object creation.
    
    ---
    
    ## **9. Execution Order Summary**
    
    | **Order** | **Phase** | **Component Executed** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | 1 | Static | Parent static blocks & static variables |
    | 2 | Static | Child static blocks & static variables |
    | 3 | Instance | Parent instance variables & blocks |
    | 4 | Constructor | Parent constructor |
    | 5 | Instance | Child instance variables & blocks |
    | 6 | Constructor | Child constructor |
    
    ---
    
    ## **10. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | **Phase** | **Analogy** |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Static Initialization** | Setting up company-wide resources (e.g., network, database setup) – done once. |
    | **Instance Initialization** | Preparing a workspace for a new employee (personalized setup). |
    | **Constructor Execution** | Welcoming the employee with a final onboarding (custom setup). |
    | **Parent–Child Initialization** | Company rules (parent) always set up before team-specific rules (child). |
    
    ---
    
    ## **11. JVM / Compiler Behavior**
    
    | **Stage** | **Behavior** |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Class Loading** | JVM loads classes into the **method area**, executing all static blocks and initializing static variables. |
    | **Object Creation** | The **heap** is allocated, instance variables initialized, instance blocks run, and constructors executed. |
    | **Inheritance** | JVM ensures superclass initialization completes before subclass execution. |
    | **Bytecode Mapping** | Static blocks are compiled into a `<clinit>()` method; instance blocks are part of `<init>()` constructor. |
    
    ---
    
    ## **12. Important Points**
    
    - Static blocks execute **once per class** — at class load time.
    - Instance blocks execute **before every constructor call**.
    - **Parent class always initializes first**, for both static and instance contexts.
    - **Multiple static/instance blocks** execute **in declaration order**.
    - Constructors execute **after** instance blocks.
    - The sequence is consistent across all Java versions and JVMs.
    
    ---
    
    ## **13. Summary**
    
    | **Step** | **Phase** | **Component Executed** | **Frequency** |
    | --- | --- | --- | --- |
    | 1 | Parent Static | Static blocks + static variables | Once per class |
    | 2 | Child Static | Static blocks + static variables | Once per class |
    | 3 | Parent Instance | Instance variables + instance blocks | Per object |
    | 4 | Parent Constructor | Constructor | Per object |
    | 5 | Child Instance | Instance variables + instance blocks | Per object |
    | 6 | Child Constructor | Constructor | Per object |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Java follows a **top-down initialization hierarchy** where **static initialization happens once**, and **instance initialization** happens each time an object is created.
    
    Within inheritance, **parent class initialization always precedes child class initialization**, ensuring a well-defined and predictable setup for every object.
    
- **Object Lifecycle Management**
    
    ---
    
    ## **1. Definition and Explanation**
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Object Lifecycle Management** in Java refers to the **entire journey of an object** — from its **creation**, through **usage**, to its **destruction (garbage collection)** by the JVM.
    
    It involves:
    
    - How objects are **created**
    - How they are **used or reused**
    - How and when they become **eligible for garbage collection**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    In Java, objects are **dynamically allocated** in the **heap memory** using the `new` keyword.
    
    Unlike low-level languages like C or C++, Java automatically manages memory through **Garbage Collection (GC)** — meaning developers don’t manually destroy objects.
    
    The **JVM** ensures that unused objects (those with **no active references**) are automatically cleaned up to free memory and prevent memory leaks.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Phases of an Object Lifecycle**
    
    | **Phase** | **Description** |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Creation** | Object allocated in heap memory using `new` or factory methods. |
    | **Usage** | Object used by application logic (methods, fields, etc.). |
    | **Dereferencing** | References to object are removed or go out of scope. |
    | **Garbage Collection** | JVM identifies and removes unreachable objects. |
    | **Finalization (optional)** | Deprecated step — before object removal, GC may call `finalize()` (now discouraged). |
    
    ---
    
    ## **2. Object Creation Patterns**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    Object creation in Java refers to how and where instances of a class are **instantiated**.
    
    There are **multiple ways** to create objects, beyond just `new`.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Common Object Creation Methods**
    
    | **Method** | **Description** | **Example** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **1. Using `new` keyword** | Most common and direct method | `Car c = new Car();` |
    | **2. Using Class.forName()** | Reflection-based creation | `Car c = (Car) Class.forName("Car").newInstance();` |
    | **3. Using `clone()`** | Copies an existing object | `Car c2 = (Car) c1.clone();` |
    | **4. Using Deserialization** | Reads from object stream | `ObjectInputStream ois = new ObjectInputStream(...);` |
    | **5. Using Factory / Builder Pattern** | Indirect, via static methods | `Car c = CarFactory.createCar("SUV");` |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example 1: Standard Object Creation**
    
    ```java
    class Car {
        Car() {
            System.out.println("Car object created");
        }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Car c = new Car(); // using new
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Car object created
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example 2: Using Factory Method**
    
    ```java
    class Car {
        private Car() { }
    
        public static Car createCar() {
            System.out.println("Car created using Factory Method");
            return new Car();
        }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Car c = Car.createCar();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Car created using Factory Method
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - Factory or Builder patterns are used in large systems to **centralize object creation** logic, allowing **controlled instantiation** and sometimes **object reuse** (as in object pools).
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example 3: Cloning**
    
    ```java
    class Employee implements Cloneable {
        int id;
        Employee(int id) { this.id = id; }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) throws CloneNotSupportedException {
            Employee e1 = new Employee(101);
            Employee e2 = (Employee) e1.clone();
            System.out.println("Clone created: " + e2.id);
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Clone created: 101
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **3. Object Pooling Concepts**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    **Object Pooling** is a design technique where a fixed number of reusable objects are **pre-created and stored in memory**, and **recycled** rather than frequently created and destroyed.
    
    It is used to **improve performance** in systems where object creation is **costly** (e.g., database connections, threads).
    
    ---
    
    ### **Key Idea**
    
    > Instead of creating new objects each time, the system borrows an object from the pool and returns it when done.
    > 
    
    ---
    
    ### **Benefits**
    
    - Reduces **object creation overhead**.
    - Improves **memory management** and **performance**.
    - Prevents frequent **garbage collection** by reusing objects.
    - Ideal for **heavyweight objects** (e.g., `Connection`, `Thread`).
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Simple Object Pool**
    
    ```java
    import java.util.*;
    
    class Connection {
        private boolean inUse = false;
    
        void connect() {
            System.out.println("Using connection: " + this);
        }
    
        void disconnect() {
            inUse = false;
        }
    
        boolean isAvailable() {
            return !inUse;
        }
    
        void setInUse() {
            inUse = true;
        }
    }
    
    class ConnectionPool {
        private List<Connection> pool = new ArrayList<>();
    
        ConnectionPool(int size) {
            for (int i = 0; i < size; i++) {
                pool.add(new Connection());
            }
        }
    
        synchronized Connection getConnection() {
            for (Connection c : pool) {
                if (c.isAvailable()) {
                    c.setInUse();
                    return c;
                }
            }
            return null; // all busy
        }
    
        synchronized void releaseConnection(Connection c) {
            c.disconnect();
        }
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            ConnectionPool cp = new ConnectionPool(2);
    
            Connection c1 = cp.getConnection();
            c1.connect();
    
            Connection c2 = cp.getConnection();
            c2.connect();
    
            cp.releaseConnection(c1);
    
            Connection c3 = cp.getConnection();
            c3.connect();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output**
    
    ```
    Using connection: Connection@6d06d69c
    Using connection: Connection@7852e922
    Using connection: Connection@6d06d69c
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    - Only two connections exist in the pool.
    - The released connection is **reused** instead of creating a new one.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Real-world Examples of Object Pooling**
    
    | **Component** | **Framework / Library** | **Description** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **JDBC Connection Pool** | HikariCP, C3P0, DBCP | Reuses DB connections efficiently |
    | **Thread Pool** | `ExecutorService` | Manages reusable worker threads |
    | **String Constant Pool** | Java String intern pool | Reuses identical String literals |
    | **JMS / Socket Pooling** | ActiveMQ, Netty | Reuses network resources |
    
    ---
    
    ### **When to Use Object Pooling**
    
    - When object creation/destruction is **expensive**.
    - For **multithreaded** applications (e.g., web servers).
    - For **database or I/O-heavy** operations.
    
    ---
    
    ### **When Not to Use**
    
    - For **lightweight objects** (simple POJOs).
    - When **memory is limited** or objects vary in configuration.
    - When **thread safety** or synchronization overhead outweighs benefits.
    
    ---
    
    ## **4. Garbage Collection Eligibility (No References)**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Definition**
    
    An object becomes **eligible for Garbage Collection (GC)** when **no live references** to it exist in the running program — meaning it is **unreachable**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Explanation**
    
    - Java does not allow **explicit object destruction**.
    - The **Garbage Collector (GC)** automatically reclaims memory occupied by unreachable objects.
    - GC runs **periodically**, not immediately when objects become unreachable.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Ways an Object Becomes Eligible for GC**
    
    | **Condition** | **Example** | **Description** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **1. Reference set to null** | `obj = null;` | Removes reference explicitly |
    | **2. Reference reassigned** | `obj1 = new Car(); obj1 = new Car();` | Old object no longer referenced |
    | **3. Object goes out of scope** | Inside a method | Object reference lost after method exits |
    | **4. Island of Isolation** | Two objects refer to each other but are not reachable from any thread | Cyclic references eligible for GC |
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example 1: Nullifying Reference**
    
    ```java
    class Demo {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Demo d = new Demo();
            d = null; // eligible for GC
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example 2: Reassigning Reference**
    
    ```java
    class Car {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Car c1 = new Car();
            Car c2 = new Car();
            c1 = c2; // first object is now unreachable
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example 3: Object Goes Out of Scope**
    
    ```java
    class Test {
        void method() {
            Student s = new Student(); // local object
        } // s goes out of scope here; eligible for GC
    }
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example 4: Island of Isolation**
    
    ```java
    class A {
        B b;
    }
    class B {
        A a;
    }
    
    public class Main {
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            A a = new A();
            B b = new B();
    
            a.b = b;
            b.a = a;
    
            a = null;
            b = null;
            // Both A and B objects are now unreachable (island of isolation)
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    Even though `A` and `B` reference each other, they are **not reachable** from any active thread — so GC can collect both.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Checking Eligibility for GC (Indirectly)**
    
    There is **no direct way** to check if an object is GC-eligible, but we can hint GC using:
    
    ```java
    System.gc(); // Request GC
    Runtime.getRuntime().gc();
    
    ```
    
    **Note:**
    
    These are **suggestions**, not commands — JVM may ignore them.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Garbage Collector in Action**
    
    - GC uses algorithms like **Mark-and-Sweep**, **Generational Collection**, or **G1 GC**.
    - The GC marks all **reachable objects** and **removes unreferenced ones**.
    - It runs automatically when **heap memory is low** or **periodically** during runtime.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Deprecated `finalize()` Method**
    
    - Before GC deletes an object, it may call the **`finalize()`** method once.
    - Used for cleanup tasks (like closing files).
    - **Deprecated in Java 9** because it was **unreliable** and **non-deterministic**.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Best Practice**
    
    Use **try-with-resources** or explicit **close() methods** instead of relying on `finalize()`.
    
    ---
    
    ### **Example: Demonstrating finalize() (Deprecated)**
    
    ```java
    class Demo {
        @Override
        protected void finalize() throws Throwable {
            System.out.println("Finalize called before GC");
        }
    
        public static void main(String[] args) {
            Demo d = new Demo();
            d = null;
            System.gc();
        }
    }
    
    ```
    
    ### **Output (Non-deterministic)**
    
    ```
    Finalize called before GC
    
    ```
    
    **Explanation:**
    
    `finalize()` may or may not execute immediately, depending on GC timing.
    
    ---
    
    ## **5. Object Lifecycle Overview**
    
    ---
    
    ### **Diagrammatic Lifecycle Flow**
    
    ```
    ┌────────────────────────────┐
    │   Object Creation (new)    │
    └──────────────┬─────────────┘
                   │
                   ▼
    ┌────────────────────────────┐
    │   Object in Use (Active)   │
    └──────────────┬─────────────┘
                   │
                   ▼
    ┌────────────────────────────┐
    │   Dereferenced / Unused    │
    └──────────────┬─────────────┘
                   │
                   ▼
    ┌────────────────────────────┐
    │ Garbage Collector identifies│
    │   (unreachable objects)     │
    └──────────────┬─────────────┘
                   │
                   ▼
    ┌────────────────────────────┐
    │   Memory Reclaimed by JVM  │
    └────────────────────────────┘
    
    ```
    
    ---
    
    ## **6. Real-world Analogy**
    
    | **Lifecycle Phase** | **Analogy** |
    | --- | --- |
    | **Creation** | Buying a new car |
    | **Usage** | Driving the car |
    | **Dereferencing** | Parking and selling the car (no longer owned) |
    | **Garbage Collection** | Scrapping the car to reclaim materials (memory) |
    
    ---
    
    ## **7. Important Points**
    
    - **Object creation** is dynamic and occurs in **heap memory**.
    - **Object pooling** optimizes performance for expensive resources.
    - An object becomes **GC-eligible** when no live references exist.
    - The **Garbage Collector** runs automatically, not deterministically.
    - Avoid `finalize()` — use explicit cleanup methods or `AutoCloseable`.
    - **Strings, connections, and threads** often use pooling for efficiency.
    - Improper lifecycle management leads to **memory leaks** and **performance degradation**.
    
    ---
    
    ## **8. Summary**
    
    | **Phase** | **Concept** | **Key Behavior** |
    | --- | --- | --- |
    | **Creation** | `new`, cloning, deserialization, factory | Object allocated in heap |
    | **Usage** | Object manipulation via references | Object active in memory |
    | **Pooling** | Object reuse for efficiency | Reduces creation overhead |
    | **Dereferencing** | Losing reference or scope | Object becomes unreachable |
    | **Garbage Collection** | JVM reclaims memory | Automatic cleanup |
    
    **In summary:**
    
    Java’s **object lifecycle management** ensures efficient **memory allocation, reuse, and cleanup** through mechanisms like **object pooling** and **automatic garbage collection**.
    
    Understanding this sequence allows developers to write **efficient, leak-free, and high-performance** Java applications.
    

### 2.9 Modern Java OOP Features

- **Records (Java 16+)** (**NEW**)
    - Overview
        
        # Records in Java (Java 16+)
        
        ---
        
        ## 1. Definition and Explanation
        
        ### Definition
        
        A **record** in Java is a **special type of class** introduced in **Java 16 (finalized)** that is designed to act as a **transparent data carrier**.
        
        Records are primarily used to **store immutable data** with minimal boilerplate code.
        
        ### Purpose
        
        The main purpose of records is to:
        
        - Reduce verbosity in **data-holding classes**
        - Automatically generate common methods
        - Enforce **immutability by design**
        - Improve **readability, correctness, and maintainability**
        
        ### Simple Explanation
        
        In traditional Java, creating a class just to store data requires writing:
        
        - Fields
        - Constructor
        - Getters
        - `toString()`
        - `equals()`
        - `hashCode()`
        
        A **record automatically generates all of these** for you.
        
        ### Technical Explanation
        
        A record is a **restricted form of a class** where:
        
        - Fields are implicitly `private final`
        - Constructor, accessors, `equals()`, `hashCode()`, and `toString()` are auto-generated by the compiler
        - The class is implicitly `final`
        
        ---
        
        ## 2. Syntax
        
        ```java
        public record Employee(int id, String name, double salary) {
        }
        
        ```
        
        ### Explanation of Syntax
        
        - `record` keyword declares a record
        - `Employee` is the record name
        - `(int id, String name, double salary)` is called the **record header**
        - Each component becomes:
            - A `private final` field
            - A public accessor method with the same name
        
        ---
        
        ## 3. Auto-Generated Methods in Records
        
        For the above record, the compiler automatically generates:
        
        ```java
        public final class Employee extends java.lang.Record {
            private final int id;
            private final String name;
            private final double salary;
        
            public Employee(int id, String name, double salary) { ... }
        
            public int id() { return id; }
            public String name() { return name; }
            public double salary() { return salary; }
        
            public boolean equals(Object o) { ... }
            public int hashCode() { ... }
            public String toString() { ... }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ### Important Observation
        
        - Getter methods **do not start with `get`**
        - Accessors use the **field name directly**
        
        ---
        
        ## 4. Rules and Notes
        
        - Record fields are:
            - `private`
            - `final`
            - Immutable
        - Records:
            - Cannot extend other classes
            - Can implement interfaces
        - Records:
            - Are implicitly `final`
            - Cannot have instance fields outside the header
        - Records:
            - Can have static fields and methods
        - Records:
            - Can contain constructors, methods, and static blocks
        
        ---
        
        ## 5. Example: Basic Record Usage
        
        ```java
        public record Student(int rollNo, String name) {
        }
        
        public class Test {
            public static void main(String[] args) {
                Student s1 = new Student(101, "Amit");
                Student s2 = new Student(101, "Amit");
        
                System.out.println(s1);
                System.out.println(s1.name());
                System.out.println(s1.equals(s2));
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ### Output
        
        ```
        Student[rollNo=101, name=Amit]
        Amit
        true
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ## 6. Compact Constructors
        
        ### Definition
        
        A **compact constructor** is a special constructor used in records to **validate or normalize data** without re-declaring parameters.
        
        ### Syntax
        
        ```java
        public record Product(int id, double price) {
        
            public Product {
                if (price <= 0) {
                    throw new IllegalArgumentException("Price must be positive");
                }
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ### Explanation
        
        - Constructor name is omitted
        - Parameters are automatically available
        - Compiler inserts field assignment automatically
        
        ---
        
        ## 7. Example: Compact Constructor Validation
        
        ```java
        public record Account(int accNo, double balance) {
        
            public Account {
                if (balance < 0) {
                    throw new IllegalArgumentException("Balance cannot be negative");
                }
            }
        }
        
        public class Test {
            public static void main(String[] args) {
                Account a = new Account(1001, 5000);
                System.out.println(a);
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ### Output
        
        ```
        Account[accNo=1001, balance=5000.0]
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ## 8. JVM / Compiler Behavior
        
        ### Compiler Actions
        
        - Converts record into a final class
        - Generates:
            - Canonical constructor
            - Accessor methods
            - `equals()`, `hashCode()`, `toString()`
        - Inserts field assignments **after compact constructor validation**
        
        ### JVM Execution Flow
        
        1. Memory allocated for record object
        2. Compact constructor validation executed
        3. Fields assigned
        4. Object becomes immutable
        
        ---
        
        ## 9. Records vs Traditional Classes
        
        | Aspect | Record | Traditional Class |
        | --- | --- | --- |
        | Boilerplate Code | Very minimal | Very high |
        | Mutability | Immutable | Mutable or Immutable |
        | Inheritance | Cannot extend classes | Can extend classes |
        | Getters | Auto-generated | Manually written |
        | Use Case | Data carriers | Business logic |
        | `equals()` | Auto-generated | Manually written |
        | Thread Safety | Naturally safer | Depends on design |
        
        ---
        
        ## 10. When to Use Records vs Traditional Classes
        
        ### Use Records When:
        
        - Class represents **pure data**
        - Fields should not change
        - Value-based equality is required
        - DTOs, request/response objects
        - Configuration holders
        - API models
        
        ### Use Traditional Classes When:
        
        - Objects have **behavior and state changes**
        - Inheritance is required
        - Mutable fields are needed
        - Complex lifecycle management exists
        
        ---
        
        ## 11. Real-world Analogy
        
        A **record** is like a **printed form**:
        
        - All fields are filled once
        - No changes allowed after submission
        - Equality depends on form content
        
        A **traditional class** is like a **notebook**:
        
        - Data can be written, erased, modified
        - Behavior can be added freely
        
        ---
        
        ## 12. Important Points
        
        - Records enforce **immutability by default**
        - Records reduce bugs caused by mutable state
        - Records improve code readability
        - Records are ideal for **data transfer objects**
        - Records are not replacements for all classes
        
        ---
        
        ## 13. Summary
        
        Records are a powerful Java feature introduced to simplify the creation of immutable data carrier classes. They eliminate boilerplate code by automatically generating constructors and common methods. With compact constructors, records support validation while maintaining immutability. Records should be used for data-centric models, while traditional classes remain suitable for behavior-heavy designs.
        
- **Sealed Classes (Java 17+)** (**NEW**)
    - Overview
        
        # Sealed Classes in Java (Java 17+)
        
        ---
        
        ## 1. Definition and Explanation
        
        ### Definition
        
        A **sealed class** in Java is a class or interface that **restricts which other classes or interfaces can extend or implement it**.
        
        This feature was finalized in **Java 17** and is introduced to provide **controlled inheritance**.
        
        ### Purpose
        
        The primary goals of sealed classes are:
        
        - To **explicitly control class hierarchies**
        - To prevent unintended or unsafe subclassing
        - To make inheritance **more predictable and maintainable**
        - To enable **exhaustiveness checking** in pattern matching
        
        ### Simple Explanation
        
        In normal Java inheritance, **any class can extend another class** unless it is marked `final`.
        
        Sealed classes allow us to say:
        
        > “Only these specific classes are allowed to extend me.”
        > 
        
        ### Technical Explanation
        
        A sealed class explicitly lists its permitted subclasses using the `permits` clause.
        
        Every permitted subclass must declare whether it is:
        
        - `final`
        - `sealed`
        - `non-sealed`
        
        This ensures that the inheritance tree is **closed and well-defined**.
        
        ---
        
        ## 2. Syntax
        
        ### Basic Syntax of a Sealed Class
        
        ```java
        public sealed class Shape
                permits Circle, Rectangle, Triangle {
        }
        
        ```
        
        ### Syntax of Permitted Subclasses
        
        ```java
        public final class Circle extends Shape {
        }
        
        public sealed class Rectangle extends Shape
                permits FilledRectangle {
        }
        
        public non-sealed class Triangle extends Shape {
        }
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ## 3. Keywords Used in Sealed Classes
        
        ### `sealed`
        
        - Used to declare a sealed class or interface
        - Restricts inheritance to known subclasses
        
        ### `permits`
        
        - Lists the allowed subclasses
        - Mandatory unless subclasses are in the same file
        
        ### `non-sealed`
        
        - Removes restriction for further subclassing
        - Opens the inheritance chain again
        
        ### `final`
        
        - Completely stops inheritance
        
        ---
        
        ## 4. Rules and Notes
        
        - A sealed class must:
            - Be declared as `sealed`
            - Specify permitted subclasses explicitly or implicitly
        - Permitted subclasses must:
            - Extend the sealed class directly
            - Declare themselves as `final`, `sealed`, or `non-sealed`
        - Sealed classes:
            - Cannot be anonymous
            - Cannot be extended by unauthorized classes
        - Sealed classes can be:
            - Abstract or concrete
        - Sealed interfaces are also supported
        
        ---
        
        ## 5. Example: Controlled Inheritance
        
        ```java
        public sealed class Vehicle
                permits Car, Bike {
        }
        
        public final class Car extends Vehicle {
        }
        
        public non-sealed class Bike extends Vehicle {
        }
        
        ```
        
        ### Explanation
        
        - `Vehicle` allows only `Car` and `Bike`
        - `Car` cannot be extended further
        - `Bike` can be extended by any class
        
        ---
        
        ## 6. Compiler Enforcement Example
        
        ### Invalid Subclass
        
        ```java
        public class Truck extends Vehicle {
        }
        
        ```
        
        ### Compilation Error
        
        ```
        class is not allowed to extend sealed class: Vehicle
        
        ```
        
        ### Meaning
        
        The compiler strictly enforces the inheritance rules defined by the sealed class.
        
        ---
        
        ## 7. Sealed Interfaces
        
        ### Example
        
        ```java
        public sealed interface Payment
                permits CashPayment, CardPayment {
        }
        
        public final class CashPayment implements Payment {
        }
        
        public final class CardPayment implements Payment {
        }
        
        ```
        
        ### Notes
        
        - Sealed interfaces are useful for **finite implementations**
        - Commonly used with **strategy-style designs**
        
        ---
        
        ## 8. Exhaustiveness Checking (Pattern Matching)
        
        ### Definition
        
        **Exhaustiveness checking** ensures that **all possible permitted subclasses** of a sealed type are handled in `switch` or pattern matching constructs.
        
        ### Why It Matters
        
        - Eliminates missing-case bugs
        - Improves compile-time safety
        - Makes code future-proof
        
        ---
        
        ## 9. Example: Exhaustive `switch` with Sealed Classes
        
        ```java
        public sealed interface Shape
                permits Circle, Square {
        }
        
        public final class Circle implements Shape {
        }
        
        public final class Square implements Shape {
        }
        
        ```
        
        ```java
        public class Test {
            public static String identify(Shape shape) {
                return switch (shape) {
                    case Circle c -> "This is a Circle";
                    case Square s -> "This is a Square";
                };
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ### Important Observation
        
        - No `default` case is required
        - Compiler verifies all permitted subclasses are covered
        
        ---
        
        ## 10. Non-Exhaustive Example (Compiler Error)
        
        ```java
        return switch (shape) {
            case Circle c -> "Circle";
        };
        
        ```
        
        ### Compilation Error
        
        ```
        the switch expression does not cover all possible input values
        
        ```
        
        ### Reason
        
        The compiler knows `Square` is also a permitted subclass.
        
        ---
        
        ## 11. JVM / Compiler Behavior
        
        ### Compiler Behavior
        
        - Validates permitted subclasses at compile time
        - Injects metadata into class files
        - Enables exhaustive pattern analysis
        
        ### JVM Behavior
        
        - Loads sealed class metadata
        - Enforces access rules at runtime
        - Prevents illegal subclass loading
        
        ---
        
        ## 12. Sealed vs Final vs Non-Sealed
        
        | Aspect | sealed | final | non-sealed |
        | --- | --- | --- | --- |
        | Inheritance Control | Restricted | Not allowed | Open |
        | Subclass Limit | Explicit list | Zero | Unlimited |
        | Use Case | Domain modeling | Utility classes | Extension points |
        | Compiler Checks | Strong | Simple | None |
        
        ---
        
        ## 13. When to Use Sealed Classes
        
        ### Recommended Use Cases
        
        - Domain-driven design (states, roles, types)
        - Finite hierarchies
        - Pattern matching logic
        - API design with strict extension rules
        - Security-sensitive class hierarchies
        
        ### Avoid When
        
        - Open-ended inheritance is required
        - Framework extension points are needed
        - Dynamic plugin systems are involved
        
        ---
        
        ## 14. Real-world Analogy
        
        A **sealed class** is like a **company policy document**:
        
        - Only approved roles exist
        - No unauthorized roles allowed
        - Changes must be deliberate
        
        A **non-sealed class** is like an **open job market**:
        
        - Anyone can apply and extend
        
        ---
        
        ## 15. Important Points
        
        - Sealed classes improve **type safety**
        - They make inheritance **explicit and intentional**
        - They work best with **pattern matching**
        - They reduce runtime errors through compile-time checks
        - They complement records and pattern matching features
        
        ---
        
        ## 16. Summary
        
        Sealed classes introduce controlled inheritance in Java by explicitly defining which classes can extend or implement a type. With `sealed`, `permits`, and `non-sealed`, Java provides a powerful mechanism for designing safe, closed hierarchies. When combined with pattern matching, sealed classes enable exhaustive compile-time checking, leading to more robust and maintainable code.
        
- **Enhanced Interfaces** (**NEW**)
    - Overview
        
        # Enhanced Interfaces in Java (Java 9+ and Java 17+)
        
        ---
        
        ## 1. Definition and Explanation
        
        ### Definition
        
        **Enhanced Interfaces** refer to the improvements made to Java interfaces starting from **Java 8**, with significant additions in **Java 9** and **Java 17**.
        
        These enhancements allow interfaces to contain **default methods**, **static methods**, **private methods**, and **sealed interfaces**.
        
        ### Purpose
        
        The enhancements were introduced to:
        
        - Improve **code reuse** inside interfaces
        - Avoid code duplication in default methods
        - Support **controlled implementations**
        - Make interfaces suitable for **modern API and framework design**
        
        ### Simple Explanation
        
        Earlier, interfaces could only contain:
        
        - Abstract methods
        - Public static final constants
        
        Now, interfaces can:
        
        - Share method implementations
        - Hide helper logic
        - Restrict which classes can implement them
        
        ### Technical Explanation
        
        Modern interfaces act as:
        
        - **Contracts** (what must be implemented)
        - **Partial implementations** (how something is done)
        - **Hierarchy controllers** (who can implement)
        
        ---
        
        ## 2. Interface Private Methods (Java 9+)
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.1 Definition and Purpose
        
        ### Definition
        
        A **private method in an interface** is a method that:
        
        - Is accessible **only within the interface**
        - Cannot be overridden or accessed by implementing classes
        
        ### Purpose
        
        Private methods are used to:
        
        - Share common logic between default methods
        - Avoid code duplication
        - Improve maintainability of interfaces
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.2 Syntax
        
        ```java
        interface Calculator {
        
            default int add(int a, int b) {
                return process(a, b);
            }
        ```
