        ```java
            default int multiply(int a, int b) {
                return process(a * b, 1);
            }
        
            private int process(int x, int y) {
                return x + y;
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.3 Rules and Notes (Private Interface Methods)
        
        - Private methods:
            - Are implicitly `private`
            - Cannot be `abstract`
            - Cannot be overridden
        - Allowed modifiers:
            - `private`
            - `private static`
        - Not allowed:
            - `protected`
            - `public`
        - Accessible only inside the interface
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.4 Example: Private Method Usage
        
        ```java
        interface Logger {
        
            default void info(String msg) {
                log("INFO", msg);
            }
        
            default void error(String msg) {
                log("ERROR", msg);
            }
        
            private void log(String level, String message) {
                System.out.println(level + ": " + message);
            }
        }
        
        public class Test implements Logger {
            public static void main(String[] args) {
                Test t = new Test();
                t.info("Application started");
                t.error("Null pointer occurred");
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ### Output
        
        ```
        INFO: Application started
        ERROR: Null pointer occurred
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.5 JVM / Compiler Behavior (Private Interface Methods)
        
        ### Compiler Behavior
        
        - Compiles private methods as **non-inheritable helpers**
        - Ensures they are only callable inside the interface
        
        ### JVM Behavior
        
        - Loads private methods as part of interface metadata
        - No dynamic dispatch involved
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.6 Real-world Analogy
        
        Private interface methods are like **internal utility tools** inside a machine:
        
        - Used by components internally
        - Not exposed to users or external systems
        
        ---
        
        ## 3. Sealed Interfaces (Java 17+)
        
        ---
        
        ## 3.1 Definition and Explanation
        
        ### Definition
        
        A **sealed interface** is an interface that **restricts which classes or interfaces can implement it**.
        
        ### Purpose
        
        Sealed interfaces are used to:
        
        - Model **finite and well-defined hierarchies**
        - Enforce domain constraints
        - Enable **exhaustiveness checking** in pattern matching
        
        ### Technical Explanation
        
        A sealed interface explicitly lists permitted implementors using the `permits` clause.
        
        Each permitted implementor must declare itself as:
        
        - `final`
        - `sealed`
        - `non-sealed`
        
        ---
        
        ## 3.2 Syntax
        
        ### Sealed Interface Declaration
        
        ```java
        public sealed interface Payment
                permits CashPayment, CardPayment {
        }
        
        ```
        
        ### Implementations
        
        ```java
        public final class CashPayment implements Payment {
        }
        
        public final class CardPayment implements Payment {
        }
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ## 3.3 Rules and Notes (Sealed Interfaces)
        
        - A sealed interface:
            - Must declare `sealed`
            - Must specify permitted implementors
        - Permitted implementors:
            - Must directly implement the interface
            - Must be `final`, `sealed`, or `non-sealed`
        - Sealed interfaces:
            - Cannot be implemented by anonymous classes
            - Can extend other interfaces
        - `permits` clause can be omitted if all implementors are in the same source file
        
        ---
        
        ## 3.4 Example: Controlled Implementation
        
        ```java
        public sealed interface Notification
                permits EmailNotification, SmsNotification {
        }
        
        public final class EmailNotification implements Notification {
        }
        
        public non-sealed class SmsNotification implements Notification {
        }
        
        ```
        
        ### Explanation
        
        - Only two implementations are allowed
        - `SmsNotification` allows further extension
        - `EmailNotification` does not
        
        ---
        
        ## 4. Exhaustiveness Checking with Sealed Interfaces
        
        ---
        
        ## 4.1 Definition
        
        **Exhaustiveness checking** ensures that all permitted implementations of a sealed interface are handled in a `switch` expression.
        
        ---
        
        ## 4.2 Example
        
        ```java
        public sealed interface Shape
                permits Circle, Square {
        }
        
        public final class Circle implements Shape {
        }
        
        public final class Square implements Shape {
        }
        
        ```
        
        ```java
        public class Test {
            static String identify(Shape shape) {
                return switch (shape) {
                    case Circle c -> "Circle";
                    case Square s -> "Square";
                };
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ### Compiler Behavior
        
        - No `default` case required
        - Compiler guarantees all implementations are covered
        
        ---
        
        ## 5. Interface Private Methods vs Default Methods
        
        | Aspect | Private Method | Default Method |
        | --- | --- | --- |
        | Access Level | Private | Public |
        | Overridable | No | Yes |
        | Purpose | Helper logic | Provide behavior |
        | Accessible in Class | No | Yes |
        | Introduced In | Java 9 | Java 8 |
        
        ---
        
        ## 6. When to Use Enhanced Interfaces
        
        ### Use Private Interface Methods When:
        
        - Multiple default methods share logic
        - Logic must not be exposed
        - Code duplication must be avoided
        
        ### Use Sealed Interfaces When:
        
        - Implementations are finite
        - Domain rules must be enforced
        - Pattern matching is required
        - API extension must be controlled
        
        ---
        
        ## 7. Important Points
        
        - Interfaces are no longer purely abstract
        - Private methods improve interface maintainability
        - Sealed interfaces enforce strict design boundaries
        - Enhanced interfaces align Java with modern language design
        - Widely used in frameworks and APIs
        
        ---
        
        ## 8. Summary
        
        Enhanced interfaces significantly expand the role of interfaces in Java.
        
        Private methods enable internal code reuse without exposing implementation details, while sealed interfaces provide strict control over who can implement an interface. Together, these features allow developers to design safer, cleaner, and more maintainable APIs while supporting modern language features like pattern matching and exhaustive compile-time checks.
        

### 2.10 OOP Design Principles & Patterns (**NEW SECTION**)

- **Design Principles** (**NEW**)
    - Overview
        
        ## 1. Introduction to OOP Design Principles
        
        Object-Oriented Design Principles are **guidelines** used while designing software systems using Object-Oriented Programming concepts.
        
        They help developers create applications that are:
        
        - Easy to understand
        - Easy to maintain
        - Easy to extend
        - Less error-prone
        - Flexible to future changes
        
        These principles are **not rules enforced by the compiler**, but **best practices** followed by experienced developers and software architects.
        
        Design principles act as a **bridge between OOP concepts (class, object, inheritance, polymorphism, etc.) and real-world software architecture**.
        
        ---
        
        ## 2. Why OOP Design Principles Are Required
        
        Without proper design principles, software may suffer from:
        
        - Tight coupling between classes
        - Difficulty in modifying existing code
        - High risk of bugs during changes
        - Poor reusability
        - Code duplication
        
        By applying OOP design principles, we achieve:
        
        - Loose coupling
        - High cohesion
        - Better reusability
        - Maintainability
        - Testability
        
        ---
        
        ## 3. Core OOP Design Principles
        
        The most important and widely accepted OOP design principles are:
        
        1. **Single Responsibility Principle (SRP)**
        2. **Open–Closed Principle (OCP)**
        3. **Liskov Substitution Principle (LSP)**
        4. **Interface Segregation Principle (ISP)**
        5. **Dependency Inversion Principle (DIP)**
        
        These five principles together are called **SOLID Principles**.
        
        In addition to SOLID, commonly used supporting principles are:
        
        - DRY (Don’t Repeat Yourself)
        - KISS (Keep It Simple and Stupid)
        - YAGNI (You Aren’t Gonna Need It)
        - Favor Composition over Inheritance
    - **SOLID principles** overview
        - Single Responsibility Principle (SRP)
            
            # 4. SOLID Principles (Detailed Explanation)
            
            ---
            
            ## 4.1 Single Responsibility Principle (SRP)
            
            ### Definition and Explanation
            
            **A class should have only one reason to change.**
            
            This means:
            
            - A class should perform **only one well-defined responsibility**
            - It should not handle multiple unrelated functionalities
            
            SRP promotes **high cohesion**.
            
            ---
            
            ### Simple Understanding
            
            One class = One job.
            
            ---
            
            ### Technical Explanation
            
            If a class handles multiple responsibilities, any change in one responsibility may affect the others, increasing the risk of bugs.
            
            ---
            
            ### Bad Design Example (Violating SRP)
            
            ```java
            classEmployee {
            voidcalculateSalary() {
            // salary calculation logic
                }
            
            voidgenerateReport() {
            // report generation logic
                }
            
            voidsaveToDatabase() {
            // database logic
                }
            }
            
            ```
            
            Problems:
            
            - Salary logic change
            - Report format change
            - Database change
                
                All affect the same class.
                
            
            ---
            
            ### Good Design Example (Following SRP)
            
            ```java
            classSalaryCalculator {
            voidcalculateSalary() {
            // salary calculation logic
                }
            }
            
            classReportGenerator {
            voidgenerateReport() {
            // report generation logic
                }
            }
            
            classEmployeeRepository {
            voidsave() {
            // database logic
                }
            }
            
            ```
            
            ---
            
            ### JVM / Compiler Behavior
            
            - Compiler does not enforce SRP
            - JVM treats all classes normally
            - Responsibility separation is a **design-time decision**
            
            ---
            
            ### Real-world Analogy
            
            In an office:
            
            - Accountant handles salary
            - HR handles reports
            - Database admin handles storage
            
            One person doing all jobs leads to confusion.
            
            ---
            
            ### Important Points
            
            - Improves maintainability
            - Reduces impact of changes
            - Easier unit testing
        - Open/Closed Principle (OCP)
            
            ## 4.2 Open–Closed Principle (OCP)
            
            ### Definition and Explanation
            
            **Software entities should be open for extension but closed for modification.**
            
            This means:
            
            - Existing code should not be modified
            - New functionality should be added by extension
            
            ---
            
            ### Simple Understanding
            
            Do not change tested code; extend it.
            
            ---
            
            ### Bad Design Example (Violating OCP)
            
            ```java
            classDiscountCalculator {
            doublecalculate(String type) {
            if(type.equals("REGULAR")) {
            return10;
                    }elseif(type.equals("PREMIUM")) {
            return20;
                    }
            return0;
                }
            }
            
            ```
            
            Adding a new customer type requires modifying this class.
            
            ---
            
            ### Good Design Example (Following OCP)
            
            ```java
            interfaceDiscount {
            doublecalculate();
            }
            
            classRegularDiscountimplementsDiscount {
            publicdoublecalculate() {
            return10;
                }
            }
            
            classPremiumDiscountimplementsDiscount {
            publicdoublecalculate() {
            return20;
                }
            }
            
            ```
            
            New discount types can be added without changing existing code.
            
            ---
            
            ### JVM / Compiler Behavior
            
            - Polymorphism resolves method calls at runtime
            - JVM uses dynamic binding for overridden methods
            
            ---
            
            ### Real-world Analogy
            
            A mobile phone:
            
            - You install new apps (extension)
            - You don’t modify the operating system code
            
            ---
            
            ### Important Points
            
            - Uses abstraction and polymorphism
            - Reduces regression bugs
            - Supports scalable design
        - Liskov Substitution Principle (LSP)
            
            ## 4.3 Liskov Substitution Principle (LSP)
            
            ### Definition and Explanation
            
            **Subclasses must be substitutable for their base classes without affecting program correctness.**
            
            ---
            
            ### Simple Understanding
            
            Child class should behave like parent class.
            
            ---
            
            ### Rule Explanation
            
            If class `B` extends class `A`, then object of `B` should be usable wherever `A` is expected.
            
            ---
            
            ### Bad Design Example (Violating LSP)
            
            ```java
            classBird {
            voidfly() {
                    System.out.println("Bird is flying");
                }
            }
            
            classOstrichextendsBird {
            voidfly() {
            thrownewUnsupportedOperationException();
                }
            }
            
            ```
            
            ---
            
            ### Good Design Example
            
            ```java
            interfaceBird {
            }
            
            interfaceFlyingBirdextendsBird {
            voidfly();
            }
            
            classSparrowimplementsFlyingBird {
            publicvoidfly() {
                    System.out.println("Sparrow flying");
                }
            }
            
            classOstrichimplementsBird {
            }
            
            ```
            
            ---
            
            ### JVM / Compiler Behavior
            
            - JVM allows method overriding
            - Logical correctness must be ensured by developer
            
            ---
            
            ### Real-world Analogy
            
            A vehicle:
            
            - All cars can drive
            - Not all vehicles can fly
            
            ---
            
            ### Important Points
            
            - Prevents unexpected runtime behavior
            - Promotes correct inheritance hierarchy
        - Interface Segregation Principle (ISP)
            
            ## 4.4 Interface Segregation Principle (ISP)
            
            ### Definition and Explanation
            
            **Clients should not be forced to depend on interfaces they do not use.**
            
            ---
            
            ### Simple Understanding
            
            Create small, specific interfaces instead of large, general ones.
            
            ---
            
            ### Bad Design Example
            
            ```java
            interfaceWorker {
            voidwork();
            voideat();
            }
            
            ```
            
            Robots don’t eat.
            
            ---
            
            ### Good Design Example
            
            ```java
            interfaceWorkable {
            voidwork();
            }
            
            interfaceEatable {
            voideat();
            }
            
            classHumanimplementsWorkable, Eatable {
            publicvoidwork() {}
            publicvoideat() {}
            }
            
            classRobotimplementsWorkable {
            publicvoidwork() {}
            }
            
            ```
            
            ---
            
            ### JVM / Compiler Behavior
            
            - Compiler enforces method implementation
            - Design prevents unnecessary method implementations
            
            ---
            
            ### Real-world Analogy
            
            TV remote:
            
            - Only buttons relevant to your TV
            - Not buttons for AC, washing machine, etc.
            
            ---
            
            ### Important Points
            
            - Reduces side effects
            - Improves flexibility
            - Easier maintenance
        - Dependency Inversion Principle (DIP)
            
            ## 4.5 Dependency Inversion Principle (DIP)
            
            ### Definition and Explanation
            
            **High-level modules should not depend on low-level modules. Both should depend on abstractions.**
            
            ---
            
            ### Simple Understanding
            
            Depend on interfaces, not concrete classes.
            
            ---
            
            ### Bad Design Example
            
            ```java
            classKeyboard {
            }
            
            classComputer {
            Keyboardkeyboard=newKeyboard();
            }
            
            ```
            
            ---
            
            ### Good Design Example
            
            ```java
            interfaceInputDevice {
            }
            
            classKeyboardimplementsInputDevice {
            }
            
            classComputer {
                InputDevice device;
            
                Computer(InputDevice device) {
            this.device = device;
                }
            }
            
            ```
            
            ---
            
            ### JVM / Compiler Behavior
            
            - Interface references resolved at runtime
            - Enables dependency injection frameworks
            
            ---
            
            ### Real-world Analogy
            
            Switchboard:
            
            - Switch works with any appliance
            - Does not depend on specific device
            
            ---
            
            ### Important Points
            
            - Promotes loose coupling
            - Supports testability
            - Essential for frameworks like Spring
            
            ---
            
            # 5. Supporting Design Principles
            
            ---
            
            ## 5.1 DRY (Don’t Repeat Yourself)
            
            Avoid code duplication.
            
            Common logic should be centralized.
            
            ---
            
            ## 5.2 KISS (Keep It Simple and Stupid)
            
            Do not over-engineer solutions.
            
            Simple code is easier to maintain.
            
            ---
            
            ## 5.3 YAGNI (You Aren’t Gonna Need It)
            
            Do not add features until they are required.
            
            ---
            
            ## 5.4 Favor Composition over Inheritance
            
            Use **HAS-A** relationship instead of **IS-A** where possible.
            
            ---
            
            # 6. Important Points (Exam & Interview)
            
            - SOLID principles are design-time concepts
            - Not enforced by compiler or JVM
            - Improve maintainability and scalability
            - Widely used in enterprise applications
            - Essential for Spring, Hibernate, Microservices
            
            ---
            
            # 7. Summary
            
            Object-Oriented Design Principles provide **guidelines for writing clean, flexible, and maintainable Java applications**.
            
            By following SOLID and supporting principles, developers can design systems that are robust, extensible, and enterprise-ready.
            
    - **Law of Demeter** ("don't talk to strangers")
        
        # Law of Demeter (LoD) — “Don’t Talk to Strangers”
        
        ---
        
        ## 1. Definition and Explanation
        
        ### Definition
        
        **Law of Demeter (LoD)** is an Object-Oriented Design Principle that states:
        
        > A method should communicate only with its immediate collaborators and not with objects returned by other objects.
        > 
        
        This principle is also popularly known as:
        
        - **“Don’t Talk to Strangers”**
        - **Principle of Least Knowledge**
        
        ---
        
        ### Purpose
        
        The primary goal of the Law of Demeter is to:
        
        - Reduce **tight coupling**
        - Minimize **dependency chains**
        - Improve **maintainability**
        - Prevent fragile designs where one change breaks multiple classes
        
        ---
        
        ### Simple Understanding
        
        An object should:
        
        - Talk to itself
        - Talk to its own fields
        - Talk to objects passed as parameters
        - Talk to objects it creates
        
        It should **NOT** talk to:
        
        - Objects obtained from other objects
        
        ---
        
        ## 2. Formal Rule of Law of Demeter
        
        A method `M` of an object `O` is allowed to call methods of:
        
        1. `O` itself
        2. Objects passed as method parameters
        3. Objects created inside `M`
        4. Direct instance variables of `O`
        
        Anything beyond this violates LoD.
        
        ---
        
        ## 3. Syntax / Structural Pattern
        
        There is **no special Java syntax** for Law of Demeter.
        
        It is a **design-level constraint**, applied while writing code.
        
        Typical **LoD Violation Pattern**:
        
        ```java
        objectA.getB().getC().doSomething();
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ## 4. Rule Breakdown with Example (Violation)
        
        ### Example Scenario
        
        ```java
        class Engine {
            void start() {
                System.out.println("Engine started");
            }
        }
        
        class Car {
            Engine getEngine() {
                return new Engine();
            }
        }
        
        class Driver {
            void drive(Car car) {
                car.getEngine().start(); // ❌ LoD violation
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### Why This Is a Violation
        
        - `Driver` talks to `Car`
        - Then talks to `Engine` (a stranger)
        - `Driver` now depends on Car’s internal structure
        
        If `Car` changes how it manages `Engine`, `Driver` breaks.
        
        ---
        
        ## 5. Correct Design (Following Law of Demeter)
        
        ### Improved Example
        
        ```java
        class Engine {
            void start() {
                System.out.println("Engine started");
            }
        }
        
        class Car {
            private Engine engine = new Engine();
        
            void start() {
                engine.start();
            }
        }
        
        class Driver {
            void drive(Car car) {
                car.start(); // ✅ LoD followed
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### Output
        
        ```
        Engine started
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ## 6. JVM / Compiler Behavior
        
        - Java compiler **does not detect** Law of Demeter violations
        - JVM executes chained calls without any restriction
        - LoD is enforced **only by developer discipline and code reviews**
        
        ---
        
        ## 7. Technical Explanation (Why LoD Matters)
        
        Without LoD:
        
        - Changes propagate across multiple classes
        - Testing becomes difficult
        - Code becomes fragile and rigid
        
        With LoD:
        
        - Each class hides its internal structure
        - Dependencies are shallow
        - Changes remain localized
        
        ---
        
        ## 8. Real-world Analogy
        
        ### Office Analogy
        
        You want to apply for leave.
        
        ❌ Bad approach:
        
        - You ask your manager’s secretary’s assistant’s supervisor
        
        ✅ Good approach:
        
        - You ask your manager directly
        
        You should talk only to **people you directly know**.
        
        ---
        
        ## 9. Relationship with Other Design Principles
        
        | Principle | Relation with Law of Demeter |
        | --- | --- |
        | SRP | LoD supports focused responsibilities |
        | OCP | LoD reduces change impact |
        | DIP | LoD encourages abstraction |
        | Encapsulation | LoD strengthens encapsulation |
        
        ---
        
        ## 10. Common Violations in Real Projects
        
        - Excessive method chaining
        - DTO → Entity → Nested Object navigation
        - Overuse of getters exposing internals
        - Framework misuse without service boundaries
        
        ---
        
        ## 11. Allowed vs Not Allowed (Quick Reference)
        
        ### Allowed
        
        ```java
        this.doSomething();
        parameter.doSomething();
        field.doSomething();
        new Object().doSomething();
        
        ```
        
        ### Not Allowed
        
        ```java
        this.getA().getB().getC().doSomething();
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ## 12. Important Points
        
        - Law of Demeter is a **guideline**, not a rule
        - Reduces coupling but may increase delegation methods
        - Must be applied **practically**, not blindly
        - Over-application can lead to excessive wrapper methods
        
        ---
        
        ## 13. Advantages and Disadvantages
        
        ### Advantages
        
        - Loose coupling
        - Better maintainability
        - Improved readability
        - Safer refactoring
        
        ### Disadvantages
        
        - More wrapper methods
        - Slight increase in code size
        
        ---
        
        ## 14. Summary
        
        The **Law of Demeter** enforces minimal knowledge between objects by restricting communication to **immediate collaborators only**.
        
        By following “Don’t Talk to Strangers”, Java applications become **more robust, maintainable, and less fragile**, especially in large enterprise systems.
        
- **Object Creation Patterns** (**NEW**)
    - Overview
        
        # Object Creation Patterns (Creational Design Patterns)
        
        Object Creation Patterns focus on **how objects are created**, **how creation logic is encapsulated**, and **how dependencies are managed**.
        
        Instead of creating objects directly using `new`, these patterns provide **controlled, flexible, and maintainable object creation mechanisms**.
        
        They are heavily used in **enterprise Java**, **Spring Framework**, **API design**, and **large-scale systems**.
        
        ---
        
        ## 1. Factory Method & Static Factory Patterns
        
        ---
        
        ## 1.1 Factory Method Pattern
        
        ### Definition and Explanation
        
        The **Factory Method Pattern** defines an **interface or abstract class** for creating objects, but lets **subclasses decide which class to instantiate**.
        
        Object creation is **delegated**, not hardcoded.
        
        ---
        
        ### Purpose
        
        - Hide object creation logic
        - Reduce tight coupling
        - Support polymorphism
        - Follow **Open–Closed Principle**
        
        ---
        
        ### Simple Understanding
        
        Instead of creating objects directly, **ask a factory to create them**.
        
        ---
        
        ### Technical Explanation
        
        The client depends on an **abstraction**, not a concrete class.
        
        The factory method returns objects of subclasses at runtime.
        
        ---
        
        ### Syntax Structure
        
        ```java
        interface Product {
            void use();
        }
        
        class ConcreteProductA implements Product {
            public void use() {
                System.out.println("Using Product A");
            }
        }
        
        class ConcreteProductB implements Product {
            public void use() {
                System.out.println("Using Product B");
            }
        }
        
        abstract class ProductFactory {
            abstract Product createProduct();
        }
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### Example Implementation
        
        ```java
        class ProductAFactory extends ProductFactory {
            Product createProduct() {
                return new ConcreteProductA();
            }
        }
        
        class ProductBFactory extends ProductFactory {
            Product createProduct() {
                return new ConcreteProductB();
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### Client Code
        
        ```java
        ProductFactory factory = new ProductAFactory();
        Product product = factory.createProduct();
        product.use();
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### Output
        
        ```
        Using Product A
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### JVM / Compiler Behavior
        
        - Compiler enforces method overriding
        - JVM resolves factory method calls dynamically
        - Object type decided at runtime
        
        ---
        
        ### Real-world Analogy
        
        A vehicle factory:
        
        - You ask for a vehicle
        - Factory decides whether to give car or bike
        
        ---
        
        ### Important Points
        
        - Uses inheritance
        - Adds flexibility
        - Slightly increases number of classes
        
        ---
        
        ## 1.2 Static Factory Method Pattern
        
        ---
        
        ### Definition and Explanation
        
        A **static factory method** is a **static method that returns an object**, instead of using constructors.
        
        ---
        
        ### Purpose
        
        - Meaningful method names
        - Control instance creation
        - Ability to return cached objects
        - Ability to return subclasses
        
        ---
        
        ### Syntax
        
        ```java
        class User {
            private String name;
        
            private User(String name) {
                this.name = name;
            }
        
            public static User createUser(String name) {
                return new User(name);
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### Example Usage
        
        ```java
        User user = User.createUser("Ayush");
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### JVM / Compiler Behavior
        
        - Static method call resolved at compile time
        - Object creation controlled internally
        
        ---
        
        ### Advantages over Constructors
        
        | Aspect | Constructor | Static Factory |
        | --- | --- | --- |
        | Naming | Fixed | Meaningful |
        | Caching | Not possible | Possible |
        | Subclass return | No | Yes |
        | Control | Limited | High |
        
        ---
        
        ### Real-world Example
        
        `Integer.valueOf()` instead of `new Integer()`
        
        ---
        
        ### Important Points
        
        - Widely used in Java API
        - Preferred over constructors in many cases
        
        ---
        
        # 2. Singleton Pattern
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.1 Definition and Explanation
        
        The **Singleton Pattern** ensures that **only one instance of a class exists** and provides a **global access point** to it.
        
        ---
        
        ### Purpose
        
        - Control shared resources
        - Ensure consistency
        - Reduce memory usage
        
        ---
        
        ### Simple Understanding
        
        Only one object for the entire application.
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.2 Eager Initialization Singleton
        
        ### Implementation
        
        ```java
        class Singleton {
            private static final Singleton INSTANCE = new Singleton();
        
            private Singleton() {
            }
        
            public static Singleton getInstance() {
                return INSTANCE;
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### JVM Behavior
        
        - Instance created at class loading time
        - Class loading is thread-safe
        
        ---
        
        ### Advantages
        
        - Simple
        - Thread-safe
        
        ---
        
        ### Disadvantages
        
        - Instance created even if not used
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.3 Lazy Initialization Singleton (Thread Safe)
        
        ### Implementation
        
        ```java
        class Singleton {
            private static Singleton instance;
        
            private Singleton() {
            }
        
            public static synchronized Singleton getInstance() {
                if (instance == null) {
                    instance = new Singleton();
                }
                return instance;
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### JVM / Compiler Behavior
        
        - Synchronization ensures thread safety
        - Slight performance overhead
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.4 Double-Checked Locking (Best Practice)
        
        ```java
        class Singleton {
            private static volatile Singleton instance;
        
            private Singleton() {
            }
        
            public static Singleton getInstance() {
                if (instance == null) {
                    synchronized (Singleton.class) {
                        if (instance == null) {
                            instance = new Singleton();
                        }
                    }
                }
                return instance;
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### Important Points
        
        - `volatile` prevents instruction reordering
        - Efficient and thread-safe
        - Used in enterprise systems
        
        ---
        
        ### Real-world Analogy
        
        Government:
        
        - Only one Prime Minister at a time
        
        ---
        
        ### Key Notes
        
        - Avoid excessive use
        - Acts like global variable if misused
        - Used for logging, configuration, cache
        
        ---
        
        # 3. Builder Pattern (For Complex Objects)
        
        ---
        
        ## 3.1 Definition and Explanation
        
        The **Builder Pattern** separates the **construction of a complex object** from its representation.
        
        It is used when an object:
        
        - Has many optional fields
        - Requires readable creation code
        - Needs immutability
        
        ---
        
        ### Problem Without Builder
        
        ```java
        User user = new User("A", 25, "India", "IT", true, false, true);
        
        ```
        
        Unreadable and error-prone.
        
        ---
        
        ## 3.2 Builder Pattern Structure
        
        ---
        
        ### Example Implementation
        
        ```java
        class User {
            private final String name;
            private final int age;
            private final String country;
            private final boolean active;
        
            private User(UserBuilder builder) {
                this.name = builder.name;
                this.age = builder.age;
                this.country = builder.country;
                this.active = builder.active;
            }
        
            static class UserBuilder {
                private String name;
                private int age;
                private String country;
                private boolean active;
        
                UserBuilder setName(String name) {
                    this.name = name;
                    return this;
                }
        
                UserBuilder setAge(int age) {
                    this.age = age;
                    return this;
                }
        
                UserBuilder setCountry(String country) {
                    this.country = country;
                    return this;
                }
        
                UserBuilder setActive(boolean active) {
                    this.active = active;
                    return this;
                }
        
                User build() {
                    return new User(this);
                }
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### Client Code
        
        ```java
        User user = new User.UserBuilder()
                .setName("Ayush")
                .setAge(25)
                .setCountry("India")
                .setActive(true)
                .build();
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### JVM / Compiler Behavior
        
        - Method chaining via returning `this`
        - Object created only once at `build()`
        
        ---
        
        ### Advantages
        
        - Readable code
        - Immutable objects
        - Flexible object creation
        
        ---
        
        ### Disadvantages
        
        - More classes
        - Slightly more code
        
        ---
        
        ### Real-world Analogy
        
        Ordering a custom burger:
        
        - Choose ingredients step by step
        - Final product built at the end
        
        ---
        
        # 4. Comparison Summary
        
        | Pattern | Use Case | Key Benefit |
        | --- | --- | --- |
        | Factory Method | Object creation delegation | Loose coupling |
        | Static Factory | Controlled creation | Readability & caching |
        | Singleton | Single shared instance | Resource control |
        | Builder | Complex object creation | Readability & immutability |
        
        ---
        
        # 5. Important Points (Interview Focus)
        
        - Prefer **static factory methods** over constructors
        - Singleton must be thread-safe
        - Builder is best for objects with many optional parameters
        - Creation patterns reduce coupling
        - Widely used in Spring and Java APIs
        
        ---
        
        # 6. Summary
        
        Object Creation Patterns provide **clean, controlled, and scalable ways to create objects in Java**.
        
        By using **Factory**, **Singleton**, and **Builder** patterns appropriately, developers can design **robust, maintainable, and enterprise-grade applications**.
        
- **Common OOP Anti-patterns** (**NEW**)
    - Overview
        
        # Common OOP Anti-patterns
        
        Object-Oriented **anti-patterns** are recurring design mistakes that **appear useful initially** but **cause serious problems** in maintainability, scalability, and correctness over time.
        
        Understanding anti-patterns is as important as learning design principles, because **most real-world bugs originate from bad design, not syntax errors**.
        
        This section covers **three critical OOP anti-patterns** frequently seen in Java enterprise applications.
        
        ---
        
        ## 1. Circular Dependencies
        
        ---
        
        ## 1.1 Definition and Explanation
        
        A **circular dependency** occurs when **two or more classes depend on each other directly or indirectly**, forming a cycle.
        
        Example:
        
        - Class `A` depends on class `B`
        - Class `B` depends on class `A`
        
        This violates **loose coupling** and leads to fragile systems.
        
        ---
        
        ## 1.2 Simple Understanding
        
        Two classes are **stuck together**, and neither can function independently.
        
        ---
        
        ## 1.3 Technical Explanation
        
        Circular dependencies tightly bind components, causing:
        
        - Difficult object creation
        - Initialization failures
        - Stack overflow risks
        - Testing complications
        - Dependency injection failures
        
        In layered architectures, circular dependencies are **architectural violations**.
        
        ---
        
        ## 1.4 Example (Circular Dependency)
        
        ```java
        class OrderService {
            PaymentService paymentService;
        
            void placeOrder() {
                paymentService.processPayment();
            }
        }
        
        class PaymentService {
            OrderService orderService;
        
            void processPayment() {
                orderService.placeOrder();
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ## 1.5 Problems in This Design
        
        - Infinite call loop risk
        - Impossible to instantiate cleanly
        - Tight coupling
        - Violates SRP and DIP
        
        ---
        
        ## 1.6 Resolution Strategies
        
        ### 1.6.1 Introduce an Abstraction
        
        ```java
        interface PaymentProcessor {
            void processPayment();
        }
        
        ```
        
        ```java
        class OrderService {
            PaymentProcessor processor;
        
            void placeOrder() {
                processor.processPayment();
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### 1.6.2 Invert the Dependency (DIP)
        
        - High-level modules depend on interfaces
        - Low-level modules implement interfaces
        
        ---
        
        ### 1.6.3 Event-Based Communication
        
        - Use callbacks or events instead of direct calls
        - Break direct dependency chains
        
        ---
        
        ## 1.7 JVM / Compiler Behavior
        
        - Compiler allows circular references
        - JVM may fail during initialization
        - Dependency Injection containers often detect and reject cycles
        
        ---
        
        ## 1.8 Real-world Analogy
        
        Two managers waiting for approval from each other.
        
        No decision ever happens.
        
        ---
        
        ## 1.9 Important Points
        
        - Circular dependencies are **design-time errors**
        - Difficult to refactor later
        - Always indicate **poor responsibility separation**
        
        ---
        
        ## 2. God Object Anti-pattern
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.1 Definition and Explanation
        
        A **God Object** is a class that:
        
        - Knows too much
        - Does too much
        - Controls too many responsibilities
        
        Such a class violates **Single Responsibility Principle (SRP)** and becomes a maintenance nightmare.
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.2 Simple Understanding
        
        One class tries to run the entire application.
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.3 Technical Explanation
        
        God objects typically:
        
        - Contain hundreds of methods
        - Access multiple layers (UI, service, DAO)
        - Perform unrelated business logic
        - Have many instance variables
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.4 Example (God Object)
        
        ```java
        class ApplicationManager {
            void login() {}
            void calculateSalary() {}
            void generateReport() {}
            void saveToDatabase() {}
            void sendEmail() {}
            void validateUser() {}
        }
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.5 Problems in This Design
        
        - Extremely high coupling
        - Low cohesion
        - Hard to test
        - Any change risks breaking unrelated features
        - Impossible to reuse
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.6 Correct Design (Refactoring)
        
        ```java
        class AuthService {
            void login() {}
            void validateUser() {}
        }
        
        class SalaryService {
            void calculateSalary() {}
        }
        
        class ReportService {
            void generateReport() {}
        }
        
        class NotificationService {
            void sendEmail() {}
        }
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.7 JVM / Compiler Behavior
        
        - Compiler sees no error
        - JVM executes normally
        - Problems appear only during maintenance and evolution
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.8 Real-world Analogy
        
        One employee acting as:
        
        - Accountant
        - HR
        - Manager
        - Security
        - IT Support
        
        Eventually everything fails.
        
        ---
        
        ## 2.9 Important Points
        
        - God objects emerge gradually
        - Often created due to deadline pressure
        - Refactoring early is critical
        
        ---
        
        ## 3. Anemic Domain Model vs Rich Domain Model
        
        ---
        
        ## 3.1 Anemic Domain Model
        
        ---
        
        ### Definition
        
        An **Anemic Domain Model** consists of:
        
        - Classes with only fields
        - Getters and setters only
        - No business logic
        
        All logic resides in service classes.
        
        ---
        
        ### Example (Anemic Model)
        
        ```java
        class Account {
            private double balance;
        
            public double getBalance() {
                return balance;
            }
        
            public void setBalance(double balance) {
                this.balance = balance;
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ```java
        class AccountService {
            void withdraw(Account account, double amount) {
                if (account.getBalance() >= amount) {
                    account.setBalance(account.getBalance() - amount);
                }
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ### Problems
        
        - Violates encapsulation
        - Business rules scattered
        - Domain objects become data containers
        - Harder to maintain consistency
        
        ---
        
        ## 3.2 Rich Domain Model
        
        ---
        
        ### Definition
        
        A **Rich Domain Model** places:
        
        - Data
        - Behavior
        - Business rules
        
        inside the **same domain object**.
        
        ---
        
        ### Example (Rich Model)
        
        ```java
        class Account {
            private double balance;
        
            void withdraw(double amount) {
                if (amount <= 0) {
                    throw new IllegalArgumentException();
                }
                if (balance < amount) {
                    throw new IllegalStateException();
                }
                balance -= amount;
            }
        
            double getBalance() {
                return balance;
            }
        }
        
        ```
        
        ---
        
        ## 3.3 Comparison Table
        
        | Aspect | Anemic Domain Model | Rich Domain Model |
        | --- | --- | --- |
        | Business logic | Service layer | Domain object |
        | Encapsulation | Weak | Strong |
        | Maintainability | Low | High |
        | Object behavior | Passive | Active |
        | OOP purity | Poor | Strong |
        
        ---
        
        ## 3.4 JVM / Compiler Perspective
        
        - Compiler treats both models the same
        - Design quality impacts runtime correctness
        - Rich models reduce invalid state creation
        
        ---
        
        ## 3.5 Real-world Analogy
        
        Anemic:
        
        - ATM card knows nothing
        - Bank clerk does everything
        
        Rich:
        
        - ATM card enforces rules
        - Bank clerk just initiates actions
        
        ---
        
        ## 3.6 Important Points
        
        - Rich domain models align with OOP philosophy
        - Anemic models resemble procedural programming
        - Enterprise applications strongly prefer rich models
        
        ---
        
        ## 4. Key Design Takeaways
        
        - Circular dependencies destroy modularity
        - God objects indicate SRP violations
        - Anemic models weaken encapsulation
        - Anti-patterns compile successfully but fail architecturally
        - Early detection saves massive refactoring effort
        
        ---
        
        ## 5. Summary
        
        Common OOP anti-patterns are **silent design killers**.
        
        They do not break compilation, but they **break scalability, flexibility, and correctness** over time.
        
        Avoiding circular dependencies, eliminating god objects, and preferring rich domain models are **mandatory practices** for building **clean, maintainable, and enterprise-grade Java applications**.
